## S. 7

Japanese architecture boasts some of the finest
traditional timber-frame buildings in the world:
the pristine Grand Shrine at Ise, ritually rebuilt at
twenty-year intervals for over a millennium; the
pagoda and the Golden Hall at Höryü-ji in Nara,
two of the oldest, wooden buildings in the world;
Katsura Imperial Villa in Kyoto, hailed by Modernist architects in Europe and America for its
sophisticated design; and many serenely beautiful
monasteries perched on mountaintops or tucked in
valleys across the land. These masterpieces are the
creations of the master carpenter: his traditions, his
materials, his methods, and his tools. The Way of the
Carpenter is a fascinating exploration of the role
played over long centuries by the master carpenters of Japan and their tools in the creation of
temple, shrine, castle, residential, and tea house
architecture, richly illustrated with over 160 color
and monochrome photographs and drawings.

The author, the first Westerner to be initiated
into a traditional carpentry guild, begins by illuminating the significance of the “Way” of the art and
craft of carpentry in Japan, revealing the religious
rituals that surround the carpenter’s tools and the
building process as well as the secret traditions
and the trade’s transmission from master to disciple over the centuries. He discusses the organization of the carpenters’ guilds, the apprenticeship
system, and the divisions of labor and specialization within the profession. He reviews the materials of Japanese carpentry, the woods the masters
worked with, and outlines the primary methods of
design and construction—for the traditional master
carpenter did both.

An exhaustive, illustrated catalogue of the hundreds of carpenter’s tools follows. Dividing them
into tools of the mind, for design, and tools of the
hand, for construction, the author documents the
details of their form, use, and relation to other
tools, past and present. Several extinct and nearly
extinct tools are described as well.

Unique to this work is the retracing of the evolution of Japanese tools over the centuries, which
can be as compelling as a detective story. Sifting
through archaeological finds, graffiti written on
the back of roof timbers, painted scrolls, and liter-

## S. 14

Preface

As a child of missionary parents living in Izu, Japan, in the Occupation
years, I can scarcely claim to have been born with a silver spoon in my mouth.
However, I can claim to have had Japanese carpenters’ tools in my hands from
early childhood. In those days of desperate and rapid rebuilding of a whole
nation, camps, churches and houses were springing up throughout the Izu
Peninsula under the direction of my father, who was both carpenter and priest.
My earliest memories, therefore, are of building sites. I have a clear picture as a
three-year-old of clambering around the roof frame of the new rectory in Itö. The
fragrance of unseasoned timber was as familiar to me as the odor of the “honey
buckets” of the night-soil carriers in the neighboring fields.

It may have been this childhood experience which drew me inexorably over
the years of scholarly study and research in art and architecture to the building
sites of Japan and the patient but energetic world of the master builders and
restorationists. Their enterprises may be eclipsed by the onslaught of high
technology, “intelligent” buildings and manufactured housing in the vast urban
developments of Japan today, but their practice continues in the ancient
architectural centers of Kyoto and Nara.

In 1979, when still a graduate student in fine arts at Harvard University, I
became a part of this special world by working with traditional Japanese
carpenters in the reconstruction of an eighteenth-century Kyoto merchant's shop
and residence at the Boston Children's Museum. The same master carpenters
took me under their tutelage when I returned to Japan in 1980-81 to work at
Enryaku-ji, the great Buddhist temple complex among the cedars of Mount Hiei,
overlooking Kyoto. Much of the Kakushö-an, a subprecinct, was being rebuilt by
carefully coordinated teams of master carpenters (daiku), roof tilers, wall
plasterers and stone masons. Under their guidance, I became aware of the
extraordinary complexity of construction processes and the infinitely varied
techniques for using traditional tools. I was the first non-Japanese admitted into
the membership of the Kyöto Dentö Kenchiku Gijutsu Kyökai (Kyoto Guild of
Traditional Master Builders). Subsequently, I continued my involvement with

PREFACE + VI

## S. 15

the hereditary master builders, and the study of their tools and techniques,
which has made this book possible.

In this book I have combined a discussion of tool evolution with a technical
analysis of tool types and techniques in the hope of narrowing the gap between
these two fields which may sit awkwardly in each other's company. I have tried
to be both artisan and academic. A technical study gives little sense of why tools
were invented and how they affected building form. A purely academic
approach overlooks the logic of practical problems and their resolution. Only a
dual approach meets bofh challenges because the tools themselves are the link
between practice and principle in the Way of the Carpenter.

I am deeply indebted to Yasui Kiyoshi, master carpenter and restorer of
Katsura Imperial Villa, for his assistance; to Inagaki Eizö, professor emeritus of
the Department of Architecture of the University of Tokyo; Karai Kunio,
formerly of the Takenaka Carpentry Tools Museum in Kobe; and to John M.
Rosenfield, Abby Aldrich Rockefeller Professor of Oriental Art at Harvard
University. Ihave learned much from the many artisans and craftsmen alongside
whom I have worked, as well as from the tool collection and curators of the
Takenaka Carpentry Tools Museum, the major repository of carpenters’ tools in
Japan. I am also grateful to The Japan Foundation for a Professional Fellowship
in 1986-87.

Conventional Japanese proper name order, family name first, is followed.
Macrons have been omitted from common nouns like Shinto and daimyo which
appear in modern English-language dictionaries and which are not italicized.
Special terms are italicized on first appearance; thereafter they are not.
Traditional measurements retain italics for clarity. All translations from Japanese
as well as the line drawings and photographs are my own unless otherwise
acknowledged. A glossary giving the full name and Japanese characters for

technical terms used in this study is also appended.

## S. 26

CHAPTER ONE

The Way of the Carpenter

Three small, unassuming Japanese planes rest on my desk. The gift of a retired
master carpenter, none is larger than the span of my outstretched fingers nor
greater in width than the base of my thumb. Two are straight, one sweetly
curved. The oak block of each plane is scarred with use, the cutting blade
pockmarked with rust. In such objects John Ruskin may have glimpsed the
romance of ruin but these tools evoke more than nostalgia for a disappearing
craft. They form tangible links with the people and processes of building in the
past. They are not passive objects meant for casual perusal in a museum. They
were the instruments of energetic mediation between man and materials in a
world which has now all but disappeared. Pulled across a piece of wood, each of
my planes still carves its distinct signature into the grain—a neat groove, a
serrated path or a graceful arc. The true home of such tools is not a desk top nor
a display case but in the hands of a carpenter standing among the fresh shavings
of a building site. They belong to the dynamic world of traditional Japanese
building practice as an inseparable part of a way of work which is also a way of
life. This is the Way of the Carpenter.

The simple word “way” has a wide range of meanings. A way is the method
or manner of doing something. On a grander scale it may be a custom or
convention upon which a society operates. A way serves as a direction, route,
course or path. It forms patterns of thinking and action. Jesus of Nazareth
pronounced himself “the Way, the truth and the life” while Lao-tse made the
Tao, or the Way, the basis of social and governmental ethics. In Japan, the
concept of way (michi or dö) is manifest in many aspects of culture and belief.
Shinto is the Way of the Gods and Butsudö the Way of the Buddha. Bushido, the
Way of the Warrior, has passed into the universal vocabulary of the martial arts;
these include aikido, judo and kendo. Tea ceremony is known as chadö, the Way
of Tea, calligraphy as shodö, the Way of Writing, and flower arrangement is kadö,
the Way of the Flower.

The concept of Way is crucial for understanding carpenters and their tools in
Japan. The term dögu, which is most commonly used for tools in Japan, means

THE WAY OF THE CARPENTER + 3

## S. 27

literally “the way of the tool.” It reflects the association of the process of building
with a broader pattern of meaning best defined by the concept of Way. Dögu
consists of two characters A. Dö means “way,” and gu is best translated as
“implement” or “means.” When a carpenter’s chisel or plane is called a dögu,
therefore, it is being recognized as an implement or a means of a special Way.
When the Chinese characters for dögu were adopted into the Japanese language
many centuries ago, they did not refer to carpenters’ tools at all. They
designated the important implements used in Buddhist ritual as aids to
enlightenment. Moreover, they referred to the clothing and other equipment
essential to the priestiy way of life, such as the prayer mat used for meditation
and the bowl used for begging. When the samurai emerged as a ruling class in
the twelfth and thirteenth centuries, the meaning of the term broadened to
include many of the special items that were part of their way of life and warrior
creed including armor, spears and sword sheaths. By the fourteenth and
fifteenth centuries, the meaning of dögu had widened to include the tools of the
carpenter.

The adoption of a word with deep religious associations like dögu to
designate carpenters’ tools is neither sacrilege nor a violation of sacred tenets.
Historically, Japanese carpenters were far more than manual workers because
the process of designing and erecting a building was more than a routine
construction procedure. There is a universal association between the process of
creating buildings and the spiritual dimension of the human psyche. In Ruskin’s
words, “great architecture makes us believe what we would not otherwise have
believed.”' The creativity necessary for sustaining the process of construction is
sparked by the same impulses of imagination and awe as fire spiritual beliefs,
and the process of building shares many of the same attributes as religion,
including sequence, order, orthodoxy, hierarchy, organization and awareness of
higher imperative. The construction of buildings dedicated to higher purposes
of religion on one level, and to the creation of metaphors of faith on another,
may guide the initiate along the path of inner development as well as sharpen
the definition of a faith. It is no accident that religion makes common use of
architectural imagery such as the House of God and the Gateway of Heaven,
and that the symbols of Freemasonry are tools of the medieval builder.

The process of building in Japan thus entailed far more than the procedures of
erecting and decorating structures. It became a special way of life, a pattern of
existential belief which, over the course of many lifetimes, took on the richness
of the term Way. This is seen in the observance of complex rituals based on
Shinto rites throughout the process of construction, in the esoteric nature of the
organization and training of the profession of the carpenter, and in his rituals

and reverance for tools. Let us see how each of these aspects contributes to the
Way of the Carpenter.

SHINTO RITES AND
THE WAY OF THE CARPENTER

The deep religious meaning of the Way of the Carpenter is reflected in the many
4 » THE WAY OF THE CARPENTER

## S. 28

solemn ceremonies and rituals that have been performed since ancient times as
an integral part of the building process. Most of these ceremonies follow the rites
of the native Shinto religion, but there are wide variations in observances
depending on local or family customs. Examples of Shinto rites to be observed
during the construction of the Grand Shrines of Ise are contained in the Engishiki, one of the earliest written records of imperial court etiquette, compiled in
the early tenth century. The Engi-shiki includes detailed lists of the offerings to be
made to appease the gods or kami on such important occasions as the Pillar-
Setting Ceremony and the Completion Ceremonies. The cyclic rebuilding of the
Grand Shrines of Ise every twenty years is the supreme example of architecture
transformed into ritual. Thirty-two major ceremonies and festivals are spread
over the eight years of the actual building operation, ranging from the
Yamaguchisai, expiatory prayers offered to the kami of the mountain where the
sacred trees to be used in the construction will be felled, to the solemn
ceremonies for transferring the sacred objects from the old shrine buildings to
the newly consecrated sanctuaries. Every stroke of an adze and every cut of a
saw is presided over or executed by carpenters who have been specially purified
and who wear white clothing to avoid defilement of the sacred site.

Even in contemporary Japan, ground-breaking, ridge-raising and completion
ceremonies are still observed in many building projects. The ground-breaking
ceremony (jichinsai) begins the on-site construction process with rituals designed
to appease the tutelary gods of the building site and to pray for the safety of all
concerned in the building operation. Young bamboo stalks are used to mark the
four corners of a sacred enclosure on the building site. These are joöined by a
sacred rope of new straw (shimenawa) bearing sacred, folded white invocatory
papers. An altar is erected with a separate table for offerings of rice, salt, fruit
and sak&. Blessings and purification rites are performed with a switch of leaves
of sakaki (Cleyera japonica), the sacred Shinto tree.

The most important ceremony, celebrating completion of the assembly of the
timber-frame, is the ridge-raising ceremony (jötöshiki or muneageshiki). It includes
the ceremonial raising and positioning of the ridgepole, thanksgiving for the
safe erection of the frame, and prayers for the long life and well-being of the
building and its inhabitants. The official ceremonies are regularly followed by
feasting and celebration. The completion ceremonies (rakuseishiki), which
involve all participants as well as neighbors and friends in prayer, feasting and
toasting, are less rigid in form.

In traditional practice, the chief master carpenter himself frequently officiated
at the ceremonies, donning the robes of a Shinto priest and intoning invocations
and blessings ancient in origin (figs. 1 and 2). The chief master carpenter was
formerly responsible for making the ritual implements and decorations for these
ceremonies, such as the ridge plaque and sacred bow and arrow that were
attached to the ridgepole during the ridge-raising ceremony, but this practice
has largely lapsed (fig. 3).

THE MAN AND THE WAY—
THE MAKING OF THE CARPENTER

The daily life and activities of the carpenter, and the organization of the building
profession generally, had much in common with that of the craft guilds of
medieval Europe. They entailed careful management of relationships with
patrons and clients, healthy competition with other workshops and intensive
training of the next generation of carpenters. The Japanese carpentry profession,
like the guilds of Europe, reflected religious piety and ritual in its professional
activities, having much of the character of an enclosed religious order. The kami
who watched over the carpenters were ”kindred spirits” to the patron saints of
the European building guilds. A meticulous process of selection and initiation
into its various secret practices lay at the basis of the life and strength of the
carpentry profession. The accumulated wisdom of generations of experience in
architectural style and construction procedures was enshrined in secret
hereditary writings. These ensured conformity to the articles of faith in their
own design tradition from one generation to the next within each workshop.

Carpenters followed the customary pattern of training in which manual skills
were mastered within a disciplined way of life governed by a strict sense of
ritual and hierarchy. Reverence for tradition was paramount. The body of
precepts and practices of one’s predecessors in the tradition were held in sacred
trust to be mastered, followed and passed on intact to the next generation.
Carpentry was as worthy of devotion as any religious faith and never lacked
suitable aspirants to its life of discipline and devotion.

Boys in their thirteenth year were considered to be sufficiently endowed both
physically and spiritually to undertake the contractual terms of service of
apprentices, furnish a guarantor and abide by the required solemn vows to
master the techniques and maintain the secret skills of the particular workshop
or “house” to which they were admitted. The master, for his part, guaranteed
the provision of proper food and clothing and certain other small bounties such
as New Year gifts. However, his side of the agreement was less strictly regulated

THE MAN AND THE WAY « 7

## S. 31

than that of the apprentice, and though the guilds officially adopted a stance of
tacit oversight of the appointment and training of apprentices, it was left largely
to the good sense and good will of the individual guild member to care for his
apprentices in an appropriate manner. In the regulations of one guild of
carpenters in the city of Edo (Tokyo) for 1793 there is the stipulation concerning
apprentices that “even in cases of minor misdemeanors they are not to be treated
unjustly and they are to be given a sound training,” which is more a general
exhortation of principle than detailed regulation.?

In view of the totality of their commitment, it is surprising to find that there
are no lists of duties, gradations of study and learning, or regulations in manual
instructions as might be found in any school curriculum, especially as the
shogunate had embarked on systematic propagation of Neo-Confucian tenets of
education in the late seventeenth century. It is important, however, to bear in
mind that a high level of literacy was assumed in the case of the carpenters who
rose to the top of their profession, and this was obtained within the
apprenticeship system. In some workshops, as in the commercial houses in the
great cities of Edo and Osaka in the seventeenth century, reading and
calligraphy classes were conducted for apprentices by masters after the day’s
duties were finished. Toward the end of Tokugawa rule, the widespread
accessibility of terakoya, the area or village Buddhist temple school, to all
children desiring instruction in ethics, calligraphy and abacus meant that a lad
of twelve or thirteen years often had some such skills at the time of his
acceptance as an apprentice.

In a typical ten-year apprenticeship, the first few years would be fully
occupied with miscellaneous chores in the house workshop. A young apprentice
was forbidden to touch any tools, let alone receive instruction in their use during
that time. This applied equally to the older and often more educated apprentices
who had started their training late. The apprentice was expected to watch the
master and his assistants at work until such time as he was overcome with an
overwhelming desire to use the tools himself. Even then, only the most basic
techniques were taught directly. Skills of a more advanced nature were absorbed
by imitation of the master by the apprentice, a process known universally
throughout the arts and crafts of Japan as nusumi-geiko, “stolen lessons.” In
traditional practice, therefore, the bases of learning were total immersion in a
workshop environment and inner motivation, both occurring at an age of
extreme sensitivity and adaptability. The manual skills and creative outlook of
the apprentice were formed even as the very person was maturing. The essence
of carpenter education was to mold the person and the professional at the same
time, before “the bones hardened.” Having been exposed to the subtle
psychology of this system, I know that its techniques establish a progression
defined not by formal curriculum but by behavioral initiation, and that the
whole being is permeated with special attitudes toward collaborative process
while the techniques of tool use and building construction are being mastered.

After completing ten years as an apprentice, the aspiring carpenter could be
presented by his master to other craftsmen in a formal ceremony in which he
received a set of clothes and a set of tools. In theory he was now ready to receive
full wages as a craftsman or to set up in the profession for himself, but in

8 . THE WAY OF THE CARPENTER

## S. 32

practice it was customary to serve a further period with his master as a thanks
offering. Even after independence, the familial nature of the masterapprenticeship relationship continued for a lifetime with annual exchange of
visits and gifts.

THE RITUALS OF TOOLS

In the most immediate and practical sense the Way of the Carpenter was also the
Way of the Tool. It was the method and manner of selecting timbers,
determining their dimensions and fashioning and assembling them. The
carpenter created and used a variety of tools in the accomplishment of these
tasks. There were tools for designing the building, which may be broadly
categorized as “instruments of the mind,” and there were tools for cutting and
finishing the timbers, or the “tools of the hand.” This second category also
included special tools used for maintaining the tools themselves.

The procedures of tool use were the fundamental means for realizing
architectural form. The interaction between the carpenter and his tools and, in
turn, between his tools and the materials, formed the basis for the customs and
conventions evolved in countless family workshops. Many of the ceremonies
and rituals observed as part of the building process were specifically devoted to
tools. Although rarely seen today, special rites were performed to bless and
purify the inking of the first marking line, the first stroke of the adze and the
first stroke of the saw. According to the Engi-shiki, by the tenth century the
consecration offerings at the periodic rebuildings of the Ise Grand Shrine
included planing-knives, adzes, chisels and saws along with hen’s eggs,
chickens, sickles and mattocks. Simpler versions of these same rituals are still
observed in remoter rural areas of Japan today, where it is customary for farmers
to affix building tools to votive paintings at local shrines as an offering to the
gods, along with agricultural implements like sickles and other appropriate
rustic offerings. Some votive paintings even depict carpenters’ tools like the ink
pot and snap line (fig. 4), a logical extension of their adoration at local festivals
and religious rites. The statutory blessing of tools as part of religious rituals
performed over many generations at building sites, together with practices like
the offering of tools to local gods, bestowed on them a sacred meaning. It also
led to the creation of ceremonial tools reserved for ritual purposes. Each family
of carpenters had a special set of adzes, saws, ink pots and squares which were
reserved for use at the time of the ridge-raising ceremony. These tools, with their
cutting edges dull but their handles bright with gold and lacquer, were purified
and blessed as part of the rites dedicating the new building (fig. 5).

Tools with special religious and symbolic associations have thus been central
to the profession of the building carpenter. However, the tools of daily use were
also imbued with special significance, as we have seen, and all the
organizational, vocational and religious dimensions to the Way of the Carpenter
were focused on the actual process of making and using these tools. The handles
of chisels and saws, and the wooden blocks of planes, were made by the
carpenter himself to suit the feel of his own hand and the balance of his body

THE RITUALS OF TOOLS » 9

## S. 33

Fig. 4. Votive painting (ema) of carpentry tools. 1814. Shinshö-ji, Narita.

—
Fig. 5. Eighteenth-century set of ceremonial carpenter’s tools.

10 » THE WAY OF THE CARPENTER

## S. 34

and mind. The steel cutting blades were fashioned by a smith working to the
carpenter’s specifications. Tools so formed from a blend of personality and
material technology became the embodiment of the carpenter’s technical skills
and an expression of his personality. He cherished and cared for them as if they
were his own children. He addressed them with the respect accorded his
parents. He spent many hours each day patiently and expertly sharpening their
cutting edges. The greatest care was always taken in their handling and storage.
One of the last traditionally trained surviving master temple carpenters,
Nishioka Tsunekazu, recalls:

When my uncle was still young he would put each tool carefully into
its own special oiled-paper pouch before returning it to the box .....
He would treat his planes like precious hanging scrolls, placing each
one in its own separate pouch. He put chisels in their own special
sheaths before returning them to the toolbox. These days only the
most important paring chisels are put in their own sheaths—the rest
are just dropped back in asthey are... .*

The carpenter could follow the right path only if he used the appropriate tool in

the correct way for each task. With these tools he came to challenge the very
limits of materials and techniques. Some of the most elaborate, self-conscious
joinery found in premodern buildings is no mere subservience to structural
necessity. It is joyful exaltation in perfection of craft as art and the perfection of
self.

THE SECRET WORLD
OF THE MASTER CARPENTER

Ritualistic practices explain some of the challenges and problems encountered in
the study of Japanese carpenters’ tools. It is difficult for the outsider to gain
entry into the secret world of the profession that uses and cherishes them.
Historically, the veil of secrecy descended when it came to the transmission of
the specific carpentry techniques within a particular workshop or house. These
techniques belonged to a secret and circumscribed world to which admission
was frequently denied even to other members of the same guild. There are a
number of instances where guild regulations specify that “no apprentices
belonging to other domains (han) are to be accepted” to ensure that the secrecy
of teachings was not violated. The esoteric nature of workshop practices was
thus reinforced by craft guild sanctions. The same type of secrecy prevailed in
the European guilds. Even today a strictly trained hereditary carpenter never
shows his tools to the uninitiated because these tools are the embodiment of the
techniques and the traditions entrusted to him by his forebears. There are
carpenters who will display with cheerful readiness an impressive collection of
shiny and attractive tools. The real work, however, is done with other tools
never exposed, brought out only when they are called upon to perform their
appointed task—like the samurai sword which is never drawn except for the

THE SECRET WORLD OF THE MASTER CARPENTER » 11

## S. 35

killing stroke of the legendary one-stroke swordsman. Each tool has its strictly
defined moment and place of use. It is treated with the easy familiarity of long
acquaintance when put to proper use and with something bordering on religious
devotion when put to rest.

Improper use of tools or insensitive questions and photo i
desecration of a sacred trust and tradition. To the tee aphy consttuie a
initiated into the world of architecture and the Way of the Carpenter as a
member of the modern guild for traditional building craftsmen, the Kyöto Dentö
Kenchiku Gijutsu Kyökai, I am obliged to maintain the traditions and respect its
esoteric rituals as both profession and faith.

Carpentry tools, therefore, lie at the heart of the vocational practice and
spiritual beliefs of a complex profession. Tools assume their full and proper
stature, their rightful and complete meaning, only when understood as part of
the profession they serve as instruments of the Way of the Carpenter.

12 . THE WAY OF THE CARPENTER

## S. 36

CHAPTER TWO

Men, Materials and Methods

If it wishes to avoid the boredom of fixed abstractions, the book of tools
will also be the book of materials and trades.
—- Andre Velter and Marie-Jose Lamothe, Le livre de Foutil'

There are four keys to understanding the Way of the Carpenter. The first is the
carpenter himself, the particular skills and the orderly structure of his
profession. The second is the material he works, the inherent properties and
attributes of the different woods which form the structure and much of the
detailing of the Japanese building. The third is found in his methods of building
design and construction. The fourth key is his set of tools, the dögu, which link
man, materials and methods.

It is essential to examine the first three aspects before explaining the fourth,
the carpenter’s tools themselves.

THE PROFESSION OF THE CARPENTER

Who, then, is this person, the Japanese carpenter? Is he an artisan or an artist,
architect or builder? What does he call himself? How does he organize his
profession?

The essential point to make is that the carpenter is only one of the many
craftsmen engaged in the building process. Japanese architecture is the
composite of multiple trade traditions, each of which developed over many
generations. In addition to the carpenters there were stone masons, plasterers,
roof tilers, copper sheet roofers, thatchers and shinglers. There were also the
tateguya, makers of shoji sliding screens and sliding door frames for the thick
paper screens, as well as the makers of tatami mats (fatamiya), each an
indispensable part of the residential interior. The cabinet makers, an allied
profession, crafted the chests (tansu) and other interior furnishings while the box
maker (sashimonoshi) fashioned containers of paulownia wood for storing works

THE PROFESSION OF THE CARPENTER « 13

## S. 37

of art and daily utensils. Fellers and sawyers were responsible for the harvesting
and transportation of trees as well as their metamorphosis into workable
timbers. .

These trades constitute the inner circle of the building professions. The
pattern of professions involved in the building process spread like the ripples on
the surface of a pond. Master smiths specialized in forging the laminated steel
blades for planes, chisels and saws. They also manufactured mountains of iron
nails and clamps needed to strengthen the timber-frame structure. Makers of
mulberry paper provided the coverings for the shoji screens, the width of the
paper helping to determine the spacings of the frame made by the screen
makers. Ornamental metal workers, painters, sculptors and lacquer craftsmen
each added their own special dimension to the finish of the building.

These professions were organized in similar hereditary and behavioral
patterns, but each was autonomous in administration and activity. Carpenters
and roof tilers still sit in separate groups around the communal fire for morning
teatime at wintry building sites, long accustomed to each other’s presence at the
building site but exchanging little more than formal courtesies with each other.

In the pyramid of building professions and allied craft trades, it was the
carpenter who was preeminent. This was not due to any obvious difference in
status or pay scale but simply because the upper echelon of master carpenters
was responsible for the overall design and execution of the building. The work
of the other professions in all respects conformed to the master plan devised by
the chief master carpenter. The carpenter has always projected a powerful
presence at every building site. He has left the indelible stamp of his personality
on each building he has constructed and each tool he has used. We may not
always know his name but we can sometimes recognize the special techniques
of the workshop to which he belonged. Numerous written and pictorial sources
also help us to identify him. Moreover, most buildings of consequence had a
wooden plaque (munafuda) attached to the ridgepole listing information about
the builders, particularly the carpenters, who participated in the project.
Government documents from the Edo period record with scrupulous detail the
organization and status of the vast armies of carpenters working in official
service. Families such as the Heinouchi and the Köra rose from artisan status to
become, in effect, official court architects, creating some of the most lavish and
impressive monuments ever built.

The carpenter, therefore, must be seen as both artisan and artist, architect and
builder. Like the medieval mason of Europe, the Japanese carpenter designed
and built in a single, organic process which will be described later in the chapter.
It is important to note here that this process is very different from architectural
practice today. The modern architectural profession makes a doctrinaire
distinction between design and execution. This is reinforced by separate training
for architects and builders, making it difficult to appreciate the role of the
traditional master carpenter who did both.

The Japanese word daiku, the name by which carpenters are generally known,
reflects this variety of roles. Daiku translates literally as “great craftsman” or
“chief builder.” It is significant to recall that the classical Greek term “architect”
actually also meant “chief builder.” The Japanese master carpenter, therefore,

14 « MEN, MATERIALS AND METHODS

## S. 38

Fig. 6. Chief master builder
(töryö) directs building
assembly. Detail of pl. 4,
Matsuzaki tenjin engi emaki,
scroll 6, 1311.

should be seen as an architect in the Greek meaning of the word because of his
responsibility for design as well as execution.

Originally the term daiku had no specific reference to the worker of wood. It
was reserved for the upper end of the hierarchy in all the major building
Professions. Hence the chief roof tiler was known as kawara-daiku. During the
Seventeenth and eighteenth centuries the usage changed, perhaps reflecting the
dominance of the master carpenter at the building site, and daiku became the
generic word for a carpenter. The chief master carpenter became known as the
föryö, a metonymic title which means literally “ridgepole,” the highest placed
beam in the structure of the building and, by inference, the top of the carpentry
hierarchy.

Within traditional carpentry practice there was an extensive hierarchy based
on the master-disciple relationship common to all hereditary trades, religious
institutions and performing arts. The töryö was the director of the building
Project, supervising both its design and execution and answerable only to the
Patron or client (fig. 6). In the wake of Western influence, this role has been
allocated to the architect who designs but does not build.

THE PROFESSION OF THE CARPENTER » 15

## S. 39

Fig. 7. Gatehouse (Sanmon), Nanzen-ji, Kyoto, ca. 1620.

Daiku were organized into family workshops based on a system of hereditary
transmission of skills and techniques. Different workshops specialized in
different types and styles of building construction, not unlike different sects
within the same religious faith. The most specialized categories were the miyadaiku, the carpenters responsible for temple and shrine architecture, and the
sukiya-daiku, who specialized in the construction of “teahouse-style” buildings
especially for aristocrats, warriors and wealthy merchants.

The high degree of differentiation in building practices between carpentry
specializations led to diversification of the form and function of the tools they
used. The miya-daiku devoted their energies to large-scale structures with a
preponderance of round pillars surmounted by multiple-arm bracket sets
supporting the eaves (fig. 7). These temple and shrine carpenters used sturdy
cutting tools to fashion large structural members and needed sophisticated
marking and measuring instruments to determine the all-important proportions
of the building and the curvature of the roof. Major temples and shrines had
their own hereditary carpenters, as well as members of other building
professions, living in the vicinity of their main gateway and charged with the
upkeep of the buildings generation after generation. This symbiotic relationship
with religious institutions undoubtedly influenced the organization and
character of the miya-daiku workshops. Separate schools of carpentry (ryi)

16 » MEN, MATERIALS AND METHODS

## S. 40

Fig. 8. Front elevation showing rectilinear style of temple design (Wa-yö): Great Lecture
Hall (Daiködö), Höryü-ji, Nara. Late tenth century. Höryü-ji Restoration Report.

Fig. 9. Front elevation showing curvilinear style of temple design (Zenshü-yö):
Jizödö (Hall to the Guardian Deity of Children), Shöfuku-ji, Tokyo. Late
thirteenth century. Shöfuku-ji Restoration Report.

evolved from the experience of the workshops attached to great temples such as
Höryü-ji and Kennin-ji. The Höryü-ji school of temple carpentry, based on the
venerable center of Buddhist devotion and culture of that name near Nara,
emphasized simple rectilinear design (fig. 8) while the Kennin-ji school,
influenced by Song Chinese styles in the medieval period, delighted in complex
curvilinear effects (fig. 9).

THE PROFESSION OF THE CARPENTER « 17

## S. 41

Fig. 10. Hamlet in Hakone Mountains, 1874. Farmhouse construction was
the task of the entire community in premodern Japan. The Far East, 1874.

The sukiya-daiku was the magician with materials and interior finish rather
than the master of large-scale structural framing. Although he used many of the
same basic tools as did the miya-daiku, he worked with a wider variety of
timber textures. The tateguya, for his part, specialized in making interior fittings,
particularly delicate shoji and fusuma frames, as well as wooden doors and
shelving. His tools include highly specialized hand tools similar to those used by
cabinet makers in the West.

The kobiki, or sawyers, who cut logs into required lengths and thicknesses,
were a crucial part of the process of constructing a building, though they came
to be regarded as a separate profession from the daiku. They specialized in the
use of logging saws and wide-bladed ripsaws for cutting planks, and some of
these saws were lethal if misused.

Many specializations, therefore, fall within the broad category of carpentry.
With the passage of time, these specializations became increasingly strict in
definition, analogous to the emergence of trade distinctions among building
guilds in Europe in late medieval times. A similar process of separation of
tateguya from daiku (see chapter 4) may be seen in the distinction that emerged
in England in the sixteenth century between the Guild of Carpenters,
responsible for structural work, and the Guild of Joiners, responsible for doors,
windows and paneling.

At the other end of the scale there were carpenters who were not members of

18 © MEN, MATERIALS AND METHODS

## S. 42

Fig. 11. Framework of a thatched roof farmhouse held together with rope lashings.

any specialist building trade. These were the part-time builders of the rural
dwellings, the traditional farmhouses which have now all but disappeared from
the Japanese landscape (fig. 10). Although assisted by a carpenter, who moved
around the entire district determining floor plans and making the most
important pillars and beams, most of the building process was in the hands of
the people of the village. Men and women worked together in teams,
performing the task of construction and periodic repair in the same spirit of
community solidarity in which they planted and harvested rice. They used
farming implements such as scythes and knives, along with rope and large
wooden mallets, to assemble the structure. The framing on which the outer
covering was arranged frequently consisted of little more than light saplings
bound together with smoked vines or straw rope (fig. 11). Thatching was done
with a large needle measuring up to a meter in length and a wooden paddle
used to beat the straw or reed into place. In many regions roofs were covered
with wooden shingles, which required little skill to place on the roof frame. They
were simply laid out in regular rows and weighed down with a framework of
bamboo and heavy stones.

This was building work far removed in tools and techniques from the
sophisticated mortise-and-tenon joinery framing of the great temples and
warrior mansions. It was an integral part of the way of life of the countryside but
was not a part of the specialist Way of the Carpenter.

THE PROFESSION OF THE CARPENTER « 19

## S. 43

THE CARPENTER’S WOODS

The mode of selection and preparation of wood is the second key to
understanding the Way of the Carpenter. Function and aesthetics dictated
selection of materials for the various parts of the traditional building, and woods
were chosen for their role according to their special structural Properties and
aesthetic attributes. In traditional practice, the master Carpenter himself
Preferred to select the most important timber for a building from trees in the
mountain forests. The dimensions and character of the wood available for a
Project helped to determine the form of the building. The natural curve of a
beam might dictate the configuration of the entire truss. A Prized piece of wood
owned by the patron or a pillar taken from an earlier building might set the
mood and design for a particular room. .

Many different types of timbers are used in Japanese buildings. These can be
divided into separate structural and decorative categories.

Structural Timbers

A timber-frame forms the structure of traditional architecture in Japan and
softwoods have been the preferred timber for this role. “Softwood” is not
necessarily a soft piece of wood but a misleading term for coniferous woods,
many of which are quite hard. These are distinguished from broadleafed trees,
which are known in general as “hardwood.” .

Hinoki, Japanese cypress (Chamaecyparis obtusa), is a softwood that is the
Preeminent structural wood used in Japan. It is a conifer notable for beauty,
strength and resilience without undue weight or propensity to warping. It grows
to a height of 40 to 50 meters with a fine, straight grain (fig. 12). These
characteristics make it easy to work with hand tools and to split lengthwise with
wedges to fashion pillars and beams. In modern times, hinoki grown in the
cold, mountainous regions of Kiso (Nagano Prefecture), Tamba (Kyoto
Prefecture) and Yoshino (Nara Prefecture) are the most valued timbers in Japan.
It has been grown and cut at the foot of Mount Odaigahara, known as the “roof
of the Kinki region,” for many centuries. In ancient times, hinoki flourished in
the mountains throughout the Asuka-Nara area, which was the focus of major
building activity. It was used for most monumental buildings erected between
the sixth and the thirteenth centuries. Hinoki pillars still survive in such
buildings as the five-storied pagoda of Höryü-ji, which was constructed over a
thousand years ago.

One of the most remarkable features of hinoki is its longevity. Scientific tests
have found that it is 30 percent stronger two hundred years after it is first cut.
Thereafter it gradually weakens until a thousand years later, when it has
returned to its original strength. The secret of its strength lies in the fine grain
(only about one millimeter between growth rings) which aids oil retention.

Diminishing supplies of hinoki forced builders to substitute red pine
(akamatsu; Pinus densiforia) and, more importantly, keyaki (Zelkova accuminita) for
the sweet-tempered hinoki. In their natural state these woods grow curved or
crooked, making it difficult to fashion them into the straight parts of a building.

20 »- MEN, MATERIALS AND METHODS

## S. 44

ARNO \ N Fi
ig. 12. Hinoki (Japanese cypress) pillar, Main Fig. 13. Keyaki (zelkova) pillar, Belltower,
East Gatehouse (Higashi Öte-mon), Nijö Kiyomizu-dera, Kyoto. Early seventeenth
Castle, Kyoto. Early seventeenth century. century.

Moreover, red pine is vulnerable both to damp and to attack by insects. The
deciduous keyaki, which closely resembles elm, is hard and has a convoluted
grain that makes it difficult to work (fig. 13). Although stronger than hinoki
when first cut, its coarse grain (up to five millimeters between growth rings)
causes more rapid loss of essential oil; it dries and ages more quickly and is less
able to resist rainwater as a consequence. It is not hard to imagine that such a
radical change in timber type had a strong affect on carpentry tools and
techniques. This will be examined more closely in chapter 4.

The use of hinoki, red pine and keyaki was limited principally to the
monuments and residences of the elite because of high cost and status
prerequisites. Black pine (kuromatsu; Pinus thunbergi) and horse chestnut (tochi;
Aesculus turbinata) were favored by farmers and townspeople to build their
residences and workplaces because of the lower price and ready availability.

Decorative Timbers

Connoisseurship in the selection and placement of woods required for the
interior fittings determined the character of residential architecture, especially in
the sukiya, or teahouse, style. Choice ranged from consciously rustic, unhewn

THE CARPENTER’S WOODS » 21

## S. 45

ZRRDEN

Fig. 14. Tokonoma with main pillar.

timbers, bark, thatch and bamboo to refined woods, such as the imported
rosewood (sendan; Melia azedaruch) and ebony (kokutan; Diospyros ebenum) used
for the ornamental shelving in the Shin Goten of Katsura Imperial Villa.

Japanese cedar (sugi; Cryptomeria japonica), a straight-grained conifer that is
soft and easy to work, is prized for ornamental lintels (nageshi) and door panels.
Kitayama sugi, artificially modified during growth to produce an irregularly
patterned surface, is coveted for tokobashira, the main pillar framing one side of
the tokonoma alcove (fig. 14). Unhewn Japanese cherry (sakura; Prunus japonica),
with its natural textured surface and rich color, is also frequently found in this
position. In sukiya architecture, many of the over one hundred species of
bamboo available in Japan form lively tokonoma lintels and the eaves raftering
adjacent to the garden.

The characteristic large burrs and lovely figured grain of keyaki, which make
it difficult to work with hand tools, ironically make it particularly desirable for
decorative features such as the main ledge of the tokonoma. The timber’s
hardness is well suited for carved pillar nosings, transom friezes and other
sculpture. From 1668, sumptuary legislation issued by the Tokugawa shogunal
government restricted the use of keyaki for gateways and cedar for door panels
to the mansions of the highest ranking members of the samurai class.

Shinto shrines and many later Buddhist temples had raised wooden floors,
frequently made of hinoki. Farmhouses and urban dwellings had one room with

22 © MEN, MATERIALS AND METHODS

## S. 46

a floor of pounded earth (doma), sometimes stone flagged, while the raised
wooden floor of the rest of the house was made of locally available timbers such
as black pine.

In contemporary Japan, these structural and decorative timbers are used only
for upper-class residences, restaurants and inns built in traditional style. The
threatened exhaustion of indigenous forests forced importation of hinoki in large
quantities from Taiwan in the postwar era. This timber has a lower density and
wider grain than Japanese hinoki because it is grown in a warmer climate. It also
has an alarming habit of splitting at an angle of forty degrees to its main axis.
Moreover, it shrinks and changes color to an unattractive grey hue, very
different from the constant dimensions and warm brown hue of native hinoki.
Lauan (Shorea contorta) from the Philippines, Malaysia and Indonesia is used for
Western-style interior finishing. Two-by-four studs imported from North
America are now a common substitute for conventional Japanese timber framing
and wall construction.

The character and qualities of the timbers used in traditional construction do
much to account for the character of Japanese woodworking tools. Reliance on
softer density conifers for structural purposes and some interior detailing has
lead to the razor-sharp cutting edges on saws, chisels and planes for which
Japanese tools are renowned. A wood of lower density demands a tool with a
sharper blade if the cutting stroke is to be carried out cleanly.

It should also be remembered that many of the timbers are exceedingly
valuable. In traditional practice the most important timbers were specially
selected and often transported over great distances. The main pillars for the
rebuilding of the great temple complex of Tödai-ji in Nara at the end of the
twelfth century had to be brought from mountain sides over a thousand
kilometers away to the west. Today a single trunk of top-grade cedar may cost as
much as an imported Mercedes-Benz. A carpenter cutting such expensive woods
literally cannot afford to make a single mistake, another reason for insistence
upon superlative quality in his tools.

METHODS OF BUILDING DESIGN
AND CONSTRUCTION

Historically there are many different types of Japanese buildings and a variety of
techniques for building them. Although most share common features such as a
timber-frame structure with in-fill walls and a graceful overhanging roof,
Buddhist temples and Shinto shrines, residences and castles, each required a
specialized branch of the carpentry profession to build them. Each of these
building types was different in scale, proportions, site organization, decorative
emphasis and symbolism. There are cinnabar-red painted temples with massive
framing like Tödai-ji and delicately proportioned plain wooden shrines like Ise.
Sumptuous palatial complexes like Nijö Castle in Kyoto stand in sharp contrast
to the restraint of Zen-inspired halls of meditation at the Ryögen-in of Daitoku-ji
two kilometers to the north. The daunting scale of the walls and keep of Himeji
Castle is difficult to equate with the microcosm of the three-and-three-quarters

METHODS OF BUILDING DESIGN AND CONSTRUCTION « 23

Eu

## S. 47

tatami-mat tearoom which became popular at the same time as the fortifications
of Himeji were constructed at the turn of the seventeenth century. Dramatic
differences in form and appearance do not always correspond with differences
in function. Shrines are usually restrained and temples ornate in appearance.
However, one of the most famous buildings in Japan is the Nikko Töshö-gü, a
shrine built with an ostentatious enthusiasm exceeding that of even the most
exuberant temple or palace. It contrasts sharply with some temples displaying
Shinto-like restraint such as Murö-ji, south of Nara.

The desires of the patrons, the conventions of the carpenters and other
craftsmen, the nature of the site and the character of the available materials,
together dictated the form of the building as these factors also dictated the
specialist workshop to which the patron turned. Yet all buildin

gs shared a
common sequence to design and construction.

Siting

After consultation concerning the size, function and budget of a building, the
master builder examined the site to determine the building’s exact placement
and favorable orientation based on both practical and geomantic considerations.
A southern orientation was the almost universal rule where conditions
permitted, for it assured both maximum sunlight and Protection from
malevolent cosmic forces dwelling in the north.

Measurement and Customary Design by Modules

In traditional practice, the construction of the building was not based on
detailed architectural drawings determined by stylistic theories but upon
modules grounded in traditional practice. Architectural drawings came into use
only by the sixteenth century, but more as a guide for patrons than for directing
the builders’ activities. The basic organization of the construction process was
based on a simple grid plan showing pillar placement set out on a large, flat plan
board kept at the building site.

Many of the precise dimensions of the parts of a building were more the
natural result of a process of interaction between man, materials and tools than
of arbitrary design will. Architecture was the art of the possible, and the
measurements of early buildings, including the famous temples and palaces of
Nara and Kyoto, were determined as much by the way the pieces of wood split
or could be cleaved as by any conscious intent.

Detailed designing was carried out during the construction process using
proportions based on modules that were themselves relative to the
Circumstances of practice. These proportions, or kiwari ("wood dividing”), were
applied automatically, and in many instances subconsciously, by the builder in
accordance with custom. Systems of proportions differed according to period,
region and individual workshop but were basically similar to those operating in
Classical and Renaissance architecture. In Japan, as in the Mediterranean region,
the primary module was the span of the post-and-lintel bay, the ken. This was
divided into six subunits called shaku (“feet”), each further divided into ten sun

24 . MEN, MATERIALS AND METHODS

## S. 48

(inches”). Each sun consisted of ten bu, and each bu of ten rin. Thus a pillar

Might be one shaku wide, or one-sixth of the width of the bay, in a number of

Uildings but the actual length might differ according to workshop tradition,

h yon of the particular building, or size and type of available structural
ers.

The tatami mat became a second important module of design in the sixteenth
and seventeenth centuries. With floors completely covered in tatami, pillars
were positioned according to the dimensions and arrangement of mats,
reversing the previous procedure in which mats were lain between existing
Pillars. This marked a shift from a structural module to a spatial module in
Japanese design. Like the ken, however, dimensions were not standardized until

© emergence of three basic mat sizes by the eighteenth century. Of these the

Oma (1,970 millimeters by 985 millimeters), which was used in Kyoto, was the
argest. The inakama (1,880 millimeters by 909 millimeters) was mainly found in
the Kanto region, while the Edo-tatami (1,757 millimeters by 879 millimeters) was
associated with Edo, the city that was the headquarters of the Tokugawa
Shogunate from the seventeenth century.

These measurements and modules also governed the dimensions and
Proportions of tools. Today the international metric system is in universal use in
Japan. In 1886 the Japanese government standardized measuring units according
to metric values in order to simplify international trading relationships.
Traditional modules thus came to have the specific metric measurements of a 3.
03 centimeter sun, a 30.3 centimeter shaku and a 1.82 meter ken.

Many of the dimensions of tools used today are still determined by the
traditional units as standardized in 1886 and, often as not, are based on the
simple logic of the older rule of thumb. Expressing such measurements in
modern metric equivalents tends to disguise their logic. For instance, it seems
Puzzling that a modern carpenter should have a jack plane which is 60.6
Centimeters in length until it is remembered that under the traditional
measurement system this was exactly 2 shaku long. Moreover, the width of the
arm of the carpenter’s square, the blade of a medium sized butt chisel and the
sole of a plough plane all measure 1.515 centimeters—not the most meaningful
of modern measures—which corresponds to 5 bu in traditional measure, 5 bu
being one of the units most commonly used as a module in interior details of
Japanese buildings. Measurements used for Japanese tools, therefore, make
better sense if the traditional units are kept in mind. For a fuller understanding
of the dimensions of Japanese tools, the table on page 8 provides traditional
measuring units alongside modern metric and U. S. equivalents.

Selection and Orientation of Timber

The carpenter believed that the spirit of the tree had to be accommodated in its
new location at the building site, but he had to know the environment in which
it grew to maturity for practical reasons as well. In this context the old adage
that the carpenter “does not buy a piece of wood but buys the mountain” (ki o
kawazu yama o kau) is very true. The original orientation of the timber to the
north or south had to be respected. This was not only to placate its spirit but also

METHODS OF BUILDING DESIGN AND CONSTRUCTION + 25

## S. 49

| y { . a J
w h }

ae { “) |

Fig. 15. Top, a carpenter using a chisel and mallet to cleave a piece of straight-grained timber;
center, a snap-line to mark a beam; bottom, and a sashigane and bamboo brush to draw a line
on the end of a pillar. Detail of pl. 2, Kasuga gongen genki-e, scroll 1, 1309.

to ensure the suitability of a timber to its new location in a building; south-side
wood is better adapted to warmth and should once more face south, whereas
north-side wood is better able to withstand cold temperatures as it confronts the
penetrating winds of winter.

Prefabrication of the Parts

Major pillars and beams and much of the interior detailing were prefabricated in
the carpentry workshop before assembly at the building site (pl. 2). Each timber
was measured and marked for cutting and labeled to indicate its location on the
grid of the plan board. The ink pot and snap line (sumitsubo) and carpenter’s
square (sashigane) were the main tools in this process (fig. 15).

The most time-consuming part of the prefabrication process was the cutting of
the mortise-and-tenon joints which held the building together. There were two
main types of joinery: spliced or scarf joints (fsugite) (fig. 16) for connecting
beams and sills end to end; and angled joints (shiguchi) (fig. 17) for securing
members at oblique and right angles. Some joints, like the dovetail, were simple
in design. Others were more complicated, particularly the larger horizontal

26 ® MEN, MATERIALS AND METHODS

## S. 50

Fig. 16. Gooseneck mortise-and-tenon Fig. 17. Angled joint (shiguchi).
joint (kama-tsugi). Example of spliced
or scarf joinery (fsugite).

Fig. 18. Roof framing crossbeam with tenon and scarf mortise
shown during restoration dismantling. Eighteenth century.

framing members with multiple beam intersections (fig. 18). Irrespective of
form, absolute precision in the measuring and cutting processes was essential to
ensure success in the assembly of the building.

METHODS OF BUILDING DESIGN AND CONSTRUCTION » 27

## S. 51

On-site Assembly

After the ground-breaking ceremony, the site of the building was laid out with a
grid of string markers corresponding to the plan board grid, and stone
foundations were then positioned. Water levels were used to ensure that the
ground or the podium on which the building sat had an horizontal surface. The
prefabricated parts for the frame were then assembled on the foundations. The
erection of the frame, which was a carefully rehearsed Performance, took as little
as a single day in the case of smaller residential buildings. Pillars were set on
their footings and braced with horizontal beams and ties. Pent-roofed (“lean-to”)
sections were added around the completed core frame (moya) of the building.
The roof truss of heavy horizontal ties and smaller vertical posts was placed on
top of the wall frame (pls. 3 and 4). Even today the activity at a construction site
during the assembly phase resembles an agitated beehive, with the carpenters
swarming everywhere in rapid motion.

Completion of the framing operation was marked by the ridge-raising
ceremony. Then followed the laying of roofing materials, which required a high
level of skill and precision. Tiles were laid in steady progression from ridge to
eaves and from right to left along the curving roof surface, pantiles (concave)
preceeding covertiles (convex) and the ridge sealed with capping tiles. In the
case of heavy terracotta tiling, its insistent vertical load also anchored the frame
to the foundations and strengthened the joinery interfaces against earthquake
jolts. Shingle and thatch roofs were built up from eaves to ridge and secured
with bamboo cleats and wooden battens. The roof provided convenient shelter
for the craftsmen who completed the walls and interior detailing. Sliding screens
and tatami, which were also prefabricated, slid readily into their prescribed
positions. Landscaping of the site proceeded concurrently, affecting visual
transitions and integration of the building with its environment. Customary
religious ceremonies marked the end of the project.

Having established a broad overview of the building professions and the
activities of the carpenter at the building site, it is now possible to examine in
detail the actual tools used for this process.

28 » MEN, MATERIALS AND METHODS

## S. 52

CHAPTER THREE

The Carpenter’s Set of Tools

The fourth key to understanding the Way of the Carpenter is through his tools.
The tools—the dögu—link the men, the materials and the methods in building
practice to create the structures and timber detailing Japanese architecture.
What, then, are these tools and how are they used?

The simple answer is that Japanese carpenters’ tools fall into four categories:
(1) design tools, particularly marking and measuring tools such as the
carpenter’s square; (2) cutting tools, particularly saws and chisels; (3) finishing
tools such as planes; and (4) tools for maintaining the tools themselves, such
as whetstones and files. There are, thus, both “instruments of the mind” and
“tools of the hand,” which reflects the dual vocation of the daiku as designerbuilder.

Some difficult problems of selection are encountered in explaining these tools
in detail. Tool types and usage have changed considerably during the course of
the fifteen hundred years of Japanese building for which records are available. If
the tools used today only are described here, many of the valuable discoveries
and much of the excitement in the Way of the Carpenter will be missed. Another
problem is that frequently there is more than one explanation for how a
particular chisel or a certain type of plane is used. There are also striking
regional differences in Japanese carpentry techniques and tool usage. In
premodern days a carpenter from the cold climate of northern Japan would have
found much interest in comparing his tools with those of a carpenter from the
warm regions of southern Kyushu—had they been able to understand each
other’s dialect. Even the names of tools and their parts may differ from one area
to another and from one historical period to the next. There may also be some
marked differences in tools used by carpenters specializing in different parts of
the same building.

The tools a carpenter uses today are by no means identical with those used by
his father a generation ago, and his grandfather would have been amazed at the
changes that have taken place in the last hundred years. Even the basic form of
such seemingly immutable tools as saws and planes has changed quite

THE CARPENTER’S SET OF TOOLS + 29

## S. 53

-|

dramatically. An encyclopedia could scarcely contain sufficient space for the |
answers to the deceptively simple “what” and “how” of tools.

Most of the major tool types used in traditional Carpentry are conveniently
included in a 1870 photograph showing two daiku at work (fig. 19). In the
foreground a carpenter uses a wide-bladed striking chisel (tataki-nomi) and steelheaded hammer (gennö) to start fashioning a mortise in a beam. Beside him rests
a sumitsubo, the indispensable marking instrument for all carpenters. Propped
against a wooden support is an adze (chöna), used for rough finishing of timber
surfaces, together with a sashigane and a single-edged ripsaw (tatebiki-noko),
indicated by the slightly concave curve of the top edge of its blade. In the
background another daiku uses a finishing plane to create a smooth surface on a
plank. Some caution must be exercised in assessing this seemingly irrefutable
documentary evidence. The tools are real but a suspicion lingers that the men
are not carpenters but actors set up by a keen foreign Photographer of the exotic.
What daiku ever worked on a beam Iying directly on the ground without
wooden blocks for stabilizing it and also helping to keep it clean? Even the angle
of the planing board is too steep for actual use. Nevertheless, the information
about different tool types which were in general use in 1870 remains useful.

Today the typical building site looks very different from this late nineteenthcentury scene. The modern carpenter uses a combination of power tools and
some hand tools for final finishing (fig. 20). Tools that were once mainstays of
the building professions, such as the adze and the wide-bladed ripsaw, are now
virtually extinct, and the number as well as the variety of specialized planes and
chisels has decreased dramatically. However, standard tools in common use
until a generation ago may be identified by checking the listings in a survey
conducted by the Institute of Labor Science (Rödö Kagaku Kenkyüjo) in 1943-44.!
The survey reached the startling conclusion that the daiku needed a minimum of
179 tools for full-scale work. Most numerous were the chisels of which no fewer
than 49 were deemed essential, reflecting their important role in making the
mortises and tenons, the basis of the traditional structural system. Closely
following the chisels in importance were the 40 required planes; 26 gimlets; 17
different measuring and marking tools such as squares, sumitsubo and marking
gauges; 12 saws; 9 different nail pincers and claws; 6 hammers and mallets; 1
adze; 1 hewing axe;'6 whetstones; and 5 files. Western influence is also evident
in the inclusion of a brace and bits, spirit level, adjustable wrench, spanner and 2
screwdrivers.

By the time the Institute of Labor Science survey was actually published,
delayed in the aftermath of the war until 1959, its editors indicated postwar
trends by noting that “today, with the gradual mechanization of building
processes, there are some tools among these which can no longer be considered
essential.”? Karai Kunio of the Takenaka Carpentry Museum estimates that the
ordinary carpenter today regularly uses no more than about eighty tools,
including power tools. A top-quality carpenter may still possess over two
hundred tools but will actually use only about one hundred and twenty of these.

Whatever the changes reflected in, or anticipated by, the Labor Science list of
tools, it serves as an important benchmark for establishing the identity of tools
in service at the end of the age of traditional building practice in Japan. This

30 » THE CARPENTER’S SET OF TOOLS

## S. 54

Fig. 19. “Japanese carpenters” and Fig. 20. Hand and power saws
their tools. The Far East, 1870. at a modern building site.

chapter introduces and explains the characteristics and function of the most
important of the tools in the listing, together with other essential instruments of
traditional practice, which were disappearing even in 1943. Such doomed items
include the long measuring rod, the plan board, manuals of building
proportions and style, as well as scale models of buildings, all indispensable to
the carpenter’s role as both architect and builder in traditional practice.

Tools are presented, as far as practical, in the sequence in which they have
been used at the building site. We shall start with design, measuring and
marking tools, follow by cutting and finishing tools and conclude with tools
used to make and maintain the others.

INSTRUMENTS OF THE MIND—
TOOLS OF DESIGN

The master carpenter planned and prepared building construction with the plan
board and measuring and marking tools, including the carpenter’s square, the
long measuring rod and the inkpot and pen. These are instruments of the
creative mind, conditioned by the collective inherited knowledge of a workshop
tradition, as well as by the personal experience of the individual carpenter. The
master carpenter’s phenomenal memory was aided by secret manuals of
building proportions and style as well as models to establish overall spatial
effects and guide assembly.

INSTRUMENTS OF THE MIND « 31

## S. 55

Plan Board (ita-zu or ezu-ita)

At first glance, the plan board (fig. 21) may not appear to be a tool at all. We tend
to see building plans as the province of the architect, separated from the process
of building. However, in the Way of the Carpenter, the plan board serves as an
instrument of cardinal importance in designing and building as one organic
process. In traditional practice, the daiku as both the architect and builder drew
the main drawings used for planning and constructing the building on this plan
board.

The plan board bears little resemblance to the modern foreman’s clipboard
with its folded blueprints. As the name suggests, it was a simple plan set out on
thin pieces of board. It varied in size but could be as large as three-by-two
meters, depending on the scale of the project. A board was used for its durability
and ease in handling. It could be propped up in a central position at the building
site with the drawings clearly visible to all workers on the project.

The plan was boldly drawn in ink with the position of pillars, verandas and
entrances marked. It rarely included any written notations or units of measure
because the design of the building was based on customary modules, such as the
span of the bay, discussed earlier. Detailed application of the module to decide
actual dimensions was predetermined by the tradition of the carpenter’s
workshop, hence the absence of specific measurements. Constant reference was
made to the plan board when the individual members of the structure were
being measured, marked for cutting and labeled with their ultimate location.
The individual members were identified by a grid system of cross references
using numbers and the traditional i-ro-ha phonetic sequence. The most common
system has the kana syllabary running across the top of the plan from right to

32 » THE CARPENTER’S SET OF TOOLS

## S. 56

left, the standard arrangment of any sequence in Japanese writing, with
Numbers running down the right-hand side of the board. There are regional
Yariations; in the Tohoku region of northeastern Honshu, for example, the
Numbers ascend the board from the bottom right. For the more complex
buildings like large-scale temples, a gable elevation would sometimes be
Superimposed on the plan, or even an entire elevation drawn, but for the most
part the entire process of construction was based upon the simple plan board.
No identification of the slope of the roof, instructions concerning materials or
Jöinery type, nor any detailed working drawings were normally included. A
daiku was expected to select unerringly the type of joint required for each
Situation and the type of wood that should be used for the different parts of the

uilding. Even at construction sites today, some carpenters still take the scaled
plan, elevation and sections prepared by the architect and then draw a
traditional plan board for their working reference.

The Carpenter’s Square (sashigane or kanejaku)

The Measuring and marking of timbers (sumitsuke) is the most critical part of a
“arpenter’s work. The success of the building project depends upon the accuracy
0f this process even more than it does in the West because there is no allowable
Margin for correction once the rapid assembly process commences. The accuracy
Of the measurements, therefore, will be confirmed only when the building is
Completed, by which time it is too late to correct deviations. Measuring and
Marking is supervised by the chief master carpenter. The first and foremost of
his tools is the sashigane.

The sashigane (fig. 22) looks very similar to the Western set square but in
Japanese building practice this tool is more versatile and of far greater
Importance. It forges the link between the basic plan set out on the plan board
and the construction process by providing a system for calculating actual
dimensions, angles and curvature. In other words, it is the means by which
theory is translated into practice. For this reason alone it might be called the
inost indispensable of all the carpenter’s tools because it is an implement both of
design and execution, reflecting the role of the master carpenter as designer
through the actual process of building.

. The sashigane is used first to set right angles and to measure and mark the
timbers in the prefabrication phase of construction. Various scales of
Measurement are indicated on both front (omoteme) and back (urame) of each arm
(fig. 22). The outer edges of the front arms have the basic units in either
traditional measure, from ri to shaku, or contemporary metric units. The arms
themselves are 5 bu or 1.515 centimeters in width, which makes them a “ready
teckoner” for one of the most useful of the traditional modules used by
Carpenters. Before all measurements were standardized in 1886, the sashigane
and long measuring rule were used to establish the precise correlation between
the modular order and physical dimensions.

The sashigane is also used by the carpenter to set angles. By trigonometry the
Sashigane determines angles other than ninety degrees and can set the graceful
line of the eaves (fig. 23). For example, one shaku of horizontal run to six sun of

INSTRUMENTS OF THE MIND « 33

## S. 57

Fig. 22. Carpenter’s squares (sashigane). They are made now from
stainless steel but originally were fashioned from bamboo.

vertical rise creates a roof at the desirable rain-shedding angle of approximately
thirty degrees (fig. 24).

The rear of the sashigane has several different scales that considerably
simplify the daily work of the daiku as both designer and worker of wood,
reflecting some of the special characteristics of the Japanese design tradition.
Together with the units on the front, they furnish the specific dimensions, angles
and curves set out as a general plan on the plan board. The rear arms are marked
along the outer edges with the square root of the units of measure on the front
side. This permits the carpenter to determine the curvature of roof-framing
members by using tangents and parabolas based on the units of measure shown
on the front scales. The most difficult part of the entire building to design and
build was the eaves area, the noki-mawari, and many of the techniques for using
the root scales on the rear of the sashigane were invented to ensure that the
horizontal members and supports of the eaves edge rose in the precisely
coordinated curves that are one of the most beautiful features of Japanese
architecture. Building lore includes the delightfully evocative expression
“swallows sing but carpenters weep at the eaves” (daiku to suzume wa noki de
naku), which speaks vividly of the trials and tribulations involved in making
sure that the two layers of rafters and their horizontal supports (kioi and kayaoi),
employed for the most important buildings, were correctly positioned (fig. 30).

The root scale on the rear of the long arm may also be used to calculate the

the dimensions of a squared timber which can be cut from a log: (a) Draw the
diameter AB. Measure the radius OB using the urame scale (urame a); (b) Flip the
carpenter’s square over to the front side (omoteme) and take a reading of the OB
distance in standard measure (omoteme a); Use this measurement to determine the
postion of D on the log; (c) Use the same procedure to fix the position of C. Then
draw a straight line between C and D. Position E, F, G and H by drawing lines at
right angles through C and D. Finally link up E, F, G and H to complete the
dimensions of the timber which is to be cut from the log.

dimensions of a squared piece of timber cut from a round trunk. The ingenious
procedure for performing this calculation is set out in figure 25, and its historical
significance is explained in chapter 4. The rear of the longer arm also has a
reversed standard scale, which is useful for determining restricted-access short
measures like the depth of a mortise or the length of a tenon. In addition to these
important functions of measuring and designing, the rear inside edge of the long
arm has a special scale which divines good or ill fortune (monjaku), giving
recognition to unseen forces in the lengths of the parts of the building.

The sashigane was once made from bamboo, which does not expand or
contract to distort measurements and can be bent to draw curved lines. Today it
is made from stainless steel with flexibility similar to bamboo. The arms are

concave in section with the outer edges thicker to prevent the ink of the marking
pen from blotting.

## S. 60

Fig. 26. Left, chief master builder holds long measuring rod (kensao)
and plan board (ita-zu); right, markings on measuring rod to establish
