lintel (kamoi).

Long Measuring Rod (shakuzue or kensao)

The shakuzue is a long pole marked with shaku units. It was either 10 shaku (1 j0)
or 6 shaku (1 ken) in length (fig. 26). This tool, along with the sashigane, was one
of the simplest but most important instruments for turning the design set out on
the plan board into an actual building. It established an exact linear value for
plan board modules when laying out the building site and measuring the length
of pillars and beams. As a major tool of both design and execution, like the
sashigane, it was particularly important for designing on the vertical plane. The
chief master carpenter marked the pole with the height of lintels, ceilings and
other horizontal members after carefully studying the plan board. The exact
measurements were based on his inherited knowledge of proportions. It was
thereafter a simple and efficient procedure to mark off the position of these
important members within the completed building frame.

The growth of a Western-style architectural profession in Japan from the latter
part of the nineteenth century eroded the design prerogatives of the carpentry
profession and rendered the practice of design by plan board, square and
measuring rod obsolete, despite its efficiency and subtle accomplishments.

INSTRUMENTS OF THE MIND « 37

## S. 61

Carpenters’ Manuals and Pattern Books

Carpenters’ manuals and pattern books, known variously as hidensho ("secret
hereditary writings”), hinagata-bon (“pattern books”) and gijutsusho (“technique
books”), are not “how to build” manuals in the sense of the trade manuals and
amateur instruction books familiar to us today. They do not explain how to
plane a beam or make a gooseneck mortise-and-tenon joint. They set out in
written and diagramatic form the systems of proportions, or kiwari, which were
the basis for designing buildings in traditional practice. They were based on the
cumulative wisdom and experience of successive generations of carpenters in
constructing buildings that were structurally sound and conformed to
acceptable aesthetic norms. By the eighteenth and nineteenth centuries, the
aesthetic considerations of proportions had become more important than
structural concerns.

Although manuals date back to at least the fourteenth century, it was not until
woodblocks were used to print them in the later seventeenth century that they
came into general circulation outside the restricted family circles for which they
were originally intended (fig. 28). This is discussed further in chapter 4.

These books contain written descriptions of the proportions to be employed
in preparing the parts of the buildings, from primary pillars to lintels for the
tokonoma alcove. Drawings accompanying the written texts set out the basic
dimensions and show the method of using the carpenter’s square for different
building types and characteristic features, especially the roof gables. The
manuals frequently consisted of separate volumes for gateways, shoin-style
residences, temple halls and pagodas as well as shrine buildings. Vernacular
housing was rarely included. However, the fact that the woodblock-printed
manuals were available to ordinary town carpenters served to diffuse elements
of high-culture style into the buildings of the general populace.

With the erosion of hereditary practice since the 1870s, these books have
become an important aid to carpenters in preserving their building traditions.
Contemporary publications continue to present information from the old
manuals in updated form.

The Buke hinagata (Pattern book of samurai architecture), first published in
1655, is one of the most important of the manuals that establish standards for
architectural design and offer insights into the approach to design in the Way
of the Carpenter. The section describing the munekado, a gable-roof gateway
used by important aristocrats and prelates (fig. 27), reveals the typical building
blocks of the carpenter’s design method. It is useful to see what it says in literal
terms:

The width of the [two] pillars should be calculated as a sun. The pillars
are round. The principal crossbeams and the subsidiary crossbeams are
5 1/2 bu in height. Their lower surface [i.e., width] is one-third [of the
diameter of the pillar].

From this statement we can deduce that the gateway is designed using two
proportionally interrelated modules, the width of the bay and the diameter of

INSTRUMENTS OF THE MIND + 39

## S. 63

the main pillars. We know that, in accordance with general practice, the actual
measurements varied according to the particular structure. The terms sun and bu
are familiar modular building blocks. The space between the pillars is '
considered to be one shaku module wide. As ten sun make one shaku, the pillar is
one-tenth the width of the bay. Hence the carpenter is really saying: “The
circumference of the [two] pillars should to be calculated as one-tenth of the
width of the bay. The height of the principal and subsidiary crossbeams is 0.55 '
of the diameter of the main pillars, and their width is 0.34 of the diameter of
the pillars.” The similarity of these Japanese design manuals to the treatises
of building proportions by Vitruvius and Palladio is intriguing, perhaps
the inevitable result of a similar conjunction of trabeated building practice with
the need for standardization within an increasingly idealized conception of
style.

In the latter nineteenth century, some printed manuals were written to explain
the complicated techniques for building the eaves using the sashigane. Until
then, manuals had concentrated on aspects of building proportion, but the
breakdown of the traditional training system for apprentices meant that basic
building techniques had to be explained in written and diagramatic form for the
first time. A good example is an 1883 manual that includes a diagram showing
the intersection of the eaves horizontal support beam (kayaoi) with the corner
rafter. This is the most important part of the structure of the roof frame and is
critical for creating a graceful roof line (fig. 29). The manual also includes
detailed diagrams showing the method for arranging the horizontal supports for
double-layer raftering (fig. 30). It is a comment on the extraordinary
circumstances of the day, the breakdown of hereditary transmission of
knowledge and technique, that explicit information about fundamental building
Practices was freely distributed in printed form.

Models

Two types of models were used by daiku, wooden models and “pop-up plans” '
(okoshi-ezu). Both were employed in particularly complex projects to enable the
daiku to judge the precise effects created by the combinations of materials, light
and space, and to experiment with special design and assembly techniques. The
Pop-up plans were made from a single piece of paper and folded up to create a
three-dimensional model (fig. 32). The wooden models were made to scale and
showed major structural features as well as special details. A scale of one to ten
was most frequently employed, which meant that models of larger buildings
sometimes exceeded three meters in height (fig. 31). Model construction
demanded great skill, and considerable care was needed to make all the
structural features to precise scale. Sometimes models still remain for buildings
that no longer survive, which makes them priceless historical sources.

Models are still made as part of the restoration of historic buildings, and
many of them are preserved in the Tokyo National Museum and the National
Museum of Japanese History at Sakura, Chiba Prefecture. Today it costs almost

as much to make a model of a historic building as it does to build a ordinary
house.

Ink Pot and Snap Line (sumitsubo)

The uniquely shaped sumitsubo (pl. 1, figs. 33 and 34) is the traditional symbol
of the carpentry profession. Sumitsubo, richly lacquered and inlaid with mother
of pearl, have long served with ornamental carpenter’s squares and adzes at
Shinto offering altars erected at ground-breaking and ridge-raising ceremonies
(fig. 5). In appearance the sumitsubo resembles a shallow-draught river barge
propelled by a paddle wheel. As its name implies, it is the standard container for
soot-black ink (sumi) used in conjunction with a bamboo brush for marking
purposes. It also serves a variety of other important functions including that of a
snapline for marking timbers and a plumbline for ensuring verticality.

The sumitsubo consists of a block of wood, usually the intricately grained
keyaki (zelkova), with one end round like a gourd and hollowed out to form the
ink pot. A wad of cotton saturated in sumi is placed in this cavity. The opposite
end holds a wheel-shaped spool wound with some seven meters of line. This
line runs through the body of the sumitsubo across the inked cotton wad and
out through a small hole drilled in the wood. The string is tied to a small piece of
rounded wood that has a sharp pin projecting from it. The tool is now ready for
marking long timbers with a straight line by the simple expedient of drawing

42 » THE CARPENTER’S SET OF TOOLS

## S. 66

Ag
W.H.C BT.

Fig. 33. Sumitsubo (ink pot and snap line), symbol of the carpenter.

the line out over the ink to a predetermined point on the timber. It is secured in
position by pushing the attached pin into the wood. The line is then pulled taut
and allowed to snap back against the wood. A clear, straight mark is left down
the entire length of the timber, even when its surface is irregular. The string is
wound again onto the wheel, thereby re-inking it in readiness for the next use.
The line must be extremely light but strong, without any fine hairs or fluff which
would leave a furry impression on the wood. The best type of line is made of
thread from wild silkworms in Nagano and Hiroshima prefectures.

The sumitsubo works on the same principle as the chalk line of Western
woodworking tradition. European sawyers also used a long string soaked with
lampblack and water to leave a firm black line to guide their work.

The carpenter customarily has a separate sumitsubo for black and for red ink.
The sumi is mixed with seaweed glue (funori) to make it insoluble in water so
that important markings and numbering will not be washed away by rain
during the building process. The red ink sumitsubo is intended for use in sukiya
architecture, particularly on timbers in important parts of the building that will
be visible to the naked eye. The ink in this case is made from soluble red ochre
Powder so it can be washed off after the measuring, cutting and assembling
processes have been completed. In addition to its functions as an ink pot and
Pluck line, the sumitsubo is sometimes used as a plumb line to test verticality, its
body acting as the plumb weight (fig. 6).

The size and decoration of sumitsubo vary considerably with the variation in
the building practices of different workshops and regional locations. Most
sumitsubo in use in residential projects are approximately 20 to 30 centimeters in
length. Traditionally, larger ones were required for temple and shrine
construction because longer pillars and beams were used. These were often
twice the size of the ordinary sumitsubo and their line length was doubled to
approximately 14.5 meters. Sumitsubo made in the Kanto region, around what is
today Tokyo, have handles to roll up the inking line, but those from older

INSTRUMENTS OF THE MIND «+ 43

## S. 67

Fig. 34. Sumitsubo and bamboo drawing pens.

traditions in the Kansai (Kyoto, Nara, Osaka) region do not have handles, the
line being rewound by simply rotating the wheel rapidly with a stroking action
of the hand.

The ink pot provides the carpenter with the opportunity for self-expression
through the carving of its shape as well as decoration. It is the most consciously
decorative of all his tools, its surfaces often embellished with carvings of cranes,
turtles and carp swimming in the sea. Decorating the body of the sumitsubo
with felicitous creatures from the storehouse of Asian mythology became
popular in the 1880s and 1890s with the establishment of a special profession of
sumitsubo makers. At the same time, the shape of the ink pot became square
with rounded corners. Earlier sumitsubo were shaped like a heart to represent
the leaves of the bodhi tree under which Gautama Buddha is believed to have
attained enlightenment. Thus the traditional design of the sumitsubo is another
important reminder of the religious dimension to the Way of the Carpenter.

Bamboo Drawing Pen (sumisashi)

This tool (fig. 34) is one of the simplest in the carpenter’s collection. It consists of
a piece of bamboo usually 15 to 25 centimeters in length, although sometimes as

44 « THE CARPENTER’S SET OF TOOLS

## S. 68

Fig. 35. Top, three plane sole gauges (awase-jögi); center left and bottom,
miter joint gauges (fomegata-jögi); center right, pillar profile gauge (osa-jögi).

long as 30 centimeters. Depending on the length, its width varies from 1.0 to 1.5
centimeters. One end is pointed, and the other is cut flat at an angle of about
twenty degrees with approximately thirty lateral cuts 2 centimeters down the
grain to form a coarse, brushlike bristle. The tool is used for marking and
labeling timber to be cut by the simple method of dipping it into the ink pot. The
pointed end is used for writing characters and numbers, and the flat, splayed
end is used for drawing lines.

As the ends wear down with use, the bamboo inking pen is repeatedly recut
until only a short stub remains, rather like a pencil. Moreover, in much the same
way as the mechanical pencil with inserted lead has come into widespread use
in recent years, a brass handle holding pieces of cut bamboo has been developed
to replace the more humble inking pen.

Gauges

Measuring Gauges The two main measuring gauges (jögi) (fig. 35) are the
miter-joint gauge (fomegata-jögi) for marking 45-degree angles and the block
gauge (hakogata-jogi) used to draw a line over two intersecting surfaces of a
square pillar. A third gauge, the profile gauge (osa-jögi or osa-maruta-biki), is a

INSTRUMENTS OF THE MIND » 45

## S. 69

Fig. 36. Technique for using the profile gauge Fig. 37. Single line-marking
(osa-jögi) to transfer the irregular contour of gauge (suji-kebiki).
the stone footing to the pillar base.

special measuring tool designed for transferring the irregularly shaped outline
of a stone footing onto the bottom of a pillar (fig. 36). It consists of several
hundred narrow strips of bamboo held securely in place by an iron frame. This
is tapped over the surface of the stone, creating an accurate outline of the profile
which is subsequently transferred to the bottom of the pillar.

Marking Gauges Marking gauges (kebiki) are used to place guidelines along
the surfaces of wood. The marking gauges facilitate fast and accurate
preparation of timbers to suit the most fastidious craftsman. They are especially
important for marking timbers selected for interior fittings such as sills for
sliding screens (shikii) and lintels (kamoi and nageshi). These guidelines must be
laid out with great precision, particularly so that the interior detailing fits
exactly and screens move smoothly.

Marking gauges are made from a rectangular wooden block with a narrrow
outrigger-style arm set through it at right angles. Into this arm is set, in turn, the
marking knife which lightly scores the surface of the wood. The gauge is
adjusted by moving the arm, which is secured by a small wooden wedge
or—with modern gauges—by a screw. The rectangular block itself runs along
the edge of the wood to allow a straight, parallel line to be cut.

There are three principal marking gauges: (1) the Single-line marking gauge

46 » THE CARPENTER’S SET OF TOOLS

## S. 70

Fig. 38. Plumb lines (sagefuri).

(suji-kebiki), the basic marking gauge equipped with a single scoring knife (fig.
37); (2) the double-line marking gauge (nimai-kebiki), which has two knives set
into the arm and is used to lay down the twin parallel lines required for cutting
the grooves for sliding screens; and (3) the splitting gauge (wari-kebiki), which is
used to score thinner pieces of wood deeply along the grain, thus permitting
them to be broken cleanly into two pieces.

Plumb Line (sagefuri)

This tool consists of a heavy, cone-shaped bronze weight attached to a length of
strong line that is wrapped around a wooden handle when not in use (fig. 38). A
variation of the traditional plumb line, and now replaced by the Western spirit
level, it was a simple upright braced by two struts with a weighted string
suspended from the upright.

The Western spirit level is in general use both for determining horizontal and
vertical planes and for checking flatness. The traditional instrument used prior
to the introduction of the spirit level for checking horizontality was the
mizubakari (“water calculator”). It consisted simply of a hollowed-out piece of
timber into which water was ladled, the level of water indicating the horizontal
plane.

INSTRUMENTS OF THE MIND » 47

## S. 73

TOOLS OF THE HAND—
CUTTING AND FINISHING TOOLS

Chisels (nomi)

The chisel family is the largest single group of Japanese tools, reflecting its vital
role in fashioning the intricate joinery holding the Japanese building together.
The chisel has played a much more significant role in the evolution of Japanese
architecture than occidental chisels played in the development of Western
architecture.

The head of the chisel is made of carbon steel. This is forged onto a softer steel
section to give it resilience and to conserve precious steel (fig. 40). The head is
beveled on one side of the tip to form the cutting edge. Chisels with a head
wider than 1.3 centimeters also have bevels down the shoulders of the blade.
These bevels reduce resistance as the chisel cuts through the wood. The back of
the head is also hollow ground or fluted to facilitate cutting. The head of wider
chisels tapers into a funnel-shaped neck or shank, which gives it a distinctive
appearance. This shank culminates in the tang, or spike, which is driven into a
hardwood handle, usually made of oak or boxwood, and then secured by a steel
collar. Thus, the chisel in Japan combines the socket and tang methods of
attaching the metal blade to a wooden handle to give it greater strength than
either the simple socket or tang method alone.

Like all Japanese cutting tools, the nomi is a thoroughbred. It is a precision
instrument perfectly adapted to the tasks it performs but demanding, in turn,
the highest level of skill from its user. The cutting blade is very hard, measurin
as much as sixty-four on the Rockwell C scale of seventy, but as a result it is also
quite brittle. The carpenter must exercise utmost care to prevent nicking the
blade. The finer the steel, the greater the precision demanded of the user. .

The precision of traditional Japanese chisel manufacture and use may seem
excessive to us today. Modern glues have rendered precision unnecessary when
making firm mortises and tenons. In the Japanese building tradition, however, a
tight fit was not merely the ideal; it was the absolute minimum requirement for
everyday practice. The combination of superb steel and Ppainstaking honing at
the workshop and building site resulted in a razor-sharp edge which was no
mere craftsman’s affectation. Such an edge was essential because of the
prevalence in Japanese architecture of soft timbers, which require very sharp
ceutting tools.

Chisels were traditionally made of laminated steel of the unsurpassed
excellence associated with the work of the master swordsmiths in the age ofthe
samurai (pl. 9). A recent comparison of the performance of Japanese and
Western-made chisels has revealed that chisel blades made by Japanese handforging techniques hold their cutting edge up to six times longer than those
mass-produced by the Western drop-forged method. on .

We should not forget, however, that in part this qualitative difference is due to
the greater emphasis on industrialized mass production of tools in the West.
American chisels crafted before the industrial revolution by traditional handforging techniques were of superior quality than most of those manufactured

48 . THE CARPENTER’S SET OF TOOLS

today. Perhaps the superior Japanese chisels oftoday would find more congenial
company with a gouging chisel made in 1775 found by Eric Sloane in an old
Stone fence: Bright and silverish, its edge is keen; it has no rust.... I have
compared the a Chisels (the most expensive, that is) by leaving them in the

that of other chisels, to facilitate heavy-duty cutting. The shank of the chisel is at
least six centimeters in length to enable the cutting of deep mortises. The
requirements of different stages in the mortise-cutting process demand chisel
heads of various widths. Those wider than about three centimeters (1 sun) in
width, commonly called hiro-nomi (“wide chisels”), are used to outline the
mortise with shallow vertical cuts, leaving the sumi markings still visible. The
rough cutting stage is commenced with a medium-sized chisel, usually 2.5
centimeters (8 bu) in width (fig. 44). The hole is excavated by cutting at an angle
of about forty-five degrees from each side alternately (fig. 45) once the medium
and small-headed chisels have roughed out the hole. A wide chisel is then used
to smooth the interior walls. Japanese master carpenters did not use gimlets or
augers for drilling starter holes for mortises, a significant difference from the
practice in the West.

A chisel with a shank of approximately 5.5 centimeters, fractionally shorter
than that used for the mortise-cutting chisel, is used for fashioning tenons. Much
of the work of making larger tenons is performed by using a saw, but more

Butt chisel (öire-nomi) Butt chisels are used for nonstructural work,
particularly for the delicate joinery and bevels on lintels and sills (fig. 47). The
shank of the butt chisel is about 4.5 centimeters long, 2 to 3 centimeters shorter
than framing chisels in order to give more control in detailed work.

The master carpenter usually owns a set of fifteen butt chisels (fig. 48). The

TOOLS OF THE HAND « 53

## S. 79

Fig. 50. Technique for using
long-handled paring chisel.

Fig. 49. Paring chisels (tsuki-nomi).

heads range in width from 3 millimeters (1 rin) to 4.84 centimeters (6 bu). A
medium-sized chisel is 1.515 centimeters (5 bu) in width; this is the half-sun
measure which is a common “rule of thumb” in traditional construction.

Paring Chisels (tsuki-nomi) Paring chisels (fig. 49), also called slicks, are used
to finish work begun with striking chisels, in particular mortise holes and tenon
arms. As their name implies, they are guided by hand pressure, not by striking
with a hammer. The end of the wooden handle is, therefore, gently rounded to
fit comfortably into the palm of the hand and does not require the steel hoop
used to strengthen the handle of the striking chisel. The handles of larger paring
chisels may be as long as forty centimeters to provide for a firm grip and good
control. Unlike the seated posture adopted for using the striking chisels, the
carpenter works with paring chisels standing up, one foot resting on the timber
and the chisel carefully centered beneath the head. Greatest control of the tool is
obtained by using the left hand to stabilize the end of the handle and by bearing
down while the right hand grasps the handle near the shank in an overhand grip
(fig. 50). However, to speed up the operation, it is not unusual for some of the

paring of rough surfaces to be carried out using a striking chisel without a
hammer (fig. 51).

54 © THE CARPENTER’S SET OF TOOLS

## S. 80

Fig. 51. A common shorteut of using
a striking chisel for paring work.

Fig. 52. “Sword-guard” chisels
for deep nail holes.

Special-Purpose Chisels Within the broad family of Japanese wood-working
chisels there are a number of chisels designed for special purposes including

more than eighty different types of gouges used by sculptors. Four types of
special chisels are of particular importance to the carpenter.

“Sword-guard” chisel (tsuba-nomi) (fig. 52) Contrary to popular belief, iron
nails are used in Japanese building construction. Often square in the shaft and
up to twenty to thirty centimeters in length, these nails fasten together many of
the vital parts of a building, such as the raftering and eaves framing (fig. 53). The
sword-guard chisel is designed specifically to cut the deep, square holes needed
for these nails. The blade of the tsuba-nomi is square in section and tapers
toward the head, thus creating a hole into which the square Japanese nail is
rammed.

It is difficult to extract a standard striking chisel after it has been sunk as
deeply as twenty to thirty centimeters into a dense-grained structural timber.
The sword-guard chisel is the answer to this problem. By striking it repeatedly
on alternate sides in the direction of the handle, the chisel is inched out of its
predicament.

## S. 82

“Bottom-cleaning” chisel (sokosarai-nomi) This hook-headed paring chisel
(fig. 42) is used for cleaning waste out of the bottom of a mortise. It is designed
to avoid damaging the brittle cutting edge of striking chisels caused by levering
them back and forth.

“Strike-through” chisel (uchidashi-nomi) This striking chisel (fig. 42) has a
head with no cutting bevel. It is designed simply to break through the wood
when cutting a pass-through joint.

“Trowel” chisel (kote-nomi) This paring chisel (fig. 54) has a stepped or
chanked shank resembling a trowel handle or a dogleg. It permits the cutting of
trench-shaped, sliding dovetails recessed into the bottom of mortises,
particularly important for fitting structural braces.

Saws (nokogiri)

The next major family of Japanese tools is the saw. Like the members of any
family, Japanese nokogiri share common attributes but are also intriguingly
different from each other. In fact, the saw has evolved from an inefficient, thickbladed implement, more effective for its grinding than its cutting action, into a
diverse and highly specialized family of precision instruments with varied blade
shapes and teeth configurations used for different types of woods and cutting
actions. The physical appearance of the Japanese saw and the method of its
manipulation are now very different from those of its distant relatives, the
European and American sawS. The most radical point of divergence is that it
cuts on the pull not the push stroke. .

Japanese saws generally consist of a wooden handle and a metal blade with
teeth down either one or both edges. The single-edged blade is commonly
known as kataba (literally, “teeth on one side”) (fig. 55), while the double-edged
saw is called a ryöba (“teeth on both sides”) (fig. 56). The handle is straight or
slightly angled and is made of cypress or cedar bound with bamboo cane. It thus
may be used with either a single-handed or double-handed grip, unlike the
modern Western counterpart, which is limited by its enclosed handle to a single
grip. The blade is made of laminated steel, usually four sheets forged together
and carefully tempered and tensioned for flexibility. Better-quality saws are
manufactured from high-grade steel. A few saws are still made from superlative
“sword” steel using traditional hand-forging techniques. The best quality handforged saw with a 2 shaku blade (approximately sixty centimeters) reputedly cuts
through three hundred logs before resharpening is necessary. .

The family of Japanese saws is divided into two main branches: ripsaws
(tatebiki-noko) and crosscut sawS (yokobiki-noko). Some roughing saws with coarse
teeth straddle both groups, being used for both crosscutting and ripping. The
double-edged ryöba, for instance, has ripping teeth down one side of the blade

and crosscutting teeth down the other.
The fundamental difference between cutting along and cutting across the

grain of a piece of wood makes for important differences in physical form and
method of use between crosscut sawS and ripsaws. Since ripsaws cut with the

Ripsaw Teeth Crosscut Teeth | |
|

Fig. 57. Teeth configuration for crosscut saws and ripsaws.

grain of the wood, they need large, sharply raked teeth (fig. 57) which look
rather like thorns, hence their name “thorn teeth” (ibara-me). The danger of teeth
breaking increases toward the end of the blade. To counteract this, the angle of
the teeth reduces toward the tip. Teeth nearest the handle are set at an angle to
the blade of approximately ninety degrees for harder woods and seventy-five
degrees for softer woods, but saws used on harder woods have an angle of only |
forty-five degrees at the toe. The individual teeth of a ripsaw work in a manner |
similar to the blade of a plane, cutting readily with the grain of the wood at a |
shallow angle. Unlike the teeth of crosscut saws, they do not require a beveled ||
cutting edge because they work with, rather than against, the direction of the
wood fiber. Those teeth nearest the handle, however, are much larger than those
close to the tip of the blade and are used at the beginning of each pull stroke to
start the cut.
If the teeth of the ripsaw may be said to work like a plane, those of the
crosscut saw may be said to work more like a chisel. Its teeth have to chew
laboriously through the fiber of the wood at a right angle, unlike the ripsaw |
teeth, which run freely down the grain. To facilitate this process, it is equipped
with smaller, flat-topped teeth beveled on the top as well as the leading and
trailing edges for better cutting. In order to make it easier to cut through the
wood, the leading edge of each tooth stands practically vertical, compared with
the gently raked teeth of the ripsaw.
Japanese saws are thus equipped with highly specialized teeth designed to
cope efficiently with different types of cutting actions as well as wood hardness.
In fact, the general philosophy for using a saw is to “use the teeth, not your
strength.” The carpenter’s role is to guide these teeth to their requisite position,
where they duly carry out their predetermined task, not to exhibit his physical

60 » THE CARPENTE

## S. 86

point of the blade, and the saw is held at an angle of approximately forty-five
degrees to the wood. He uses the left hand to guide the blade while the right
pulls the blade in short, sharp cutting strokes. When the cutting slot is long
enough to stabilize the blade, the left hand holds the base of the handle loosely,
and the saw is moved horizontally through most of the length, as gentle power
is applied for the pull stroke (fig. 58). The ripsaw, by contrast, is held at a steeper
angle of about thirty degrees with feet straddling the wood or right foot resting
on it, the left foot pressing against it from the side (fig. 59). The hands are held
further apart than for the crosscut saw in order to permit greater control over the
blade as it moves rapidly down the grain.

One further general point of fascination to observer and specialist alike needs
to be made. Although the same fundamental distinction between ripping and
crosscutting is observed in Japanese as in Western saws, the Japanese have
chosen a basically different approach to the actual task of sawing timbers. The
teeth of the Japanese saw face back toward the handle, indicating that it cuts on
the pull stroke. Western saws cut on the push stroke, except for bow and frame
saws which can be used on either the push or the pull stroke. Even Chinese and
Korean saws, from which the Japanese saw was originally derived, cut on the
push stroke, a fact that singles out the Japanese saw as exceptional among those
of every known building tradition.

There are general advantages in pulling rather than pushing a saw. Pulling
puts the blade of a saw under tension and makes it stronger; pushing a saw
places the blade under compression and gives rise to a tendency to buckle and
bind when passing through the wood. It is interesting to note that the old
Western bow-saw used a blade kept under tension by a tightened cord and
could be used with either a push or a pull stroke, depending on the personal
preference of the user. Although it has been likened to sawing with a picture
frame, the superior cutting performance of its narrower blade still make it the
preferred saw for some dedicated craftsmen. A further reason that the Japanese
may have adopted the pulling rather than pushing stroke for sawing is that
craftsmen tended to work sitting down. Ripsawyers, for example, did much of
the arduous lengthways cutting of timbers into long planks sitting down on the
job, as may be seen ina photograph taken in 1871 (fig. 60). Here they sit beneath
the timber, which is propped up atan angle of about forty-five degrees. To the
Western mind, accustomed to the image of a sawyer standing erect over the
trunk of a tree, this position seems to offer nothing but aching arms and a
cramped back. However, the pull-stroke saw may be operated efficiently and
smoothly in a sitting position beneath the timber. Sitting gives a stable fulcrum
for the cutting action, ensuring great accuracy down the full length of a beam,
while from his position beneath the beam the sawyer gains maximum assistance
from the force of gravity in his downward pull-cutting stroke. Many Japanese
woodworkers, especially the carpenters who specialized in making furniture,
shoji frames and sliding-door frames, generally work seated to free their legs
and feet to hold and support the timber being cut.

The special character of the Western saw derives from the logic of the pushcutting stroke, in the same way that the character of the Japanese saw is derived
from its pulling action. The blade of the Western saw is thicker than that of the

62 » THE CARPENTER’S SET OF TOOLS

## S. 88

Fig. 61. Rounded-nose roughing saw (hanamaru-noko).

of the two different cutting actions. Western saws, on the one hand, are made
from softer, more flexible steel to allow for buckling under the stress of a push
stroke. Because the steel is softer, the teeth themselves cannot take as sharp an
edge as the harder steel of the Japanese saw. The drawback, as it were, of the
harder-steel Japanese saw is that it is more brittle and the teeth more liable to
snap off when it is used or sharpened inexpertly, or if a crosscut blade strikes a
strong knot. However, the saws seem to be very forgiving, as they work just as
well with several teeth missing. The major types of saws are set out below.

Rounded-Nose Roughing Saw (hanamaru-noko or anahiki) This general
roughing saw (fig. 61) takes its name from the characteristic droop at the top of
the blade. The cutting edge is “breasted,” that is, it curves slightly outwards to
enhance the cutting action by following the natural curve of the carpenter’s arm

as it swings in the shoulder socket. High-quality Western saws are also breasted
for the same reason. The hanamaru-noko in most frequent use has a blade
forty-five centimeters long and 10.5 centimeters wide (1 shaku, 5
sun by 3 sun, 5 bu in traditional measurements) and can cut wood ranging from
nine to thirty centimeters in thickness. The most common type, the only
Japanese saw designed both for crosscutting and ripping, has large teeth, which

approximately

in design strike a balance between the two teeth types. These teeth are pointed,
as in ripsaw teeth, but the leading edges are almost vertical like those of crosscut
saws. They increase slightly in size toward the tip of the blade, a modification of
the typical ripsaw, but are beveled on the leading edge of each tooth, a
modification of crosscut teeth. Some rounded-nose roughing saws have the
thorn-ripping teeth only, but these are beveled on the leading edge to permit
crosscutting. However, like all compromises, this saw is a less effective
instrument in most respects than that intended solely for ripping or crosscutting.

Standard-Purpose Carpentry Ripsaw (maebiki-noko) The maebiki-noko (fig.
62) is used in the general course of construction for working small- and
medium-sized squared timbers ranging from three centimeters to twenty
centimeters in thickness. The blade measures approximately thirty-six to thirtynine centimeters in length (1 shaku, 2 sun to 1 shaku, 3 sun) and is about twelve

prefabricated. The back of the blade is slightly convex, unlike that of the

Double-edged Utility Saw (ryöba) The wedge-shaped utility saw, or ryöba (fig.
56), has cross-cutting teeth down one edge and ripping teeth down the other. Its

distinctive shape arises from practical dictates, not aesthetic caprice. The blade
widens toward the top to stabilize the large teeth at the end in the cutting slot.
The smaller teeth at the narrow part of the blade nearest the handle are used to
start the cutting action and do not, as a result, need a wide blade for stability.

The convenience of the ryöba makes it one of the most common of the saws in
use today. A daiku customarily has ryöba of three different sizes. The longest of
these measures approximately thirty centimeters (1 shaku) and is 11.5 centimeters
wide with about four teeth per centimeter (twelve to thirteen teeth per sun). The
long cutting edge of twenty-seven centimeters is most effective when used for
cutting timber from three to twelve centimeters in width. The medium-length
ryöba, twenty centimeters (9 sun) long and ten centimeters wide, has five to six
teeth per centimeter (six teeth per sun); the smallest saw is about twenty-four
centimeters (8 sun) long and nine centimeters wide with six to seven teeth per
centimeter (twenty teeth per sun). The largest saw is used primarily for
preparation of structural timbers, and the medium and small saws are used
more for delicate finishing work once the structure has been assembled.

Most ryöba blades are slightly concave in section to reduce friction during
cutting. Greater thickness at the edges also makes it easier to set the teeth. A few
ryöba are actually convex in section. Although harder to use and set, they have a
longer working life.

TOOLS OFTHE HAND « 67

## S. 93

As with all Japanese cutting and finishing tools, the heart of the plane is its
cutting iron, consisting of a steel tip forged onto a wrought-iron blank, and
honed to razor sharpness (fig. 68). Carpenters frequently made their own
special-purpose planes for molding and beveling without the assistance of a
blacksmith. In recent years, broken hacksaw blades of Western origin have also
found a new lease on life in this role.

A special thin-bladed crosscut saw, the’hoso-noko, is used to cut the slots at the
sides of the mouth of the block that house the cutting blade. This saw is less than
thirty-six millimeters in thickness because of the confined space in which it has
to work. The teeth are small, with a minimal set designed for cutting the harder
wood of the block.

As in the case of the saw, the carpenter works the plane with a pulling action,
giving a greater measure of control over his tool than is afforded by the pushing
action used in the west and in China and Korea. In traditional practice, the plane
was used from either a seated position or standing with the wood set at an angle
on a planing board. Carpenters favored the seated position for more detailed
work with planes as well as saws. Altogether, it is considered easier to work
with a pulling action when seated, a fact which may account for the Japanese
plane being used in this distinctive way (pl. 6). Propping up timbers on a
planing board supported by a triangular horse made it easier to plane with a
smooth, even action, since the force of gravity helped the plane block slide down
the timber. However, the angle of the wood was of critical importance: ten to

teen degrees to the horizontal was considered ideal.

Skillful use of planes is as much a matter of the accurate setting of the blade
and selecting the right plane for the task at hand as it is skill in working the tool
over the surface of the wood. Japanese planes, like saws, are sensitive
Instruments which rely upon precision rather than strength to achieve their
ends. At the Outset, the cutting blade and capping iron must be properly
Positioned for the particular type of wood and desired cutting strength. Setting
the blade is a matter of patient coaxing with a hammer. The alignment of the
blade is achieved by sighting along the sole of the plane and tapping the end of
the block gently until the required amount of cutting edge protrudes beyond the
plane’s mouth (fig. 69 and pl. 6). Judging alignment is, of course, a matter of
experience. The blade is then locked into position by tapping the capping iron
into place.

Similarly the selection of the right plane for the right job entails much
experience, especially when confronted by the almost bewildering variety of
members of the plane family. The 1943-44 Institute for Labor Science survey list
of forty different types of planes as the absolute minimum requirement for a

ly equipped master carpenter may seem a surprising number to us today, but
we should remember that a similar wide variety of planes was in common use
by carpenters, Coopers, cabinet and coach makers in Europe and America in the
nineteenth century. As Eric Sloane comments on a tool advertisement of the
early 18005, “the army of old-time wooden planes seems overwhelming.”*

Since the army of Japanese planes is likewise overwhelming (fig. 70), greater
Case in understanding may be achieved by dividing them into two broad
divisions: smoothing planes for preparing timber surfaces and special purpose

## S. 96

Finishing planes (chüshiko-ganna and nagadai-ganna) The timber is now ready
for creation of a finely finished surface with the intermediate smoothing plane
(chüshiko-ganna) and final smoothing or “jack” plane (nagadai-ganna) (fig. 74).
These planes have more sharply tapered cutting blade edges than do the
roughing planes, and their soles are designed to allow four points of contact
with the wood surface. These differences make provision for more precise
Cutting and continuous operating stokes down the entire length of the timber.
The nagadai-ganna has the longest body of any smoothing plane. A typical
length is 45.45 centimeters (1 shaku, 5 sun), and some planes can be as long sixty
centimeters (2 shaku). The block of this plane may also be up to 5.43 centimeters
(1 sun, 8 bu) wide. The long, wide block ensures an even final surface on long
Squared members such as pillars, beams and floorboards.

Special finishing planes (shiage-ganna and jöshiage-ganna) The finish on
details such as sills and lintels plays an important role in the overall aesthetic
effect of a Japanese building. (figs. 75 and 76). Two special finishing planes, the
Shiage-ganna and jöshiage-ganna, are used to put the final surface on these
timbers by bringing out the patterns of the grain. The bodies of the planes are
wide (as much as 6.06 centimeters, or 2 sun); their blades are not tapered for
deep cutting because they do not penetrate the wood surface but rather lightly
skim it. The shavings may be as thin as 0.03 millimeters with the lovely
translucence of fine shoji paper.

Anyone accustomed to working with wood will readily appreciate that the
Process of smoothing timber to an even surface is critical both to the final
appearance and to the success of much of the joinery in Japanese architecture.
Although the flatness and evenness of surface are easily judged by the
experienced eye, the carpenter also relies on a special measuring gauge, the
@wase-jögi, to ensure that the timber is planed flat. These simple wooden straightedged gauges are also used to verify the configuration of the sole of a plane (fig.
77).

Special-Purpose Planes (fig. 79) The number and variety of special-purpose
planes indicates the highly specialized and personalized nature of Japanese
Carpentry tools. Many of the planes in this category are made by the carpenter
himself for fulfilling a special purpose and creating a special effect. An entire
book could be filled with descriptions of these gentle and clever tools, but this
discussion must be limited to those used most commonly in traditional building

Practice.

Corner-cutting planes (kiwa-ganna) These planes seem like any other small
smoothing planes except that the blades have a raked eutting edge specially
designed for creating the sharp corner intersections in joinery (fig. 79). Kiwaganna are divided into those raked to cut left-side corners and those raked for
right-hand side operation. However, carpenters have been known simply to
reverse a plane and use a pull action to cut the opposite corner.

Grooving planes (anazuki-ganna) Anazuki-ganna are used to make the groove

## S. 100

plane, the sokotori-jakuri-ganna (fig. 82 and pl. 8). This plane has a very tall and
narrow body designed to fit snugly into the 5-bu groove to stabilize the plane
against sideways wobble. These planes expel shavings from the side because
they are too high for a standard top aperture to do the job efficiently.

Chamfer and molding planes (kakumentori-ganna and soridai-ganna) The
beveled corners of square pillars and lintels, which are a subtle but significant
feature of Japanese architecture, require their own special beveling or chamfer
planes, some of which are very intricate in design. Bevels or chamfers are more
than just an aesthetic feature of Japanese architecture. They are used to eliminate
many of the sharp intersecting wooden surfaces and so make the buildings safer
for their users and appealing to the eye. The width of the bevel is determined as
part of the proportional order of a building. The bevels of pillars of large-scale
gateways and residences may be one-tenth the width of the pillar. Those on
pillars of smaller buildings and residences may be as narrow as one-fifth of the
pillar’s width.

There are many different types of chamfer planes, or kakumentori-ganna (fig.
83 and pl. 7). Each is designed to cut a bevel of a certain width and angle. The
sole of the block is cut into the shape necessary to guide the blade into the
required path. Simpler planes have a sole cut at right angles so that the plane fits
snugly to the corner of the pillar to ensure a perfect finish. More complicated
planes may have several fluted grooving tracks, and the blade is serrated to cut
multiple grooves. Some planes have twin blades arranged like pincers, designed
to cut the sliding dovetails indispensable for joining flat pieces of wood.

Another important type of plane are the small soridai-ganna. These come in
many variations and are used to make many of the curved edges and rounded
surfaces that complement the right-angled post-and-lintel system of the building
frame, particularly the curved window frames and ceiling support struts. A
molding plane has a rounded sole that cuts into the surface of the wood to shape
it into elegantly curved window frames, struts and beams (fig. 84). The contour
of the sole itself has a prescriptive effect on the character of the curve.

Most molding planes cut with the grain down the length of the piece of wood,
but the convex-grooving plane (sotomaru-ganna) and the concave-grooving plane
(uchimaru-ganna) (fig. 85), known in the West as “rounds” and “hollows,” cut
across the grain to make rounded tracks in the surface of the wood.

The carpenter makes many of his own chamfer and molding planes. Often
they are only finger-size and comprise a single cutting blade without capping
iron. They are one of the most subtle expressions ofthe personal nature of the art
and craft of the Japanese master carpenter. Looking at the variety of these planes
makes it possible to appreciate the intimate link between human creativity and

the means of expressing it in built forms.

Gimlets (kiri)

TOOLS OF THE HAND » 77

## S. 101

„nnecessary for structural work. Mortises were completely cut by chisel, not
begun with “starter holes” as in Western practice. The holes required for sinking
larger nails had to be Square because the nails themselves were square in section
to prevent them from rotating or slipping under stress. These holes were cut bya
Sword-guard chisel or with a forked-head square gimlet. This gimlet is known as
the uchikomi-giri (“striking gimlet”). Since it is square in shape and struck with a
hammer, not rotated by hand, it could be categorized as a chisel rather than as a
gimlet.

Tools for ceutting round holes were imported from the West in the later

neteenth century. The Labor Science Survey reveals that by the 1940s the largehandled gimlet and the standard Western brace and bit had become a regular
Part of the equipment used for building carpentry.

Hammers

Hammers (fig. 87) vary greatly in size, shape and materials precisely anog
to function. They differ from their Western counterparts in one irn Ah
aspect: they have no claws for extracting nails. This task is performed by

78 * THE CARPENTER’S SET OF TOOLS

## S. 102

Fig. 87. The hammer family: left to right, two wooden mallets (kizuchi); steel doublefaced hammers for use with chisel (gennö); carpenter’s hammers (kanazuchi).

nail-extractors (fig. 89). The three main types of Japanese hammers are wooden
mallets, metal double-faced hammers and the carpenter’s hammer.

Wooden Mallet (kizuchi) These are similar to European mallets but range in
size from small-scale tapping instruments to large mallets with handles over a
meter long (fig. 88). Their primary function is to help assemble the frame
elements by exerting sufficient force to drive recalcitrant members together
without splitting the joints or bruising the wood. The large mallet, more like a
wooden sledgehammer, is used for setting the ridgebeam, main pillars and
crossbeams.

Steel Double-Faced Hammer (gennö) The gennö is used primarily to strike
chisels in the process of creating mortises, although the smallest gennö is
sometimes used to drive nails. It has a wooden handle of oak or holly, either
straight or slighly curved, which is approximately twenty centimeters long. The
iron head is cylindrical with flat striking faces of steel. The head is heavy relative
to the area of its striking face. The head of the standard-sized gennö weighs 300
grams and the striking surface is twenty-three millimeters by twenty-six

## S. 104

millimeters, a fact that permits chisels to be driven into the wood with
considerable force.

Carpenter’s Hammer (kanazuchi) The kanazuchi has a metal head with two
operating surfaces, one flat and the other tapered sharply like the Western
cobbling hammer. This hammer is used for the sole purpose of driving nails, a
fact that flies in the face of the commonly held belief in the West that no nails are
used in traditional Japanese building practice. As noted before, nails are not
used for structural joinery but they do perform a number of functions in the
typical building, including securing roof battens and eave end-tiles.

In the nailing process, after the hole for the nail is chiseled, the flat hammer
head is used to drive the nail about three-quarters of its length into the wood.
The head is then reversed and the operation completed by using the tapered
end. In this way, the narrow head of the hammer strikes only the nail and does
not bruise the surrounding wood. Thus, the head combines the roles of hammer

and nail punch.

Nail Extractors (kugi-nuki)

This tool is like a pair of long-handled pincers and, as the name implies, is used
for removing nails (fig. 89). It is first depicted in a twelfth-century painting
showing scenes of hell, in which miscreants pay for their evil doings by having
teeth and tongues efficiently removed by this tool.

aintenance tools are essential for the proper
functioning of cutting and finishing tools. The most important are the
whetstones, used for sharpening the blades of chisels and planes, and the files

and rasps needed for sharpening the teeth of saws.

Whetstones (toishi)

The razor-sharp edges needed for Japanese tools are created by painstaking and
frequent honing. A carpenter may spend many hours each day sharpening his
blades. This is essential to realize the full potential of their superlative steel. It is
commonly said that four hours of sharpening are necessary to allow six hours of
cutting on a typical working day. The patience with which the carpenter applies
himself to this seemingly tedious task is, in reality, a form of self-discipline and
meditation as he prepares himself as well as his tools for the task ahead.

The whetstones (figs. 90 and 91) are as specialized as the tools they serve. The
1943-44 survey listed a minimum of eight whetstones as essential for carpenters
work. In fact, fifteen to twenty is a more common number to be found at a
building site. The stones are usually kept immersed in water ready for use. They

SERVICE TOOLS « 81

## S. 105

(toishi) left to right: from

82 « THE CARPENTER’S SET OF TOOLS

## S. 106

are mounted on a wooden base to steady them and are used lengthways for
maximum honing effect.

Whetstones range from very coarse (800 grit) to extraordinarily fine (6,000
grit). The coarse stones (arato) are used to hone the beveled surface of cutting
blades. Medium stones (nakato) are the biggest group of whetstones and serve to
transform the blades of a tool into fine cutting edges. Finishing stones (awasedo)
hone the blade to perfection and remove the burr from the back of the blade.

Files and Rasps

The effectiveness of the cutting action of the saw depends upon the care with
which its teeth are sharpened. A flat rasp is used to ensure that all the teeth are
properly aligned. The teeth are then set to left and right with quick, deft taps of a
sharp-nosed hammer. Files which are diamond shaped in section are then used
to sharpen each tooth. The teeth of the ripsaw are filed at an angle of ninety
degrees to the blade, but those of crosscut saws are filed at an angle of only
forty-five degrees (fig. 92). The file dances in a carefully rehearsed sequence
from leading edge to trailing edge and back to the leading edge again before
filing of the top bevel completes the routine.

During filing, the saw is gripped firmly in a portable wooden vice consisting
of two thin boards. These hold the blade but leave the teeth exposed. Today it is
more economical and efficient to return high-quality saws to the maker for
resharpening. It is, moreover, cheaper to buy a new saw than to resharpen a
poor-quality saw.

The file is such an unpretentious tool that it is easy to overlook its importance
to the carpenter. It is instructive to see this tool through the eyes of Fukuzawa
Yukichi (1835-1901), newspaper magnate and founder of Keio University. One of
the most vivid experiences of his youth in the 1850s that he recounts in his
autobiography is a description of a file maker at work:

I knew how to make an ordinary file from a steel bar, after a fashion,
but the fine file for sharpening saws was beyond my art... [W]hen I
first came to Yedo (later Tokyo), I was surprised at the sight of a boy, an
apprentice to a blacksmith, making a sawfile. .. .The boy had the file on
a piece of leather on an anvil, and was chiseling away at very fine
notches as if he never realized there was any wonder in it. I stopped
and watched him, thinking what a great city of industry this must be
where even a youngster could make a saw-file such as myself had
never dreamed of making.

TOOLS CLOSE TO EXTINCTION

There are four important tools which have virtually disappeared from the
building sites of modern Japan. They are discussed in greater detail in chapter 4
but take their rightful place at this point to complete an examination of the set of
tools customarily used in traditional building practice.

TOOLS CLOSE TO EXTINCTION + 83

Fig. 93. Ninteenth-century adze

Fig. 95. A ship Carpenter (funa-daiku) at

## S. 108

Adze (chöna)

The adze (fig. 93) consists of a curved wooden handle fitted with a heavy,
steel-tipped blade usually about ten centimeters wide. The handle was forced
into its distinctive curve by repeated heating and gentle pressure to give it the
most effective shape for an efficient chopping action. The handle was only about
fifty to sixty centimeters in length, making the adze suitable for use seated as
well as standing.

The adze was used in premodern building practice for rough finishing the
surfaces of pillars and beams and for making hewn planks even. It was
particularly useful for fashioning the smooth, rounded surfaces needed for
temple and palace pillars. It also performed the essential preparatory work for
the fine, silklike surface created by the planing knife or the block plane, although
it is common to find the distinctive, dimpled finish of its blade as an attractive
feature of castle towers and gatehouses, where smooth surfaces were not
essential. The adze takes much skill and patience to use properly and safely. The
rough surface of the wood is slowly whittled away with a carefully directed
chopping action akin to the pecking motions of a farmyard hen. The wood is
smoothed first in one direction and then from the opposite direction (fig. 94).
Edward Morse, when he saw Japanese carpenters using adzes in 1877, was
amazed at their skill but appalled at the risk:

It is alarming to see a man hewing a beam standing on it with his
naked feet and bringing down his razorlike adze in vigorous strokes
within half an inch of his naked toes. Apparently they rarely cut
themselves, as I have examined many for the marks of scars, or the gap
for missing toes, and in only one case have I found a scar, and when I
called the carpenter’s attention to it he smiled and showed me another
big one on his leg, pointing to his adze and smiling again.

The adze is one of the most venerable of all Japanese carpentry tools, its use
dating back to ancient times. It is included in the 1943-44 Labor Science list and
could still be seen in general use at building sites and in the wooden ship
construction yards of rural Japan, such as at Imaihama in the Izu Peninsula, into
the 1950s (fig. 95). The universal adoption of power tools since the end of the
19505 guaranteed its relegation to the museum shelf. The only craftsmen who
still use it as a matter of course are the makers of the koto, which is made of

paulownia wood hollowed out with a round-headed adze.

Hewing Axe (masakari)

The wide-headed hewing axe (fig. 96) was used for smoothing the sides of
pillars and beams. Rough hewing was performed with a long, pendulum-like
swinging motion, which emitted sounds like the rhythmic ticking ofa
grandfather clock (fig. 97). Finer finishing was carried out with a two-handed
grip on the handle and a carefully directed, gentle chopping action. The adze
could also be used as a hewing axe by the simple ploy of removing the head

TOOLS CLOSE TO EXTINCTION ° 85

Fig. 96. Hewing axes (masakari).

from its customary position at the tip and replacing it sideways at a ninetydegree angle to the handle.

Planing Knife (yari-ganna)

Until the late sixteenth century the yari-ganna was still used in conjunction with
the adze to smooth the surface of timbers. The adze was the roughing tool and
the yari-ganna, with its long wooden handle and razor-sharp, spear-like blade
sharpened on either one or both sides, smoothed surfaces to a silken sheen (fig.
98 and pl. 2).

The yari-ganna was operated normally with long, even strokes, and the
cutting edge was angled at approximately thirty degrees to the direction of the
grain. The blade was set at an angle of approximately forty-five degrees to the
axis of the handle to make it easier to hold, and the cutting edge sometimes had
two sections. The main part, from the center toward the rear of the blade, was
used for rough finishing; the point area was reserved for fine finishing (figs. 98
and 99). The yari-ganna was used mainly with a pulling action to ensure better
control. It demanded the sharpest steel for its cutting edges and the ultimate
level of skill from its user.

By the time of the Institute of Labor Science survey in 1943-44, the yari-ganna
was no longer part of the carpenter’s set of tools. Its use has been revived
recently, however, by master carpenters, working at the construction of new

## S. 112

CHAPTER FOUR

The Invention and Evolution of Tools

This is the ”how” and “when” chapter. It looks at how tools evolved in form and
when they appeared as an indispensable part of the carpenter’s tool set. It also
addresses the issue of the way innovations in tool technology and form
influenced the design of buildings as well as affected the training and
organization of the carpentry profession. These are crucial questions for
understanding the evolutionary dynamics of the long architectural tradition of
Japan, for the on-site performance of tools influenced the nature of building
form as much as any conscious stylistic decisions by the carpenters or the
inherent qualities of the wood used. The nature of certain tools also dictated the

organization of building activities.
It is important to ask “when” b

familiar to us today represent only the lat r
development. The tools which were used to make the temples of ancient Nara

were not the same as those used to build the feudal palaces of Kyoto. It would
be a serious mistake to imagine that the carpenters who shaped the venerable
hinoki pillars of the temple of Höryü-ji were equipped with today’s doubleedged saw and versatile block plane. Those seventh-century carpenters would
have been puzzled if suddenly presented with modern carpenters’ tools because
of the great changes that had occurred in the intervening centuries. Indeed,
Höryü-ji would have looked very different had modern tools been used in its

construction.
It is essential, therefore, to trace t

he various stages in the evolution of

Japanese tools down through the major historical epochs to the present in order
to understand Japanese architecture in the context of the ways and means by
which it was produced. To do this it is important to consider tools and
architecture in relation to broad changes in society, patterns of religious and
political belief, and influences from abroad. The relationship between different
tool types needs careful clarification, for improvements in the design of one tool
frequently led to modification of others. This approach represents a break with
existing studies of tools written in Japanese. These are based on an analysis of

EARLY STONE IMPLEMENTS

Japan during the pre-Jomon period had its share of stone chopping, scraping
and cutting implements dating back to at least 27,000 B.c. From the distribution
of these artifacis, it 15 assumed that man-made Shelter was built by Paleolithic
man, but firm evidence of building Construction dates to the later millennia of
the subsequent Jomon period (ca. 10,000-300

1 } B.C.). This long period witnessed
the evolution of a relatively sedentary pattern of life based ei Ehnting and food
gathering and a sophisticated Ceramic culture. Traces of pit houses dating from

ca. 3500 B.c. have been excavated from the large mounds of discarded shells
which accumulated at settlements astline. Clusters of pit houses

92 © THE INVENTION AND EVOLUTION OF TOOLS

## S. 116

Fig. 101. Polished stone axe or adze Fig. 102. A stone-headed axe and adze from
heads with breasted cutting edges. the Jomon and early Yayoi periods.

heads of the axes and adzes were lashed to wooden handles (fig. 102). The adze
handle was fashioned from a forked branch with one arm shortened for the head
and the other serving as the handle. These chopping tools were not specially
designed for building work and would have been turned to the tasks of hunting
and food preparation as readily as the felling and of hewing timbers.

Jömon man became highly skilled with his stone-headed axes and adzes,
vigorously attacking the huge cedars and other trees found in abundance in the
ancient forests. Many of the extant axe and adze heads are large, measuring as
much as thirty centimeters in length, seven to ten centimeters wide at the cutting
edge and five centimeters thick. They were also designed for maximum
efficiency, with breasted cutting edges to enhance the cutting action, and their
mass would have given them considerable striking force. Structures of some size
were able to be built with these simple tools. Over eighty large pillar stumps
dating to 2500-2000 B.C, and still bearing the marks of strenuous efforts with
stone axes, have been excavated from the Chikamori site in Shiga Prefecture
while others have been found at the sixth century B.C Mawaki site in Fukui
Prefecture. At Fudödö, in Toyama Prefecture on the Sea of Japan, the earth floor
and pillar marks of a large rectangular building have been discovered. It
measures seventeen meters long and eight meters wide and has sixteen large
pillar holes. Four stone-lined hearths are aligned along the central axis of the
building. It must have served an important ritual role in the community, judging
by the central location.

Stone continued to be used for tools until about the third century A.D., when
it was eclipsed by the arrival of bronze and iron from the Asian continent. It is
interesting to note that some stone axe and adze heads were imported even
before the arrival of the first metal implements from the continent. Large stones

EARLY STONE IMPLEMENTS « 93

## S. 117

ground into smooth planes with cutting edges sharper than those previously
existing in Japan and dating to 100 B.c.-A.n. 100 have been excavated from the
Ikegami site in Osaka Prefecture. These appear to have been created on the

Asian continent with the assistance of iron tools and imported to Japan where
their handles were made locally.

much of later Japanese civilization in social, political, religious and cultural
terms. From the third century B.C. there were major movements of population
into the Japanese archipelago from the Asian mainland. These immigrants

echniques of wet rice cultivation and established stable,
agricultural communities with increasingly Specialized use of iron tools. Bronze
and iron arrived almost simultaneously in the Japanese archipelago. The softer
bronze was reserved for ritual implements like mirrors, ceremonial swords and
bells (dötaku), while iron was used for more practical agricultural and hard-

The Yayoi settlements of Toro and Yamaki in Shizuoka Prefecture, which
thrived in the fourth and fifth Centuries, are two of the Most important sources
of information available about Yayoi period building techniques and tools. In a
society based on wet rice eultivation, there was a close relationship between
implements needed for agriculture and tools used for building purposes.
Extensive systems of embankments and water channels were created for the
inundation of the rice fields, requiring axes, adzes, knives, chisels and gouges to

make wooden supports for retaining walls. These same tools fashioned wooden
farming implements such as Spades, hoes and takes, as well as dug-out canoes
for crossing streams and rivers.

94 » THE INVENTION AND EVOLUTION OF TOOLS

## S. 118

Fig. 104. Structure of pit dwelling.
EVOLUTION AND NEW VERNACULAR BUILDINGS ® 95

THE METAL R

## S. 119

construction of interior stone hearths became common practice in ordinary
dwellings. The durability of the joined structure also transformed construction
work from a recurring seasonal or annual activity into a more occasional and
professional project. Rethatching with fresh straw or river reeds was still
necessary every few seasons, but the frame of a dwelling would last thirty years,
an entire Yayoi lifetime.

Although advances in pit dwelling construction were important, the most
significant architectural development of the Yayoi period was the advent of a
completely new building type, the raised-floor structure (figs. 105 and 106). At
the outset it was used for storing the harvested rice safely away from the damp
earth of the low-Iying areas where rice cultivation of necessity took place, but it
later evolved into the residential style for members of the ruling elite and the
form for Shinto architecture.

The appearance of these granaries and residences may be reconstructed from
archaeological evidence and drawings of buildings incised on a bronze mirror
excavated at Takarazuka, near Osaka. The typical storehouse had six posts from
three to four meters in length inserted into the ground to the depth of at least
fifty centimeters. The walls were made from tree trunks split into planks and
held together with log-cabin style interlaced corners (fig. 105). The entrance was
customarily set into the side of the building to allow for easy access in storing or
removing the grain. The roof was thatched, and its supporting frame was made
from light timbers lashed together to create a triangular truss, a distinctive
feature being the way in which the rafters projected beyond the ridgepole to
which they were lashed. Although the roof differed little in structure and
appearance from that of the Jamon pit dwellings, the frame beneath is clearly the
product of efficient iron cutting tools. The posts are longer and straighter than
those in use before the introduction of iron tools and much narrower than the
great, roughly hewn tree trunks found in the larger scale Jömon structures. The
hewing of the wall planks required axes to split the trees as well as axes and
adzes capable of hewing them to an acceptably smooth finish. The interlocking
corners and lap joints for the floor joists demanded tools with sharper cutting
edges than those available to Jomon man. Ladders were carved from single
Pieces of wood and had concave risers hollowed with adzes to prevent rodents
running up them. Ladder tops were guarded by baffle boards to turn back
rodents that had bypassed the curved risers. These oval-shaped guards required
skilled carving to create the necessary center aperture.

The raised floor structures used for residential Purposes were similar in most
respects to the granaries. They were, however, larger in scale and their doorways
were placed in the narrow gable end wall of the building in order to minimize
access and maximize protection and privacy.

The raised floor structure was not a Japanese invention. Although recent
archaeological discoveries point to the possibility of some form of raised floor
structures existing in the Jomon period, the typical elevated structure of Yayoi
times was introduced to Japan from China along with the techniques of rice
eultivation. This may be verified by the fact that among the ritual objects found
in the excavations of the Shizhaishan tomb, in Yunnan Province in China, built
at the end of the second century B.C., there is a cast bronze model of a building

96 - THE INVENTION AND EVOLUTION OF TOOLS

Fig. 105. “Log cabin” (azekura) wall construction. Tödai-ji, Nara Prefecture.

## S. 121

which could be mistaken for a Yayoi-period Japanese granary. The model was
probably used as a family shrine dedicated to ancestor worship. It confirms that
buildings with the same architectural features as those of the raised floor
buildings of Yayoi Japan were used for residential Purposes in China at an
earlier date. The Chinese model, which was made with considerable attention to
detail, shows a sturdy structure with thick, round pillars supporting a raised
floor. Oxen are housed in the space beneath the floor while people inhabit the
upper level. The walls are made of smoothed planks and have a rectangular
opening set into the side to serve as a door. The house is dominated by a
sweeping gabled roof which has a series of rafters crisscrossing along the
ridgepole, creating the forked effect to be seen in the drawings of Yayoi
buildings on bronze vessels. This style of raised-floor building was to become an
integral part of Japanese architectural tradition, paving the way for the creation
of palatial and religious architecture in the fifth century.

THE EMERGENCE OF ELITE ARCHITECTURE

The establishment of a stable agricultural society in the Tumulus period freed
People from the tyranny of subsistence and permitted role diversification, trade
specialization and the accumulation of wealth and power. By the fourth century
A.D. Japanese society was becoming stratified along occupational and status lines
based on extended-family clans (uji). One such uji was the Yamato, based in the
Asuka region to the south of present-day Nara. It became a powerful clan that

mony over central Japan through judicious
manipulation of political and marriage alliances and military conquest. Yamato
leaders were to create the Japanese imperial house, their authority sanctified
through association with the mythical Sun Goddess Amaterasu-ömikami. The
Yamato clan sponsored construction of monumental tombs for its dead leaders
and increasingly sophisticated residential and religious buildings. The keyholeshaped tombs, scattered like confetti over what is now called the Kansai region
and imitated by leaders in northern Kyushu and even as far east as the Kanto
Plain (the modern Tokyo region), have Pproven to be repositories of immense
historical importance. They also disclose important information about building
techniques. They consisted of impressive artificial hills faced with stone and

‚ although only one leader was actually
entombed.

The tomb of Emperor Nintoku is the largest and most impressive of these
great funeral monuments known to us today. Completed ca. A.D. 427, it was

originally surrounded by two moats to Separate the sacred resting place of this
deceased ruler from the Profane

range of tools came into use under the patronage of the Yamato clan. The
Kanakurayama tumulus in Okayama City, excavated in 1953 and roughly

later, contained the most important collection of tools. A total of 48 chisels, 42
gimlets, 25 planing knives, 9 small Crosscut saws, 9 adzes) 2 axes and 8 cutting

similar to those known to have been in use on the Asian mainland at the same
time. A few of them have a tang for inserting into the handle in the style of later
Japanese chisels but lack the securing ring that became standard. Many of the
chisel blades resemble the hiro-nomi, the more recent wide-bladed striking
chisels; the major difference is that the ancient tools have double-edged blades
with a pronounced bevel on one side and a slighter bevel on the other. A few
single-edged chisels have been recovered from fifth-century sites but the doubleedged form appears to have been the norm. The use ofa double-edge establishes
that these chisels were employed for splitting timbers and roughing out shapes,

not for fashioning sharp-edged joints, which subsequently became the primary
role of this type of chisel.

The Advent of Shinto Shrine Architecture

A separate but closely linked architectural development of the Tumulus period
was the adaptation of the raised-floor structure in the creation of Shinto shrine
100 © THE INVENTION AND EVOLUTION OF TOOLS

## S. 124

Fig. 109. Main Sanctuary, Inner Shrine (Naikü), of Ise Grand Shrine.

architecture. The two major traditions in Shinto architecture of ancient times, the
Grand Shrine of Ise (fig. 109) in Mie Prefecture and the Great Shrine of Izumo on
the Sea of Japan coast, appear to be based, respectively, on the granary and the
residence models. Ise served as the official shrine of the imperial family, housing
the bronze mirror, one of the sacred regalia of ancient emperors; the shrine at
Izumo remained a center of strong local faith, its political and religious authority
eclipsed after the fourth century by the Yamato hegemony.

It is easy to understand how the grain storehouse, which preserved the staff
of life, was transmuted into the sanctuary for the gods, the kami, who presided
over the universe and its natural processes. Since these granaries were the focus
of village festivals celebrating the rice harvest, there was a close physical
association with the performance of Shinto rituals. Similarly, the raised-floor
residence with its elitist associations was seen as a suitable dwelling place for
the gods. The haniwa clay sculptures, which were set into the outside surfaces of
the burial mounds, included models of houses which were probably intended as
temporary abodes for the kami as they visited the sacred tombs, or for the souls
of the departed as they launched on their last journey.

The sanctification of secular buildings placed special demands on carpentry
techniques. Both the Ise and Izumo shrines have been rebuilt many times since
their foundation, reconstructed as part of a process of ritual renewal. The form
of such buildings as they stand today is highly refined and symbolic, and many
of the changes occurring during their evolution toward the more abstract were
accomplished only with the benefit of the more advanced tools and techniques

THE EMERGENCE OF ELITE ARCHITECTURE ® 101

## S. 125

developed in later eras. However, it must be em hasiz,

Tumulus period, the separation of sacred architecture fa hat even from the
demanded increasing refinements in tool use and the quality Ka ar omain
and finish. At Ise the emphasis on refinement led to abstraction Of 1a Ian ne
at Izumo the emphasis was more upon scale. Judging from the ee of the
stumps of ancient cedars excavated near Izumo, the Pillars of the shrine were
Over a meter in diameter even in early times. Structures Of such size would have
entailed great strength and skill in construction techniques. It is logical to
deduce that with each shrine rebuilding, dictated as much b strectural
