THE CONSOLIDATION OF
ASIAN MAINLAND TOOLS IN JAPAN

In the middle of the sixth century, monks, scholars and temple builders from the
Korean kingdom of Paekche brought to Japan the advanced building styles
associated with the Chinese and Buddhist institutions of the Asian mainland. In
the Asuka and Nara periods, Japan entered Upon an era of conscious
modernization using the model of Chinese eivilization. Over the next two
hundred years, a series of capital cities were laid out in orderly geometric grid
patterns in the Asuka-Nara region, and impressive halls and palaces housed
institutions of centralized state control based on those of Tang-dynasty China.
Buddhism became the official state religion and &reat monasteries and temples
were founded to spread the teachings of the faith of the Buddha throughout the
provinces.

Today the grand temple complexes constructed in the seventh and eighth
centuries are still the most dramatic evidence of the Staggering progress of this
era, from the pit dwellings, log-walled houses and raised-floor granaries of a
few generations earlier to monuments of unsurpassed creative genius. New
styles of building on an unprecedented scale placed new demands on the
building professions and their tools. More sophisticated techniques for working
with wood, stone, plaster and clay were imperative. Huge Buddhist temples and
halls of state called for the ability to work with sturdy framing timbers. The
bracket sets that supported the projecting eaves consisted of multiple bearing
arms and blocks carefully shaped and precisely fitted together.

The means by which this architectural transformation of Japanese
government and society was effected has received little attention. Even the best
preserved buildings from the period have been much repaired and rebuilt. Few
tools survive and carpenters’ documents consist mostly of registers of payments
contained in temple archives and some lively graffiti hidden behind the beams
of temple ceilings. However, it is clear from early writings such as the Nihon
shoki (The chronicles of Japan), compiled in the first decades of the eighth century,
that the earliest temples were built by master artisans from the Korean peninsula
who came to Japan for building projects sponsored by powerful court families

102 - THE INVENTION AND EVOLUTION OF TOOLS

## S. 126

such as the Soga. Early efforts by the Japanese to emulate continental building
styles were tentative: end bays were narrower to compensate for the greater load
at the roof corners, and additional pillars were used to prop up eave corners in
case the new-fangled cantilevers should prove inadequate. The Japanese already
had at their disposal axes, adzes, chisels, gimlets, mallets and planing
knives—all the basic cutting and finishing tools of continental practicee—from
the Yayoi and Tumulus periods. In fact, there may have been fewer immediate
changes in building techniques and tool types than is suggested by the dramatic
appearance of temples and palaces in the region around Nara. The recent
archaeological discoveries of sophisticated palace storehouses and a wide
variety of tool types in fifth-century tombs raises the distinct possibility that the
techniques which made possible the architectural achievements of the seventh
and eighth centuries were not a major departure in construction techniques from
the previous centuries. Rather, they were the culmination of a long and logical
process.

The best evidence for carpentry tools and techniques for this period comes
from Höryü-ji, the oldest extant Buddhist temple complex in East Asia, and from
the Heijö Palace and Tödai-ji building projects of the city of Nara. The
construction of each of these had its own special character and furnishes
important insights into the development of tools.

Höryü-ji—The Temple of the Exalted Law

Founded in A.D. 607 by Shötoku Taishi (A.D. 573-621), the prince regent
responsible for consolidating the institutional power of the Yamato state, the
austere beauty of Höryü-ji established a new standard of architectural taste. It
was destroyed by fire in civil conflict of the mid-seventh century but was wholly
rebuilt by 710. The West Precinct of the temple encompasses both the famous
five-story pagoda and Golden Hall (Kondö), two of the oldest surviving
structures, dating to the later seventh century.

The Kondö is an archetypal Buddhist timber-frame building (figs. 110 and 111)
comprising a four-by-five bay structure mounted on a stone podium similar to
the sanctuaries of Greek antiquity. It is dominated visually and structurally by a
massive hip-gable roof and its wide eaves are supported by cantilever bracket
sets, which are the most ingenious feature of the East Asian building tradition.

The five-story pagoda, which stands to the side of the Kondö, rises thirty-four
meters to the tip of its bronze finial. The pagoda is stabilized against earthquake
shock by a great central pillar but the essential structure, like that of the Kondö,
is post-and-lintel framing.

The sophisticated overall effect presented by these two architectural
masterpieces was created using simple tools little changed from their
predecessors a century earlier. It is evident upon close examination of the pillars
of Höryü-ji that carpenters had mastered the techniques of quartering large
cypress trunks with wedges. An experienced eye was necessary to judge the
character of the grain and the way in which it would split. There are great
advantages in this method over even modern techniques for milling timbers.
Splitting timbers with the grain makes them resistant to oil loss and rids them of

the propensity to warp, both critical factors in the longevity of the structure.

The quartered timber was subsequently hewed into eight planes, rounded
with adze and smoothed to silken smoothness with planing knives to make the
round pillars needed for temple construction (fig. 112). Although the pillars
fashioned in this manner were carefully shaped and finished, they were sturdy
in their proportions, a direct reflection of the tools and techniques used in their
creation. It is interesting to note in Passing that this technique was by no means
unique to Japan. It was standard procedure, for example, to use wedge, adze

106 »- THE INVENTION AND EVOLUTION OF TOOLS

## S. 130

u

and drawknife on straight-grained oak when preparing ground sills and other
major beams in house-framing construction in Europe. The technique was found
still to work effectively in the recent restoration of an eighteenth-century Welsh
farmhouse.!

Tool marks left on the surface of extant timbers are virtually the only basis on
which a picture of tools and techniques used in the construction of Höryü-ji may
be formed. The woodworking tools were chisels, adzes, planing knives and
saws. There is also evidence of the use of inking lines and pens as well as
carpenters’ squares (fig. 113). Although the types of tools used in the
construction of Höryü-ji were limited in number, there appears to have been a
great variety in size in each category. The greatest number of tool marks left on
surviving timbers was made by chisels (fig. 114); twelve different sizes of blade
heads have been identified from marks uncovered on members examined
during the restoration project begun in 1934. The smallest of these is 1.8
centimeters wide and the largest is 4.5 centimeters. All the joüinery seems to have
been cut with chisels. The mortises were cut most frequently with a 2.6-
centimeter-wide blade head. Use of the wider-headed chisels appears to have
been mostly confined to angled joinery. Curved surfaces and door pin sockets
were carved with gouges. Surfaces generally were smoothed with adzes and
(mished with planing knives, which were also used for finer joinery interfaces
fig. 115).

The adzes used to smooth the surface of the timbers had three sizes of blade
head: 9.7 centimeters, 5.5 centimeters and 4.4 centimeters. The largest of these,
the 9.7 centimeter blade, became the standard size for all-purpose adzes on
building sites until a generation ago (fig. 115). The narrowest blade was used in
conjunction with the chisels in joinery work, probably in the one-handed
chopping action immortalized in medieval picture scrolls. The marks left by the
adze heads in the wood surfaces at Höryü-ji shows that they struck the timber at
right angles to the direction of the grain and at a shallow slant, in contrast with
later practice.

Rudimentary crosscut saws were also used in the construction of Höryü-ji.
Small versions of the crosscut saw, excavated from burial mounds of the
Tumulus period, were used only for the fashioning of small, delicate utensils
and ornaments. Höryü-ji provides the earliest evidence for the use of crosscut
saws in building practice, although they were of limited usefulness. Saw marks
are observable only on the butt ends of smaller framing members, such as the
inner ends of the cloud-shaped bracket arms and bearing blocks of bracket sets.
Some tenons may have been rough cut by saw before fine finishing with chisel
and adze. The ineffectiveness of these saws is demonstrated by the existence of
several half-sawn timbers seemingly abandoned midway by frustrated
carpenters. The kerf of the saws ranges from 0.3 centimeters to 0.8 centimeters,
suggesting a grinding rather than a cutting action.

Only part of one tool actually used in the construction of Höryü-ji survives
today. Undeservedly, it is a section of the singularly inefficient crosscut saw type,
estimated originally to have measured fifty centimeters in length. It is now
preserved in the Höryü-ji Treasury of the Tokyo National Museum (fig. 116).
Experiments have shown that it was extremely laborious in use and heavy in the

supervised at the highest levels of state, During the reign of Emperor Shömu (r.
72449), a government memorandum was issued which noted that the capital
still had “shingled roofs and thatched dwellings, relics of the building modes of
antiquity” and requested that an edict be issued ordering that all courtiers of
substantial rank and “all commoners able to do so should build their houses
with tiled roofs and paint them red and white.”? Certainly at the official level the
Japanese succeeded in creating major monuments to church and state, even if
archeological evidence suggests that domestic architecture fell short of the ideal.
The year A.D. 728 saw the establishment of a government department, the
Mokuryö (“Construction Bureau”), to coordinate the construction of temples and
public buildings and to coordinate urban planning. A special ministry was also
set up to build the Heijö Palace, a compound over one kilometer square
containing the administrative and ceremonial halls of government as well as the
residence of the emperor (fig. 117). In mid-century the building of Tödai-ji as the
headquarters of State Buddhism was deemed so important that another ministry
was established to carry out this task (fig. 118). Tödai-ji and the Heijö Palace
were the two most extensive and impressive Projects of the Nara period. They

were carried out with the same commitment and organization of manpower
necessary for staging modern olympic games.

Today these monuments to state and religious authority are but a shadow of

110 » THE INVENTION AND EVOLUTION OF TOOLS

## S. 134

a

Fig. 118. Model of the Tödai-ji main compound, eighth century.

their former selves. The Heijö Palace d appeared under paddy fields after the
transfer of the capital functions to Naniwa and subsequently Heian-ky (Kyoto)
at the end of the eighth century. Tödai-ji has lost most of its original buildings to
natural disaster or civil war. However, smaller temples like Töshödai-ji have
survived to indicate what the great palaces and temples of the period looked like
at their zenith. Their sturdy bracket sets, elegantly curved beams and gracefully
proportioned colonnades represented the height of fashion in the contemporary
Chinese cities of Chang-an and Luoyang-
This cosmopolitanism was a singular achievement in so short a time, but what
was the technological infrastructure upon which its great monuments were
built? Although no tools of the Nara period are known to survive, much can be
gleaned about them from the character of the extant buildings. There was
considerable scope for carving wood with chisels and planing knives but the
ability to alter substantially timbers of large dimensions was limited by the
efficiency and cutting edges of the available tool Proportions of structural
members were massive, and penetration of pillars for insertion of bracing
beams was kept to a minimum because anything more would have been too
difficult or laborious. Pillars had a simple housing for horizontal bracing cut into
the top and a small mortised tie at the base. Heavy squared beams known as
nageshi were set inside and outside the pillars at base, waist level and lintel
height (fig. 119). By the end of the eighth century, these nageshi had became
thicker in section, thus allowing the pillars they braced to be made thinner and
more elegant to the eye. { &
Excavations carried out over the last thirty years at the site of the Heijö Palace
furnish some useful clues about building techniques. Many pieces of timber
framing have been recovered in good condition, preserved for twelve hundred
years by the chemical composition of the damp earth of the rice paddies. There
is evidence of extensive use of gimlets, especially for making holes for the ropes

THE CONSOLIDATION OF ASIAN MAINLAND TOOLS IN JAPAN ® 111

used to lash timbers together to float them down the rivers from mountain
forests. It is clear, too, that the marking and labeling system called bansuke was
used in eighth-century Japan as part ofa system of prefabrication because many
of the timbers recovered from the paddies of the past were labeled with letters
and numbers as a guide to assembly sequence.

Pillars at the Heijö Palace were smaller in general than those used for the
construction of Höryü-ji but they were made by the same technique of splitting
tree trunks into quarters with oak or iron wedges or with heavy duty chisels.
The quartered pieces were then rough-hewn by axe. A clue to the next stage of
the process is provided by snap line markings which are still visible on some
timbers. These indicate that the rough-hewn timbers were pared down with
adzes to a carefully predetermined octagonal shape before being rounded off by
planing knives into the cylindrical pillar. The pillars of the most important
ceremonial buildings, such as the Great Hall of State (Daigoku-den) at the center
of the palace complex, rested on individual base stones set on an elevated
podium in the Chinese manner. However, the pillars of most buildings,
including the residence of the emperor and his family, were set directly into the
ground and had elevated floors, in keeping with earlier Japanese practice. The
main difference seems to be that pillars of the larger buildings were braced
below ground level by Ppenetrating horizontal ties to give additional support for
the weight above (fig. 120). This procedure required mortise holes to be cut
through the bottom of pillars with chisels.

112 » THE INVENTION AND EVOLUTION OF TOOLS

## S. 136

Fig. 120. Lateral bracing of Heijö Palace pillars below ground level.

The crosscut saw was still useful only for cutting timbers less than thirty
centimeters thick, less than the diameter of most pillars used in the palace
complex. Its most important function would have been for roughing out mortise
and tenons and in making furniture, particularly Chinese-style chairs and
storage chests that had become popular—even status symbols—for the

aristocratic class.
The massive structure of the Great Buddha Hall (Daibutsu-den) of Tödai-ji

today indicates the scale of construction of which the Japanese were capable in
the eighth century. A pair of nine-story pagodas, each exceeding 100 meters in
height, guarded the approaches to the inner compound containing the Great
Buddha Hall (fig. 118). This structure, a colossal eighty-six meters wide and
nearly fifty meters high, housed the giant gilt-bronze statue of the Vairocana
Buddha, which was the spiritual focus of the temple. Success in erecting
buildings on the scale of the Great Buddha Hall and the twin pagodas depended
upon the availability of timbers of enormous size capable of yielding readily to
splitting, and on efficient organization of human resources, rather than upon any
dramatic improvements in tool technology. wi

Let us look for a moment at the vast army of administrators, site supervisors,
skilled craftsmen and laborers who participated in the construction process.
They were all under the control of a specially created Office for the Construction
of Tödai-ji, a government department presided over by a senior monk and highranking aristocrats of Emperor Shömu’s court. The office was divided.into nine
separate departments, each responsible for a different aspect of the project. A
Timber-Collection Department dispatched lumbermen west to Harima, near
Kobe on the Inland Sea, to obtain the forty-eight principal pillars, each thirty
meters long and 1.5 meters in diameter, needed for the Great Buddha Hall. The
forested mountains around Lake Biwa, north of Nara, provided the smaller

timbers necessary to complete this and the many other worship and residential
lex. A Transportation Department floated the

structures in the temple comp : i :

THE CON

## S. 137

for the prefabrication and assembly of the structures—the most labor-intensive

aspect of the whole project—employed 227 site supervisors, 917 master builders

and 1,483 laborers. At peak periods over 1,000 cooks prepared meals for the
different craftsmen and laborers at the site.

The word daiku (lit., "great builder”), today commonly used for all carpenters,
came into general use at this time to designate the chief builders in the Building
Department. Immediately below them in rank were shöku (lit., “little builder”)
who helped supervise site activities. Below these were the ordinary carpenters
who were referred to as takumi (“artisan”), or mokkö (woodworker). In time, the
Chinese characters for “wood” % and ”worker/builder” T. were combined
to create a distinctive Japanese character moku 4 to describe the craft and
profession of carpentry. The term is still found today in the name of the great
traditional building firms such as Yasuimoku Kömuten of Kyoto. The term
banshö (“artisan on duty”) also came into common parlance in the Nara period. It
teferred initially to carpenters who were sent to the capital from each region
to participate in official building duties in a kind of corvee labor. Eventually
the term was applied to carpenters in general until well into the medieval era.
The origins of many of the terms which were building blocks of the language
of Japanese architecture can thus be traced to this period. Although the
Pprecise usage of many of these terms has changed over the course of the many
centuries since the Nara period, we cannot appreciate their later significance
without first understanding their origins.

It would seem, therefore, that government attempts to standardize
measurements had little long-term impact on the realm of building practice in
the Nara period. Despite official efforts at standardization, artisan workshops
stubbornly retained their own units of measure. Extant buildings from the
eighth to the eleventh centuries have shaku units of different lengths. The pagoda
of Daigo-ji, one of the few surviving tenth-century buildings, even has shaku of
different length for each of its five stories. For some, like the Konjiki-dö of
Chüson-;ji, completed in 1124, the shaku used is as much as 32.27 centimeters
long, well over the length of the official standard of the Nara period.

COURT CULTURE AND THE DECLINE OF
MONUMENTAL CON STRUCTION

Few buildings survive to give witness to the long centuries of flourishing
aristocratic culture known as the Heian period. The period was centered on the
court of Heian-kyö, the city later known as Kyoto, which was established in 794
as the new capital in order to break the excessive influence of the powerful
Buddhist sects over the state at Nara. Although the layout of Heian-kyö
represented a modified continuation of Chinese principles of grid-plan order, the
eriod to which it has given its name was an era of prolific indigenous creativity.
After the fall of the Tang dynasty in China in the early tenth century, the Asian
ic instability lasting several centuries and

mainland lapsed into chroni
ne lie more to Japan until the end of the twelfth century.

With the move from Nara to Heian-kyö, the emphasis shifted away from
monumental state projects t0 more intimately scaled buildings constructed for
the aristocratic families who dominated the court. The two most important
buildings still standing, the Phoenix Hall of Byödo-in at Uji to ae south SE
Kyoto, and the Konjiki-dö of Chüson-ji at Hiraizumı ın the far north of Honshu,

COURT CULTURE AND THE DECLINE OF MONUMENTAL CONSTRUCTION + 115

## S. 139

are the products of direct patronage by the Fujiwara, the most powerful of these
families. The Fujiwara dominated the Heian court for most of the tenth and
eleventh centuries, and these delicately proportioned buildings, which still
delight us with their infinitely detailed interior Patterning in gold, silver,
mother-of-pearl and semiprecious stones, bespeak the refined self-indulgence of
the court. If these buildings are any guide to high cultural achievements in
architecture in this period, it can be seen that the emphasis had shifted from
structure to decoration. It was the skills of the ornamenting professions, rather
than those of the master builder, that were now in greatest demand.
Shinden-zukuri, the residential form developed for the mansions of the
aristocracy of Heian-kyö, had open-plan halls linked by covered galleries in an
informal arrangment around extensive landscaped gardens. These buildings
were roofed with cypress bark shingles (hiwada-buki) which reflected a quieter,
native taste than the bombastic glazed terracotta tiling of Chinese inspiration
used with such vigor in Nara. Bark shingles are also lighter than tiles, and
complicated structural support and eaves bracket sets, like those seen on the
Nara temples and halls of state, were Unnecessary and indeed visually obtrusive.
Under these circumstances there was little change in the tools used for cutting
and finishing timbers. The methods of preparing pillars and beams, of cutting
mortises and tenons and of smoothing surfaces with adze and planing knife,
remained firmly grounded in conventions established in the Nara period and
probably stagnated because of the limited Opportunities for large-scale work.
The government of the Heian period did not maintain a large architectural
establishment, although families like the Fujiwara had in their service
considerable numbers of craftsmen, including professional makers of Buddhist

images, such as the famed Jöchö who carved the Amida Buddha housed in
gilded splendor at the Byödo-in. Moreover, there

standardize measurements that marked the Nara
like the five-story pagoda of Daigo-ji (fig. 121) is any indication, there was

untroubled anarchy in the units of measure employed by the architectural
workshops, a feature in common with medieval Europe.

tools: not one is known to survive

century. Daigo-ji Five-Story Pag

esign—Invention of the Root Scale

The Liberation of D
for the Carpenter’s Square

Despite the general stasis in the development of tools of the hand in the Heian
period, there was an important innovation made to the key tool of the mind, the
sashigane. In the twelfth century, toward the end of this period, a square root
scale was added to the rear, or urame, side of the measure’s long arm. This scale
set out the square root values of the units shown on the front of the same arm.
Its deceptive simplicity makes it eas

y to overlook the significance of this
COURT CULTURE AND THE DECLINE OF MONUMENTAL CONSTRUCTION » 117

## S. 141

innovation, which ranks among the most important of all inventions in the
history of Japanese architectural design.

The urame scale transformed the carpenter’s square into a versatile and
powerful instrument of design. When the carpenter’s square was first adopted
from the Asian mainland, probably in the seventh century, it was merely a
modular scale which could also be used for setting angles. The new scale added
in the Heian period enabled the carpenter to perform two complex design
operations with speed, precision and simplicity at any workshop or building
site.

In the first of these operations, the scale could be used to determine the
dimensions of a squared member that could be cut from a round log (fig. 25), an
ingenious procedure already described in chapter 3. To state the obvious, square
roots made logs easier to calculate. This improvement to the carpenter’s square
came as a result of the shift from round to squared pillars in residential
architecture, one of the modifications to building styles which occurred as
Japanese architecture matured in isolation from the continent. Round pillars
were the norm in Chinese and Korean buildings, but the Japanese found that,
when sliding screens became standard features, it made better sense to construct
residences with square pillars. The interface of screen and squared pillar is
neater and keeps out drafts more efficiently than that made with a round pillar.
The creation of a “ready reckoner” for the basic tool of measurement indicates
that the squaring of timbers had now become a daily concern.

The new root scale made another and even more significant contribution to
building design. Easy calculation of curvature by describing parabolas based on
square and cube roots became possible (fig. 23). The scale bestowed upon master
carpenters the ability to design a variety of complex curved forms with relative
simplicity. This marked a fundamental point of departure in the design of the
roof, the dominant visual element in the Japanese building. The simple square
root scale on the sashigane made calculation of curvature and placement of

rafters and framing elements a matter of simple tangents. The most dramatic
evidence of this development was the kara-hafu, a Japanese style of cusped gable
(despite its name which perversely means “Chinese” or “Tang” gable). Its
alternating concave and convex curves required perfection in the symmetrical
placement of rafters and ribbing (fig. 122). By the twelfth century it was to be

found adorning the side eaves of aristocratic gateways and the front barge
boards of court carriages. Thus the Heian period ended with one of the simplest

but most important developments in Japanese tool technology, one which laid
the foundation for many of the design practices of later tradition.

OFSTYLE, STEEL AND SAWS

the Kamakura (1185-1333) and Muromachi (1333-1573) periods. Likened by
scholars to the era of feudalism in Europe, it was a crucial time in the
development of new. types of tools in Japan. At the beginning of the middle

DEVELOPMENT IN THE MIDDLE AGES OF STYLE, STEEL AND SAWS + 119

## S. 143

ages, architectural activity had shrunk in both scale and frequency as the
authority of aristocratic government, which was centered in Kyoto, withered
away with the rise to power of the warrior class in the provinces. At the end of
the period, society, government and culture had all undergone profound change.
A succession of warrior governments, or shogunates, had employed
architectural forms as an expression of their Zen-inspired ideals and as a setting
for public audiences and private functions. The first shogunate, under the
Minamoto and Höjö families, founded a cluster of Zen monasteries around its
headquarters at Kamakura, near present-day Tokyo. These monasteries were the
focus for the adaptation of the architectural styles of Song-dynasty China to
Japanese circumstances (fig. 9). The second shogunate of the Ashikaga family,
for reasons of political expediency, reestablished the center of government in the
imperial capital of Kyoto. Trade connections with China, which had lapsed for
several centuries, were promoted with the new Ming dynasty, and treasures of
refined lacquerware, ceramics and ink paintings poured into the shogunal courts
at Higashiyama and Kitayama and into the major monasteries like Shökoku-ji
and Daitoku-ji. This mingling of warrior and aristocratic traditions gave
Japanese culture some of its most exquisite and distinctive architectural
expression. Tracing these changes through the design and building construction
of the Kamakura and Muromachi periods uncovers the central importance of
tool innovations to the creation of the new styles.

Song Stylistic Influence and the Mechanics of Assimilation

The power of the samurai, coupled with fresh influences from a China
temporarily calm and reunited under the Song dynasty (960-1279), created
dramatic changes in architectural style in the thirteenth and fourteenth
centuries. The gaunt, monumental style of southern Chinese coastal areas had
been tried out at the rebuilding of Tödai-ji and related projects in the later
twelfth and early thirteenth centuries, but the architecture of the Northern Song
dynasty became the new orthodoxy favored by the warrior government for its
Zen temples. These were a race apart from the earlier temple buildings, speaking
a different language of elegance and Proportion. The roofs had sweeping eave
lines with graceful radial raftering and profuse clusters of multiple-arm bracket
sets. Penetrating tie-beams (nuki) all but replaced the external tie-beams
(nageshi) except as decorative affectation (fig. 123). The most sublime
accomplishment to survive from the era is the pagoda of Anraku-ji in Nagano
Prefecture (figs. 124 and 125). With its octagonal-shaped roofs delicately uplifted
at each corner, this building reveals that elegant proportions were achieved
through stronger framing and joinery techniques.

Although the Song style was used primarily for Zen temples, giving rise to
the Japanese name Zenshü-y0 ("Zen-sect style”), its influence was not confined
solely to the religious sphere. It set new standards of taste for secular palaces
and residences, where its curved forms proved as much of a challenge for the
Way of the Carpenter as Zen was for the Way of the Warrior. The master builders
responsible for creating Kennin-ji in Kyoto, the first Song-inspired building
complex completed in the early thirteenth century, and Kenchö-ji, finished in the

DEVELOPMENT IN THE MIDDLE AGES OF STYLE, STEEL AND SawS » 121

## S. 145

city of Kamakura around 1253, were forced to improvise. They had to reproduce
the new style without complete knowledge of Song building techniques because
information was transmitted by monks and traders who had no specialist
knowledge of the ways and means by which such buildings were made in
China. To emulate the curvilinear extravagances of Song architecture, carpenters
cut deep mortises through principal pillars to house-penetrating ties, without
knowledge of the techniques or rationale for effecting these changes. It is no
wonder that many of the surviving examples of religious establishments
founded in the thirteenth and fourteenth centuries are delightfully eclectic in
style but decidedly conservative in method of construction. Many of them were
a case of a dash of Nara temple style mixed with some Song inspiration and a
great deal of homegrown architectural wisdom.

One important outcome of all these pressures for change in building style
created by foreign influence was increased experimentation with the carpenter’s
square as an instrument of design. The design potential of tangents and
triangulation, parabolas and polygons, was realized by the carpenters as they
met the new cross-cultural challenge. It could be said that in the Heian period
carpenters learned to walk with their sashigane scale; in the Kamakura period,
as the Song stimulus increased the pace of experimentation, they learned to run.
By the Muromachi period they were flying, building with a lightness that lifted
the roof from all apparent earthly constraints. Now the placid kara-hafu of
Heian, with its crouched stance, had achieved a soaring silhouette close to pure
fantasy. The eaves flared away from the high arched apex in a series of effortless
sweeps, concave curve blending into convex slope, rafters beneath accentuating
the flow of the gable with a gentle syncopated rhythm (fig. 122). This truly

represents perfect harmony between ihe instruments of the mind and the tools
of the hand.

Pictorial Evidence of Tool Techniques in the Kamakura Period

An accurate and lively answer to the question “what did the building site of a
temple like Anraku-ji or Kennin-ji look like in the thirteenth and fourteenth
centuries?” can be found in the eye-witness accounts of narrative picture scrolls
(emakimono). These scrolls, frequently of considerable length, first became an
important form of artistic expression during the Heian period. In the new,
vigorous warrior age, their focus shifted from the rarified life and loves of the
imperial court to scenes of everyday activities, including glimpses of
construction sites. The most detailed scenes are to be found in the Kasuga gongen
genki-e and the Matsuzaki tenjin engi emaki (pls. 2-4). Both paintings, reliably

dated to 1309 and 1311 respectively, are well preserved and depict carpenters at

work. There is an eye for human interest as well as a delight in detail which

makes them convincing documentary evidence of tool type and usage.

The Kasuga gongen genki-e—an alternative title is Kasuga gongen reigen-ki—(pl.
2) tells the history of the Kasuga Shrine in Nara. The first scroll shows the
construction of the Chikurin-den in 948 but, fortunately for our understanding
of the Kamakura period, the tools and techniques depicted belong to the artist’s
own time. All the tools and activities are Painstakingly drawn and readily

122 » THE INVENTION AND EVOLUTION OF TOOLS

## S. 146

Fig. 126. Detail of shrine building showing use of planing knives (yariganna). Detail of pl. 2, Kasuga gongen genki-e, scroll 1, 1309.

recognizable. The site is a hive of industry; carpenters are stripped to the waist
as evidence of their physical exertions. They are laying out the site with string
markers or carefully prefabricating the framing timbers. At center scene stands
the töryö, clad in loose pantaloons, his kensao (long measuring ruler) resting on
one shoulder as he directs operations. Nearby we can see a carpenter checking
the level of the foundations as an apprentice pours water into the water level. A
team of three carpenters energetically pound a foundation stone into its correct
position on the podium. At the adjacent workshop the timbers are being split
with wedges, marked with a snap line, and measured for roundness with the
urame scale on the back of the carpenter’s square. Beside this carpenter sits the
sumitsubo, ready for instant use. Close by, groups of carpenters skillfully
smooth rough timbers with adzes, while the long-handled planing knives, yariganna, are pulled or pushed across the surface of the wood to give it asmooth
finish (fig. 126). We can appreciate the difficulty in operating these planing
knives from the studied concentration and firm, two-handed grip of the
carpenters. It is hard to imagine a tool which demands better eye-hand
coordination. It helps explain the preference of Japanese carpenters for sitting
.down on the job. A seated position gives better ‚control for precision work and
permits concentrated activity for sustained periods of time. This would have
been essential for all the painstaking cutting and smoothing operations depicted
in medieval scrolls. Sitting rather than standing gives the arm a more stable
Pivot point, important in avoiding gouging the surface of the wood when using
the planing knife. This 1so leads naturally to a pull-action

Development of the Pull-Action Crosscut Saw

These scenes of building activity in Kamakura period paintings also provide
important evidence that, by the early fourteenth century, the Japanese had
improved the design of the Chinese crosscut saw into a leaf-shape blade and had
altered the cutting action from a thrusting to a pull-stroke. Both these
developments were a turning point in the evolution of saws in Japan.

The new, leaf-bladed saw is shown in most scenes of construction activity of
the thirteenth and fourteenth centuries, including the Kasuga and Matsuzaki
scrolls. The artists of this era took particular delight in emphasizing its existence;
they accord it the visual prominence of an exciting technological innovation. In a
scene showing the building of the Ishiyama-dera, painted about fifteen years
after the Kasuga and Matsuzaki scrolls, a carpenter waves the leaf-saw
authoritatively to emphasize his point of view in an argument with a colleague.
Meanwhile, in the foreground, another carpenter has a leaf-saw tucked jauntily
into the waistband of his trousers. In the Kasuga Shrine construction scene, the
leaf-saw occupies pride of place in a special rack on the lid of a carpenter’s
toolbox, while another such saw is gripped grimly in the teeth of a carpenter as
he hauls a heavy beam across the work site.

A leaf-shaped blade has a more efficient action than a straight-edged saw
because of its breasted cutting edge. Nonetheless, the performance of this new
tool was limited. A ceiling panel shown in the Matsuzaki scrolls is the largest
piece of timber, in any of the Kamakura-period narrative scrolls, which this type
of saw is seen cutting. Judging from the comparative size of the carpenter doing
the work, the panel can be judged to be about thirty centimeters wide and three
centimeters thick. It is significant that one Carpenter in the Matsuzaki scrolls is
using a leaf-saw with the sawdust cascading toward him. Covering the
carpenter with sawdust is the proud Prerogative of the saw which cuts on the
pull stroke, one of its few disadvantages over the pushed saw in which the dust
exits the kerf on the far side of the timber. It seems, therefore, from careful
examination of these scenes of carpenters at work that the sitting position led

naturally to a pulling action for cutting tools as well as some subtle
improvements in their design.

## S. 148

to that of the leaf-saws shown in the Kasuga and Matsuzaki scrolls was
discovered at Kusadosengen-chö, in the city of Fukuyama, Hiroshima Prefecture
(fig. 127). The remains of an entire medieval town were exposed in the local
riverbed after it changed course following a major flood in 1919. Systematic
archaelogical excavations began in 1961 and have since revealed pillar stumps
belonging to residences and shops as well as a plethora of objects from everyday
life, including ceramic bowls, wooden tubs, fragments of clothing and
thousands of coins.

The saw was excavated in rusted fragments, but X-rays showed clearly its
original shape and the precise configuration of the teeth. Reconstruction of the
appearance of the saw reveals that it originally had a long, narrow blade 40.65
centimeters in length and 4.3 centimeters at the widest part (fig. 127). It was
similar in appearance to the leaf-saws shown in the Kasuga and Matsuzaki
scrolls, so it is safe to infer that it dates to the Kamakura period, especially as the
township from which it was recovered also dates to this period. The X-rays also
revealed that the teeth originally radiated in a continuous arc around the
convex, curved cutting edge. Experiments carried out in 1981 at the research
laboratory of Gongö-ji demonstrated that this saw was not an efficient cutting
tool and that it made little difference whether it was pushed or pulled to cut the
wood, a very interesting comment on the:state of development of the saw nearly
seven hundred years ago. The distinctive, pull-action saw was still a few years in

the future. J m
The second leaf-shaped saw was also discovered in 1980, at the Shimoköri site

of a Muromachi-period building in Ueno, Mie Prefecture. The blade is in
remarkably good condition and reveals that, in the hundred years that had
elapsed from the time of the Kusadosengen-chö saw, a significant improvement
in cutting efficiency had been achieved by redesigning the cutting edge (fig.
128). The teeth of the Shimoköri blade point backwards toward the handle of the
saw, indicating that it could only have been used with a pulling action. It is

basically a crosscut saw but would have worked reasonably well for some small
ripsaw jobs such as fashioning tenonS. Several generations of experimentation

The second saw is doubly significant because of the way in which it was

made. It consists of high-quality laminated steel, a clear indication of the
advances in steel-making technology In medieval Japan. Recently, Miyano
Tetsunosuke II of Miki, a master sawsmith of Mie Prefecture, was commissioned
to reconstruct the process by which this saw was made. The eighty-three-yearold master smith, assisted by three senior apprentices, took five days of
intensive labor to re-create the process by which the saw was made in the
Muromachi period. The long and sophisticated procedure entailed repeated
heating of the steel, shaping on an anvil and quenching in water to give the
cutting edge its brittle hardness. To give the blade the necessary =

The pull-action crosscut saw of the Kamakura and Muromachi periods was to
become the prototype for the general roughing saw, the hanamaru-noko, and for
more refined crosscut saws of the sixteenth and seventeenth centuries.

The Medieval Construction Crisis

The Kasuga and Matsuzaki scrolls are of immeasurable help in capturing the
state of the art of building in Japan about the year 1300. This date is significant
because, with the advantage of historical hindsight, it marks the approximate
end of an era in the history of Japanese tools. By the time the children playing
near the toolbox at the shrine construction site had passed on their carpentry
skills to their own children, changes had occurred which forever altered the
character of cutting tools, the nature of architectural form, and the organization
of the building professions themselves.

These radical changes were precipitated by the medieval equivalent of the
modern-day energy crisis: the Japanese began to run out of straight-grained
timber of structural dimensions. The increased demand for buildings
throughout the Kamakura period created a severe drain on available material
resources. Supplies of straight-grained timber, particularly hinoki, dwindled
alarmingly. During the seventh century, which had been the busiest period of
temple construction in the Asuka-Nara region, supplies of good timber had been
readily available in the forest-clad surrounding mountains. However, by the
time of the construction of Tödai-ji in the mid-eighth century, most of the
timbers had to be carried from the mountains around Lake Biwa, well to the
north. The giant pillars for the Great Buddha Hall were brought by sea and river
over five hundred kilometers from what is now the Kobe region. The rebuilding
of Tödai-ji at the turn of the twelfth century was actually delayed for several
years, in part because of difficulty in locating replacement pillars. Timber was
eventually located in the province of Suö, near the extreme Western tip of

Honshu. . IR
The case of Tödai-ji typifies the general circumstances prevailing at the end of
in the twentieth century, when the

the Kamakura period. Unlike the situation i
Japanese have been able to import Taiwanese cypress in order to alleviate their

wood shortages, the carpenters of medieval Japan had no alternative but to
change their work practices. They now had to ‚make more joints to connect
smaller timbers and to use the capriciously grained keyaki, a type of timber
which cannot be split in a straight line, for structural needs. Tb rendered the
traditional technique of splitting and smoothing timbers useless. The shortage of
wood compounded the pressures on the carpentry a nn by the
greater use of penetrating tie beams as part of the Song-influence style. New
and improved tools were urgently needed, both for better joinery and for

longitudinal cutting of lumber.

The Invention of Steel-tipped Cutting Tools
1-tipped cutting blades was one of the great
The development in ‚ctural infrastructures between the end of the

technological innovations in archite

OPMENT IN THE MIDDLE AGES OF STYLE, STEEL ANDSAWS » 127

DEVEL

## S. 151

Heian and the modern period. The crisis created by the difficult Song styles and
the shortage of straight-grained timbers stimulated the application of techniques
of steel making to carpenters’ tools. Steel blades were infinitely sharper and
more durable than the wrought-iron tools they replaced.

In addition to the technique of laminating steel to make saw blades, the
Japanese perfected the art of making chisel blades, and later plane blades, by
adding a cap of high-carbon steel to a malleable iron core by repeated heating,
beating and quenching (pl. 9). The flexibility of blades was further enhanced by
tempering with clay, leaving the cutting edge exposed to give it a tougher
temper from quenching. The same technique had been perfected in the making
of samurai swords.

This method of making steel-tipped blades is shown in the Shokunin zukushi-e,
a series of paintings dating to about 1600 in the collection of the Kita-in, Saitama
Prefecture, which show artisans at work (fig. 129). A master smith crouches on a
coiled straw mat before the glowing coals of his forge, surrounded by the
orderly chaos of the tools of his trade. There are steel-headed hammers with
handles of different lengths for shaping the white-hot steel and pincers for
handling: his work. On the wall beside the forge are hung the draw knives (sen)
used for smoothing the surface of the steel to a fine finish, and heavy-bladed,
short-handled choppers for cutting the steel to length to make the layers for
lamination. The smith works with an apprentice or fully trained assistant, his
wife keeping an observant eye on activities while busying herself with
household duties.

The smith shown in this seventeeth-century scene has a fixed place of work,
his rustic cottage. For much of the medieval period, however, smiths were
itinerant, following builders to each Project and setting up shop at the
construction site. There they would labor from dawn to dusk, not only making
and repairing tools in close consultation with the users but also manufacturing
the multitude of nails, clamps and other metal fittings needed for finishing a
building. By the sixteenth century, smithing as a trade had stabilized in key
centers such as Kyoto, and increasing specialization followed naturally. Separate
subprofessions for the makers of saws, chisels and plane blades emerged. The
smith shown in the Shokunin zukushi-e has drawings of chisels and plane blades

fixed to the wall behind his forge for ready reference, an indication of his
personal speciality.

were, to strain the popular expressio

The change to the single bevel was & ° ae :
Prior to this, chisels had been used both for making joints and, in conjunction

with oak or iron wedges, for splitting lumber to make pillars and beams. The
cutting edge of these chisels was beveled on both sides to facilitate penetration
through the grain, a feature to be found on ‚the tomb chisels of the fifth century.
With the abandonment of cleaving as a longitudinal preparation technique in the
late Kamakura and Muromachi periods, the chisel became the specialized
instrument for joinery. A double bevel proved inaccurate for precision work; it is

DEVELOPMENT IN THE MIDDLE AGES OF STYLE, STEEL AND SAWS «

## S. 153

easier to cut a clean mortise or tenon with a single-bevel chisel because the flat
steel side of the head can be placed flush against the surface of the wood as it
penetrates the grain.

The second of these changes occurred about the same time. The addition of
the tang made it possible to apply considerably greater force to the chisel as it
cut through temperamental woods. Henceforth in Japan the tanged and
socketed form was the standard style of chisel. It is shown in the working
drawing of the tool smith in the Shokunin zukushi-e of the seventeeth century
and, interesting to note, has changed little down to the present day.

What was the impact of the more efficient chisels on building techniques?
There was a marked change in the gooseneck mortise-and-tenon joint (kamatsugi), the most important splicing joint used for connecting timbers
longitudinally. Extant buildings which have been dismantled for restoration
work reveal a clear change in the design of this joint in the late fourteenth and
fifteenth centuries. The earliest known use of a gooseneck type of mortise-tenon
is for beams and ground sills of the “lean-to” roofs added to the first story of the
five-story pagoda and Kondö of Höryü-ji in the eighth century (fig. 130.a). These
gooseneck tenons are stubby in proportions, and the heads of the tenons are
squared like a hammerhead. Although an oblique-sided tenon head similar to
the later gooseneck tenons was used on a few buildings such as the mid-eighth
century Dempö-dö of Höryü-ji and the tenth-century pagoda of Daigo-ji, the
thickly proportioned rectilinear mortise and tenon remained standard until the
fourteenth century. In the twelfth century there had been some improvements in
the design of these joints with the incorporation of lapped joints into the
hammerhead for greater strength, as seen at the Main Hall (Hondo) of Ishiyamadera (fig. 130.b). However, most gooseneck tenons made up to the fourteenth
century avoid curved and angled parts in favor of simple planes set at right
angles. It is simply easier to work with the grain in this manner.

The form of the gooseneck tenon changed considerably during the later part
of the fourteenth century. The hammerhead joint became the exception rather
than the rule as a variety of sophisticated oblique-sided tenon heads came into
use. Some of the earliest known examples were used for the penetrating head
ties of the Niö-mon of Töfuku-ji (fig. 130.c), built in 1391 under the patronage of
the Ashikaga shogunate as one of the new centers of Zen Buddhism. By the later
part of the fifteenth century, gooseneck tenons had become even more
sophisticated. The purlins and penetrating head ties of the Main Hall of the Kita
Muro-in, a subprecinct of Höryü-ji, have detached gooseneck tenons added to
stub tenons to provide additional structural strength (fig. 130.d). Later joinery

was to become even more complex, moving from the sphere of structural
necessity to the realm of self-indulgent art.

The Japanese had probably been aware O0

the twelfth century, but plentiful supplies of st
longitudinal splitting had made the use of a large ripsaw unnecessary. There

was no need to saw laboriously through a piece of timber which could be split
quickly and cleanly. As noted earlier, split timbers are also free from the warping
.endemic to wood prepared by sawing. With the depletion of timber stock in the
medieval period, however; the frame-saw came into its own. It was now possible
to cut a long piece of ill-tempered, convoluted grain lumber into cleanly finished
structural timbers or neat, thin boards for use as flooring or wall and ceiling
panels. It also prompted the creation of one of the most distinctive and beautiful
Japanese decorative arts—paintings done directly on wide cedar panels cut with

the frame-saw. Depictions of birds and flowers, mythological creatures and
g the corridors of many palaces and shrines,

exotic animals began gracin . ° :
captivating their viewers with bold color set against the clear, lively grain of the
natural wood background.

The frame-saw in Japan consisted of a steel blade from one to two
meters in length mounted on A light, H-shaped bamboo frame. The blade was
tightened by an oiled hemp rop® wound tightly with a piece of wood in the

DEVELOPMENT IN THE MIDDLE IAGES OF STYLE, STEEL ANDSAWS + 131

## S. 155

manner of a tourniquet to stretch the lower part of the frame. This device kept
the blade under tension. This tension was good for the nerves of the sawyer,
because it increased the strength of the blade in the same way as the pulling
action of later single-person saws increases their efficiency. As the blade became
loose and ineffective with heavy use, the twisted rope served to restore the
tension needed for the frame-saw.

The frame-saw is a familiar tool because it had long been in use on the Asian
mainland, possibly tracing its roots along the Silk Route back to the Roman
Empire. Saws of similar type may be seen in Roman frescoes, although
tensioning devices seem to have been an invention of medieval Europe.

The frame-saw was extremely arduous to use, as may be imagined from the
efforts of the two men immortalized in the Sanjüniban shokunin uta-awase (pl. 5).
This set of illustrations of thirty-two different professions painted at the turn of
the sixteenth century furnishes important documentary evidence of a change in
the organization of the building industry which was prompted by the
widespread adoption of the frame-saw for ripsawing.

Until the fifteenth century, there was only one main division of labor in the
organization of the timber-building profession. The task of felling and rough
hewing the lumber belonged to timber cutters who lived and worked in the
mountain regions where the best supplies of timber were to be found; the

the logs before they began detailed marking and finishing.
The advent of the ripsaw in Japan called

into existence a new profession, the
oga-hiki, or “frame-saw cutter,

” later known as kobiki, or simply “sawyer.” The
first reference to this profession appears in written records of 1443 concerning
the famous temples on Mount Köya. The scene from the painting of the thirtytwo professions establishes that by about 1500 the sawyers ranked along with
carpenters and stonemasons as one of the major building professions. As the
Sanjuniban shokunin uta-awase scene reveals, sawyers worked in pairs, the master

sawyer positioned on top of the piece of lumber to direct the cutting action while
his assistant toiled away from below.

The operation was similar to methods emplo

ago and may still be seen in remoter parts of

lepz ‚ one could be forgiven for mistaking Walter
Rose’s description of sawyers at work in England at the beginning of the

twentieth century with a first-hand account of the Japanese team at work in the
Muromachi period painting. “How fascinating it was to watch them at work,”
Rose wrote. “The ceaseless up and down movement of the large shining saw
wielded by two Strong men at the Pit; the slow creeping forward of the cut (in a
large log so slow as to be scarcely perceptible) yet ever advancing towards the
toes of the man standing on the log—though he always moved them back just in
time; the completion of the log, when only a short length of each cut remained to
be done and board after board fell off side-ways, each beautiful with grain
markings that eye of man had never before seen.”' It seems that the creation and
discipline of the rip-sawyer in building practice has been a universal experience.

The establishment of the new profession of the ripsawyer in the fifteenth
century is of great significance both to the organization of the building

At least eighteen frame-saw blades from this era are known to have survived.
The oldest is thought to be the blade preserved in a storehouse of Shakubu-ji, a
loyed for the construction

temple in Kobe (fig. 131). It seems to have been employ
of a Yakushi Hall (Yakushi-dö) and three-story pagoda in the early Muromachi

period; in which case, it may be dated to slightly earlier than the first mention of
the existence of frame-saws in documents of the 14208.

An examination of the technical features of this particular blade is rewarding,
for it reveals detailed information about forging technology and sawing
techniques in the early days of the history of the frame-saw in Japan. The blade
is 2.056 meters in length to the tips of its securing rings. The steel appears to
have been cooled after forging by rapid quenching in water to give it a harder
edge than that possible by slow annealing with a clay mixture. The teeth are
sharply raked with a pronounced set of up to 5 millimeters to each side. Their
most distinctive feature is that they are arranged facing away from the midpoint
of the blade in each direction; thus, the saw had to travel only half the length of
the cutting edge (approximately 72 centimeters) on each stroke for maximum

in one direction as shown in the Painting has now been discovered. It is
preserved in the Kimura Ethnology Museum in Imari City, Saga Prefecture, and

i Upper surface of the timber. It is easy to see why the
unidirectional frame-saw was less common and efficient than the splayed-tooth
type. It is probable that the two-man unidirectional frame-saw was an enlarged
version of the smaller, single-person frame-saw common on the Asian mainland
but not adopted by the Japanese.

TOOLS OF THE ESTABLISHMENT—
CARPENTRY IN THE SERVICE OF THE STATE

Building activity accelerated frenetically in the late sixteenth and early
seventeenth centuries as the hundred years of civil war gave way to more stable
government under strong warrior leaders who unified the country. Oda
Nobunaga, Toyotomi Hideyoshi and Tokugawa leyasu, in turn, tightened the
reins of national control, culminating in the founding of the Tokugawa
shogunate (1603-1867). The extensive resources of the nation were mobilized for

the building of a new physical establishment to house and manifest the new
political order. Kyoto reemerged from the ravages of the Önin War as a glittering
imperial metropolis, while Edo rose from the marshes of the Kanto Plain to
become the headquarters of the shogunate and the symbol of the new age.

The art of building replaced the art of war as the national industry. The
organization of carpenters into trade associations OT guilds in the new cities
occurred as part of the replacement of the general guilds (za) of the feudal

domains, with specialist craft guilds or associations (shoku-nakama) based on a
type of free enterprise. Craftsmen in the building trades comprised about onethird of the entire artisan class; carpentry was by far the largest single
component of the building industry. There were normally five times more
carpenters than plasterers and tatami-mat makers, and ten times more
carpenters than sawyeTS, stonemasons, and cabinet and screen makers, yet
wages in the different building trades were broadly comparable. In 1657,
following the Meireki Fire which devastated much of Edo, the government
regulated the wages of master artisans in order to control costs. The top level of
carpenters was to receive 3 mommte of silver (11.25 gm.), and a rice allowance, for
each day’s labor. This amount, which was the same as that specified for master
roofers, master masons and tatami-mat makers, could buy about forty liters of
rice, which was quite generous. Master sawyelS, eg es u 2
momme or silver, plus the rice allowance, for their daily E E a 2 ei
Edo period, wages did not change significantly. At Ihe = In ar y n e
in 1844, a carpenter’s wage was still 3 momme of silver plus fo0 ei Are Br
there was provision for an additional 2.7 momme for overtime and urgent work.

The government took measures !0 regulate the trades as part of its policy to
mhäte nationa 6 the period of apprenticeship was fixed by

i of three years. In 1627 this was extended to
a a apea perhaps reflecting more realistically the

TOOLS OF THE ESTABLISHMENT + 137

1 control. In 161
aw to a term

nticeship,

## S. 161

complexity of carpentry traditions by the seventeenth century. By the end of the
century all restrictions on the length of apprenticeship were lifted. An edict of
1698 states that “even service over successive generations” was permissible if in
accord with mutual agreement.“ The population of the castle towns was zoned
according to status and profession to reinforce the structure of the political and
social order. In the city of Edo, which had over a million inhabitants by the early
eighteenth century, carpenters lived south of Nihonbashi toward what is now
the Yaesu entrance of Tokyo Station. The sawyers lived in Kobiki-chö, now
Ginza 4-chöme in the heart of modern Tokyo. By the end of the period, many of
the carpenters lived in the vicinity of present-day Kanda.

The Way of the Carpenter broadened and diversified in response to the strong
demand by the new overlords for monumental structures and refined villas.
Families of elite master carpenters, like the Heinouchi, the Köra and the Nakai,
became the preeminent architectural firms of the establishment, translating the
worldiy ambitions of rulers into castle keeps and gilded palaces. They ran
complex government building bureaucracies and presided over vast armies of
craftsmen. Building projects of this period were extraordinarily labor intensive.
The official government records for the reconstruction of the Nikko Töshö-gü in
the mid-1630s show that it required some 4.5 million days of labor to complete
this grandiose mausoleum complex.” Some three hundred and eighty thousand
carpenters were engaged in the project. The Yömei-mon, the two-story gatehouse
guarding the inner precinct and possibly the most vigorously decorated
structure in the whole of Japanese history, required 55,970 days of labor (from
1634 to 1636) by carpenters to complete (fig. 136).

The Nikko shrines are not unique in the construction extravagances of the
Edo period. More than thirty carpenters were needed per square meter for the
rebuilding of the vast audience halls of the inner palace at Edo Castle, including
the Great Audience Hall (Öhiroma) in 1751. Study of the gilt-lacquered coved
and coffered ceilings of the audience halls of the Palace of the Second
Compound at Nijö Castle, still standing in Kyoto today, reveals the infinitely
complex processes of timber preparation, assembly and decoration that were
demanded for the pomp and ceremony of state.

The chief master builders, entrusted with such complex and expensive
projects, received fixed salaries and formal status in the shogunal court
hierarchy. The head of the Nakai family, which supervised all official building
projects in Kyoto and the surrounding Kansai district, had an annual income of
1,000 koku (180 kl.) of rice, equivalent to that of many of the mid-ranking
samurai who served in official capacity for the shogunate. Köra Munehiro
(1574-1646), the Chief Master Builder (daitöryoö) in charge of the construction of
the Nikko Töshö-gü and the keep of Edo Castle in the 1630s, had a yearly
stipend of 100 koku and was honored with the bestowal of the title of Lord of the
Province of Bungo (present-day Oita Prefecture) for his architectural services to
the state. Munehiro was both a master carpenter and a master sculptor,
indicative of the stylistic propensities of the age. His enormous sixteen-meter
sculpture of a tiger, prowling through a bamboo grove, carved on the gable of

one Edo mansion is reputed to have frightened away the fish in nearby
Shinagawa Bay.

New Tools of the Mind—Design Manuals

carpenters’ workshops left few written
transmission of their skills had been primarily oral and behavioral. From the

later sixteenth century, however, the demand for large-scale buildings of
unprecedented complexity exceeded the supply of trained master builders, as
can be deduced from the labor figure$ for the Nikko and Edo Castle projects. The
workshops in government service had, therefore, to resort to written manuals to
ensure rapid and accurate training of new carpenters and the successful
Completion of building orders. These manuals, known as hinagata-bon (“pattern
books”) or hidensho (“secret hereditary writings”), included both technical
drawings of buildings and written descriptions of the kiwari proportions to be

employed in their design and construction (figs. 27-30). j
The carpenters’ manua became the most important tools of the mind in the
Way of the Carpenter. They consolidated long-established tra tions in response
to new political and social circum prompted greater reliance on

stances and
Written documents and technical drawingS in building practice.
0015 OF THE ESTABLISHMENT +

## S. 163

The most widely known of all the design manuals is Shömei (“Elucidation of
craft”) compiled in 1608. It represents the point of transference of the closely
guarded oral and behavioral traditions of a family of master carpenters into
recorded form in response to what its author refers to as ”a variety of mistakes
in carrying out the building styles of our family in recent years.” Its empirical
instructions were based on actual practice but they established normative
standards for future building design for the next two hundred and fifty years of
the Edo period. Shömei was compiled by Heinouchi Masanobu, head of one
eminent family of master carpenters working in Kyoto. Comprised of separate
handwritten scrolls on gateways, residences and palaces, pagodas, temples and
shrine buildings, the manual was based on the teachings received from
Heinouchi Yoshimasa, his father and head of the family. Yoshimasa himself
added a severe injunction to the colophon in 1610, warning that those to whom
its contents were entrusted must maintain the secrecy of the traditions of design
it explains.

These records were thus a closely guarded secret within each carpenter’s
household, but less than fifty years after the compilation of Shömei all this
changed dramatically. From the middle of the seventeenth century, carpenters’
records were printed using the newly popular woodblock print format and were
put into wider circulation among the ordinary carpenters of the towns and cities.
This had a devastating effect on the closely guarded traditions of the elite
carpenter’s families but contributed to the rapid dissemination of their design
traditions throughout the populace and regions. It was bad for some carpenters
but generally good for most buildings.

The Buke hinagata, (“Pattern book of samurai architecture”) was the first of
these published design manuals (fig. 27). It was printed in woodblock form in
1655, and a second edition followed in 1658. It consists of three separate
volumes: the first devoted to warrior residences, including their ceremonial
gateways, audience halls, Nö stages and stables; the second to shrine
architecture; and the third to interior design. It was compiled by Segawa
Masashige, a master carpenter working in the city of Edo on many of the stately
mansions of the regional lords. This design manual became the handbook for
rebuilding Edo after the disastrous Meireki Fire of 1657 in which as much as 80
percent of the city was consumed, a fact which undoubtly prompted the reprint
edition. Trained carpenters were in short supply, and new members of the
profession had to be apprenticed rapidly to carry out the herculean task of
rebuilding one of the largest cities in the world. This manual established
standards for architectural design. It defined the architectural order which, in
turn, became the physical definition of the Tokugawa political order.

New Cutting and Finishing Tools

It is no coincidence that during the Momoyama and early Edo periods some of
the most remarkable buildings in the history of Japanese architecture were built,
including the majestic palace of Nijö-jö, the towering castle of Himeji, the refined
Katsura Imperial Villa and that exuberant mausoleum at Nikko. Though
disparate in form and purpose, the architectural style of all these buildings was

140 « THE INVENTION AND EVOLUTION OF TOOLS

## S. 164

decisively influenced by new cutting and finishing tools. Further innovations in
the design of saws revolutionized the methods of turning raw lumber into
architectural timbers, while the adoption from China and adaptation of the block
plane had a dramatic effect on the finish of wood surfaces. Together the new
saws and planes significantly altered the design of buildings and speeded up the

construction process. The saws and planes of this era gave carpenters greater

freedom for expression in wood surface design; at the same time, it freed them
he preparation of timbers.

Invention of the One-man Ripsaw

For all its importance in the ripsawing of timbers, the frame-saw introduced
from China in the preceding Muromachi period had serious disadvantages. It
required two men to operate and its great length made it difficult to forge. In
the late sixteenth century, sawyers in Kyoto solved both these problems by
inventing the maebiki-oga. This was a short-bladed, easy-to-forge saw which
could be operated efficiently by a single sawyer (pl. 10; figs. 137 and 138). This
new ripsaw with its distinctive, high crowned blade weighing as much as four
ove its large raked teeth aggressively through the timber

or five kilograms dr: ! t
and soon superceded the two-man frame-saw AS the mainstay in lumber
preparation. j j n i

The development of this saw underlines the Japanese talent for tool invention
in response to specific buildings needs, in this case the demand for castles such
as Edo, Osaka and Himeji, and some of the largest temple buldings constructed
in Japanese history, like the Great Buddha Hall of Hokö-ji in Kyoto. These
magnificent, monumental structures were testimony not only to Sm
ambition and religious faith but also to the efficiency of the new type of ripsaw.
The two-man frame-saw was t00 Jabor intensive to cope with the escalating
demand for timber created by massive national-scale construction projects and it
was impossible to forge in sufficient numbers to meet the ua

The one-man ripsaw took its place at the heart of building industry with the
construction of the Great Buddha Hall of Hokö-ji in a This ne
Structure, completed in 1596, was created as a symbol of the power of the
national hegemon Toyotomi Hideyoshi. Gushi kenki, a record book of master

r blade required no special armature yet readily maintained a
cutting through thick lumber. As time passed the blades
of maebiki became progressively wider. Until special forging techniques were
developed, the wider-bladed ripsaws were made from two separate pieces
riveted together. By the late nineteenth century, maebiki-oga were almost twice
as wide as they were two centuries earlier and had assumed the distinctive
hunchback appearance for which they are best known (fig. 139)

According to tests conducted at the Takenaka Carpentry Tools Museum in
Kobe, a single person using the maebiki-oga can cut timber as effectively and
quickly as two men using the frame-saw. In other words, the one-man ripsaw is

twice as efficient as the frame-SaW- It is not surprising, therefore, that the
e-saw obsolete and quickly replaced the

maebiki-oga rendered the two-man fram ;

latter at building sites like the Great Buddha Hall of Hökö-ji. Although the

## S. 167

Fig. 140. Sawyers at work in the 18305 using the wide-bladed ripsaw (maebiki-oga). “Fuji from
the Mountains of Tötömi,” from Hokusai’s print series Thirty-six Views of Mount Fuji.

such as the palaces at Nijö Castle. In the harsh, physically demanding world of
the sawyer, there was no room for sentimental attachment to outmoded tools.

The sawyer operated the one-man ripsaw either sitting beneath the wood,
using the force of gravity to aid the pull cutting stroke, or perched on top of the
angled timber. There is a graphic depiction of a team of sawyers using this saw
in an 1830s woodblock print from the Thirty-six Views of Mount Fuji by Hokusai
(fig. 140). One sawyer may be seen balancing on top of the beam, a second
sawyer working beneath, while a third man sits to the side patiently sharpening
the teeth of a saw with a file. Meanwhile, a fourth figure, perhaps exhausted
from the exertion of sawing, gazes at a distant view of Mount Fuji. The wife of
one of the sawyers, child on back, offers encouragement.

Rhythm was fundamental to the coordination of movements involved in the
cutting process. Although strength was important, a slow, measured action was
vital to sustain the effort for long periods and to avoid burning the delicate fiber
of softer woods like cedar. The sawyers created their own work songs to
accompany their actions and to ensure the correct rhythm in their cutting
strokes. One such song, retained in the folk repertoire of Miyazaki Prefecture of
Kyushu, is Fyüga kobiki uta (“Song of the sawyers of Hyüga Province”). Its
words provide insights into the trials and tribulations of the life of the sawyer. It
was sung by Hyüga sawyers as they worked and was later disseminated
through the provinces of Western Japan by farmers and seasonal workers who

144 © THE INVENTION AND EVOLUTION OF TOOLS

## S. 168

traveled in neighboring regions when farmlands were lying fallow in winter.
Hyüga kobiki uta was sung in free rhythm giving the necessary flexibility for
synchronization with the work at hand. The repetitive “ya-re,” heard at the
beginning and inserted throughout the song as a refrain, served to coordinate
the actions of the workers. The main verse of the song translates:

Ya-re ‚Ya-re !

The work of the sawyer is harder than that of the daiku.

Don't ever become the wife ofa kobiki, young maiden!

Sawing good-tempered wood, he’ll deceive you, young maiden!

And you’ll end up his wife.

One can but wonder if this had been the fate of the woman seen in the Hokusai

print.
The skill of the sawyer with the maebiki-oga is legendary in Japanese

architectural traditions. With this ungainly looking saw he could cut a board or
panel as thin as 3.5 millimeters (8 ri). In a country where timber had become
such a precious commodity, minimizing waste was a critical part of the sawyer’s
profession. A skilled sawyer prided himself on cutting twelve pieces of evenly
dimensioned timber from one single log; a less skilled sawyer could manage
only ten pieces. This difference in output meant the difference between
providing ceiling panels for an eight-mat and a six-mat tatami room from a log
of the same size. To provide panels for the eight-mat room, the less skillful
sawyer would have to take a new log from which to cut the extra two panels,

effectively doubling the cost of the ceiling. i 1
The one-man ripsaw stands out as one of the major technological and craft
a perfect illustration of

innovations in the history of Japanese architecture. It is ae okthe build
the creation ofa specialized tool in response to the specific needs © the building

industry. Further, it demonstrates the way in which such a tool has
unanticipated and far-reaching effects on the nature of architectural design and
the organization of human activity. Acknowledging the maebiki-oga as one of

Fig. 141. Crosscut lumber saw (daigiri).

two-man ripsaw, having similar rings at each end of the blade and a splayed
tooth arrangement (fig. 141). Like the earlier 08a, it was designed for two-man
operation but was operated without a tensioning frame. Instead, round wooden
handles were inserted through the end rings to provide a secure, two-handed
grip for each sawyer. The teeth varied from raked to more vertical but were set
wide (up to 4 millimeters) for maximum blade clearance through the thick
trunks. The daigiri depicted in the Wakan sansaizu-e has a straight cutting edge,
described in the commentary as “one shaku wide” (fig. 132) but by the later
nineteenth century the blades became as narrow as 10 to 11 centimeters and
were convex in shape for increased cutting power. .

The daigiri became the main tool used by the somabito, men who did the
felling and logging on remote mountain sites. It also became the common tool

for rough crosscuttin and was found in every rural community until a
8 & y
generation ago.

Adaptation of the Chinese Block Plane

The second major tool type to u

“the tsuki-ganna [push-plane]
The push-plane was a block

ndergo dramatic development in the sixteenth
€ plane. The Wakan sansaizu-e of 1712 states that
was first used more than a hundred years ago.

plane worked with a pushing action by grasping
two handles provided toward the front. It was the standard type in use
throughout China and south and west Asia and was introduced to Japan by
Chinese merchants trading in the ports of Nagasaki and Kobe at some point in
the later sixteenth century (fig. 143). The exact date of its arrival is unclear and
unimportant. What is significant is that the needs of building practice in the
Momoyama and early Edo Period converted it into a simple block plane worked
by a pulling action in keeping with indigenous preference. The block plane,
minus handles, is shown with other tools casually laid on the veranda outside
the shop of a shoji-screen maker. in the Rakuchü rakugai zu screens of 1615-16,
