duction an

## S. 171

carpenter’s tool set. It rapidly replaced the adze and planing knife for smoothing
the surfaces of wood, performing the same operation more quickly and with less
effort and—dare it be suggested—with less skill.

The block plane prompted the invention of gouging and grooving planes to
make the sliding tracks for shoji and fusuma sliding doors. Hitherto the only
traditional method was the painstaking fashioning of the track by attaching
battens to the sills. The new gouging planes made it infinitely simpler and faster
to fashion the multiple tracks and housings for the screens which give the
interior of the Japanese building its special character (fig. 75). Now the way was
open for more extensive use of sliding screens and the more flexible
arrangement of interior space. At the same time, carpenters displayed their
ingenuity by making a variety of molding and beveling planes to create different
decorative effects on pillars and other interior detailing.

These developments may seem unremarkable to us now, for the arrangement
of interior space using sliding screens has become synonymous with the whole
idea of Japanese architecture. However, it was only with the development of the
new block planes in the late sixteenth and early seventeenth centuries that the
basic method for creating the most common sliding tracks came into building
practice. Similarly, the variety of beveling planes, which seem such an

indispensable part of the carpenter’s tool set, came into use only in the
seventeenth and eighteenth century.

Trade Specialization and Tool Diversification

Until the middle of the seventeenth centur)
occurred in elite architecture. Thereafte
carpenters who built houses and shop.
burgeoning castle towns. The style of d
became more sophisticated in conscious emulation of the architecture of the
elite. Features of the shoin residence and the sukiya “teahouse style,” which had
been reserved for the Palaces and rustic retreats of aristocrats and warriors,
became a common sight in the reception rooms of wealthy merchants and
artisans, despite frequent government prohibition. The tools and techniques
necessary to create the special beveling or consciously rustic blade marks on
alcove pillars of the tokonoma alcove and decorative shelving found a place in
the building practice of town carpenters as well as sukiya-daiku.

The building profession subdivided along specialist lines to meet the
demands of the new urban market. The maker of shoji screens, byöbu frames and
doors, was known in the seventeenth century as toshöjishi ("master of doors and
shoji”); by the end of the eighteenth century, he became the more familiar
tateguya and set up shop alongside sashimonoshi, carpenters who specialized in
making wooden storage boxes with perfectly crafted joinery.'” The sawyer
profession split into separate branches: the fellers, who used axes and the newly
developed crosscut saw to harvest timber from the hillsides; and the sawyers,
who concentrated their skills on the maebiki-oga to supply squared timbers.

Tools of every kind proliferated as a Consequence, reinforcing the separation
and identity of the carpentry subprofessions as the techniques needed to master

roughing saw retained a more leaflike shape. Other saws to appear at this time

THE MODERN TRANSFORMATION—
TRADITION VERSUS THE WEST

The Meiji period was, in many ways, another Nara period: the]

apanese leaders
set out to modernize state and societ

y on the basis of foreign models, with the
same determination and success as they had in the eighth century, and with

much the same process of adoption and adaptation. This time, however, the
Source of inspiration was Europe and America, not China.

The political and intellectual revolution which unseated the Tokugawa
shogunal government in 1867 and replaced it with a revival of an emperorcentered one was carried out by junior members of the military aristocracy
under the guise of rediscovering Japan’s inner historical dynamic—fukkö, the
“rediscovery of antiquity.” But in the next few years those same young men,
faced with the responsibility of political and economic management and the
establishment of national status in a Western-dominated Asia, quickly became

ogan fukkö sank out of sight, being replaced
nei kaika—Civilization and Enlightenment—
as a basis for their planned revolution. Article Five of the Charter Oath of June

11, 1868, which reads, “Knowledge shall be sought throughout the world so as to
broaden and strengthen the foundations of imperial rule,”" became their
guiding light. Lavish imitation or modeling on Western lines was not their
intention. Okubo Toshimichi, minister of finance writing on behalf of the Meiji
government to President Ulysses Grant of the United States at the time of the

Iwakura Mission to America in 1871, summed up the theory of government
reformation clearly:

\ pect to reach the declared end at once.
It is our Purpose to select from the various institutions prevailing
among enlightened nations such as are best suited to our present

conditions, and adapt them in gradual reforms and improvementes. .. .”

In 1868 Japan’s leaders had set out to modernize

country. To bring this about, they traveled the world searching for the best
models, the most experienced teachers and the

mocl l most impressive institutions,
finding them in the modern Post-industrial-revolution countries of the West.
These they adapted to suit Japan’s special requirements, recruiting specialists
from abroad or sending their own young mer

an n away for training. It is not
surprising, then, that the result reflected the Western models.
150 »

THE INVENTION AND EVOLUTION OF TOOLS

rather than Westernize their

## S. 174

Thus the second half of the nineteenth century witnessed the conscious
dismantling of the feudal order from above, applying Article Five to every
aspect of government and society. Western ideas, systems of government,
military organization, culture, industry and technology were tested to meet the
new national priorities. By the time of Emperor Meiji’s death in 1912, Japan had
emerged from her status as a feudal, even backward-looking country, to become
the indisputable economic and military power in Asia and a friend and ally of
the great British empire in the Anglo-Japanese Alliance of 1902. Domestically,
hard work and thrift had enabled the rapidly developing Japanese industries to
reinvest more than 15 percent of profits in new plants and still leave a margin to
pass on to the people in raised living standards. h h

New Western-style factory buildings, government and commercial offices, as
well as housing and urban development, placed new demands on traditional
carpenters and family workshops, requiring speedy adjustment of design,
materials and technique. Carpenters who had risen to the top of their profession
in the service of samurai lords before 1868 had to turn their hands and their tools
overnight to creating a new P ironment suitable for a progressive

modern state. Their early efforts em d the form rather than the substance
of Western-style architecture. AN

Edo, the old political capital of the Tokugawa shogunate, was renamed Tokyo
after the restoration of imperial government, but lived on to serve the new
establishment as it had the old. However, the new government needed a new
form of physical context. It was impractical as well as a little a to
operate the new government from behind the walls of the old = la ee
Public buildings in Western styles were essential to the consoli m e power
in a consciously Westernizing nation. As part ofa Bad 0 en -
government, the Meiji leaders set out to rebuild Tokyo ne in > ® nn
principles of contemporary urban and architectura practice : = est
Buildings such as the Tsukiji Hotel, the first official ne gues
constructed in 1868, were eclectic curiousities (fig. 2 Re nn ee)
traditional timber-frame structures awkwardly rn a Ir
accretions such as cupolas and towers. They deman! h 5 EN en
imagination, not new tools and techniques. In the 187 an er a D.

## S. 175

Project of the Meiji government. The project transformed part of the old Edo
artisan quarters into an eloquent symbol of the large-scale commercial
enterprises of the Meiji period. It was carried out under Thomas Waters, a
British engineer, who was commissioned to build a new Western-style, fireproof
district. He laid out a broad avenue southwest from Kyobashi and lined it with
neoclassical brick buildings covered with a veneer of portland cement (fig. 146).
However, the project produced a classic problem of lack of communication with
Japanese carpenters working for a foreign architect. The carpenters could not
understand the foreign units of measure nor work with them. It was decided,
therefore, to make the English foot equivalent to the Japanese shaku. In this way
Waters was able to draw up plans using his customary unit of measurement,
and the Japanese could carry them out on the basis of theirs. Despite the climatic
and seismic shortcomings of building in brick and cement in Japan, the Ginza
project had a profound influence on architecture in Meiji Tokyo. It offered the
Japanese a tangible model for Progress, a physical embodiment of civilization
and enlightenment, ushering in an era of brick construction for public and
commercial buildings that was the beginning of the demise of timber-frame
construction in residential architecture as well.

Rapid advances iron and steel manufacturing in the Meiji period had an
impact on architecture, particularly the more utilitarian structures of the new
age. Iron-framed buildings, such as the Shimbashi Machinery Hall of the Japan
National Railways, dating from the early 1870s, had iron columns proudly
proclaiming “Hamilton’s Windsor Ironworks Limited, Liverpool” as the origin
for their framework. But, by the later 1880s, buildings such as the Shimbashi
Factory of the Japan Railway Bureau, completed in 1889 and now preserved at

152 » THE INVENTION AND EVOLUTION OF TOOLS

## S. 176

ally manufactured cast-iron columns and roof

Meiji-mura near Nagoya, used loc
manufacturing nuts and bolts as well as

trusses. By the 18905 the Japanese were
door hinges for their building requirementS. In the first decades of the twentieth

century, the Japanese were building massive warehouses of domestically
manufactured iron framing and red brick in the port of Yokohama to service the
war effort against Russia and their burgeoning international trade (fig. 147).
One of the most ingenious applications of Western steel-framing techniques,
which also highlights the problems facing traditional construction processes in
this era, was at the restoration of the Great Buddha Hall of Tödai-ji carried out
from 1906 to 1913. This great edifice was 2 sagging, rotting pile by the end of the
nineteenth century, despite complete rebuilding carried out under the Tokugawa

- shogunate from 1688 to 1707 (fig. 148). Neither new timbers nor old building
ed to save it. Instead, a 19.8 meters wide box truss of

techniques could be employ:
imported Shelton steel girders and diagonal braces was inserted beneath the
main timber crossbeam of the conventional truss to carry the upper levels of the
roof (fig. 149). This Western intrusion was hidden from view by a suspended
ceiling of traditional design. The working drawings from the project reveal that

the loads and stresses were © d using newly mastered principles
of Western engineering:

## S. 179

structure the size of the Great Buddha Hall. Moreover, by the early twentieth
century there were no master carpenters capable of building on that scale. There
had been no large-scale traditional architectural project in the earlier part of the
Mejji period to keep the special skills of monumental construction alive. It had
been possible for the Nakai, master carpenters in the service of the Tokugawa, to
rebuild the Great Buddha Hall at the turn of the eighteenth century because they
were less than two generations removed from the builders of the castles, palaces
and religious buildings of the early Edo period. Their fathers and grandfathers
would have transmitted to them knowledge of the problems of building the
great halls of the temple of Nishi Hongan-ji and Fushimi Castle. The carpenters
of the end of Meiji were forced to reach back beyond their collective experience
and could not handle this challenge in traditional ways.

Mastery of Western building techniques was most dramatically demonstrated
by the grand Versailles-like Akasaka Detached Palace—with its steel frame,
imposing masonry facade, sumptuous chambers and central heating— when it
was completed in 1909. Intoxication with brick and stone as building materials
rapidly turned into a bad hangover when it became apparent that they were not
suited to Japan’s high humidity and earthquake conditions. Wood continued to
be the basic building material in vernacular housing, and the government
agencies gradually focused attention upon improving the structural soundness
and general durability of conventional timber-frame residences with
improvements such as Western-style diagonal bracing.

In the time-honored tradition of the Nara and Edo period governments before
it, the Meiji government undertook reform of weights and measures as part of
the process of modernization. The Ginza project had exposed the need for
effective standards in the building sector. It was an important task of the Meiji
government to bring order to the traditional systems of weights and measures in
order to facilitate industrialization and trade as well as building practice. After
an unsuccessful attempt to standardize traditional weights and measures in
1875, the government determined that traditional Japanese units of measure
should be given precise metric equivalents. A decade after the signing of the
International Metric Agreement by sixteen nations in 1875, a decision was made
to align the Japanese standards with the predominately European-based
standard, much to the chagrin of the British and the Americans. It was decided
that one meter should comprise 3 shaku, 3 sun. One shaku thus became 30.3
centimeters and 1 ken, comprising 6 shaku, would be 181.8 centimeters (182
centimeters to the next highest decimal place). These standards were formally
adopted in 1885 and promulgated by imperial decree in 1886.

As part of the broad Spectrum of changes in the later nineteenth century, the
Japanese also set out to establish a Western-style architectural profession. This
had far-reaching consequences for the traditional carpenter and his tools. The
distinction between design and construction has been a fundamental doctrine of
European architecture since the Renaissance. In Europe before the Renaissance,
as with Japan before Meiji, there was no Separate profession of “architect.”
Although sophisticated techniques of architectural draftsmanship had evolved
in Japan by the Edo period, the building designer remained “on-site” and
“in touch” with the techniques and materials of his buildings. The art and

156 » THE INVENTION AND EVOLUTION OF TOOLS

## S. 180

craft of building were one indivisible process, as shown in the earlier chapters.

In the Meiji period the first Japanese trained to be architects in the Western
style emerged from the tutelage of Josiah Conder and the fledgling Department
of Architecture of what later became the University of Tokyo (Köbu Daigakkö).
Influential architect-scholars of this new generation, particulary Itö Chüta,
argued strongly along Western lines for the independence of the architect as a
design profession separated from the building site and working practice. This
was the beginning of the demise of the prestige and responsibility of the master
carpenter. Today there is serious reevaluation of this philosophy. Now it seems
incontrovertible that the building designer should understand construction
materials and proceses, but in the Meiji period the Renaissance notions of the
architect began to drive a wedge between carpenters and his traditional design
prerogatives.

The Sögakudö and Meiji Carpenters Tools—
Japanese Technology and Western-Style Buildings

f transformation helps bring the changes to
design and carpenters’ tools into sharper focus. This was the construction in
1889-90 of the Sögakudö, which now stands in Ueno Park in Tokyo close to its
original location. The Sögakudö served as the school where most Western-style
musicians of Japan were trained until well into the twentieth century and
contained the first Western-style concert hall in Japan. It is a rewarding case
study because many of the details of its construction were revealed during

restoration from 1983 and 1987.

cornices, small triglyphs under double-hung windows, applied pillasters and a

traversing its east-west axis and a concert hall on the second floor which seats
338 people in tiered rows. This auditorium is light and airy with a high, barrelvaulted inset ceiling braced by tensioning rods (fig. 155). The main roof of the
building has rigid triangular trussing and diagonal struts In the best Western

## S. 185

design of the building, twenty years after the Meiji Restoration was fully
launched.

When the building was dismantled for restoration, it was discovered that the
timber-frame was held together not by the imported bolts and plates, very much
in evidence, but by conventional joinery techniques not so obvious to the eye (fig.
156). The bolts and other metal fittings served merely as structural reinforcement
for traditional joints. The strengthening of traditional building frames using
Western-style metal fittings became standard practice during the Meiji period.

The restoration also discovered the use of sumi numbering according to the
bansuke system on the original timber-framing members, in much the way that
bansuke have been found on the remains of pillars of ancient Nara recovered
from under the rice paddies. Thus traditional prefabrication, together with
labeling, marking and assembly using the plan board, were the basic building
procedures employed at the Sögakudo. Furthermore, during restoration, part of
a kensao, the master carpenter’s measuring rod, was recovered. As we know,
this hand-marked ruler was used by the chief master carpenter, töryö, to
translate the design of the building from the plan board to the building by
providing actual 1:1 vertical dimensions. The ruler was usually 6 or 10 shaku in
length, or slightly over 3 meters, but in the case of the Sögakudö only a section,
approximately 60 centimeters long, reused as a batten in the wall or roof frame
Survives from this master measure. This kensao shows that the shaku, employed
by the master carpenters to build the Sögakudö, was precisely 30.3 centimeters
in length. This was the standard Meiji unit set in 1886 as part of the
rationalization of weights and measurements. The discovery of a section of the
kensao conforming to the Meiji standard suggests that the standardization of
measures in Japan was being followed four years after it was promulgated by
law, a very different situation from the attempts to standardize measurements in
the Nara and Edo periods.

Finally, the enthusiastic, indeed excessive, beveled detailing of door frames
and lintels and the execution of moldings and cornices may be Western in form
but are of superlative quality as craft, reflecting a delight in detailing which
draws from within Japanese carpentry traditions while acknowledging Western
influence.

Thus there is compelling evidence of Japanese techniques in the design and
the construction of the Sögakudö. Despite its grand Napoleonic and Renaissance
design inspirations by Yamaguchi and Kuru, this most Western looking of Meiji
buildings masks fundamental Japanese building methods. The Sögakudö reveals
one of the hidden currents coursing through the veins of Japanese architecture of
the later nineteenth and early twentieth centuries. There was no special category
of “Western carpenter” brought into existence in the Meiji period to make

Western-style buildings. Traditional Japanese tools only were used in the
creation of many aspects of the new Western building styles. The means by
which Meiji architecture was created diverged less from the well-trodden paths
of past building practices—the type of “evil customs of the past” singled out for

eriticism in The Charter Oath of 1868—than their Western appearance might
suggest.

Although the strict apprenticeship system for carpenters went into decline in

## S. 187

Meiji with the new social system, and educational reforms such as vocational
training as part of the school system undercut the traditional pattern of learning
trades, until the turn of the twentieth century, construction projects were still
largely in the hands of master carpenters who had been trained under the old
apprenticeship system at the end of the Edo period. Although some carpenters
were exposed to Western building styles in the treaty ports in the 1850s and
18605, the effectiveness of this exposure appears doubtful. Most carpenters
engaged in Western-style construction in the Meiji period rarely, if ever, met a
Western carpenter or bricklayer. Even the carpenters trained in early Meiji by the
last Tokugawa generation, in whose hands rested the responsibility of building
such “modern” architecture as Tatsuno Kingö’s Tokyo Station and Frank Lloyd
Wright’s Imperial Hotel, retained most of the attitudes and techniques of their
immediate predecessors in tradition.

Thus the basic thought processes of Edo period building practice and the
techniques of tool use were intact for an entire generation after 1868. One may
also speculate on the conceptual congruences between Japanese architectural
Practices and the forms adopted from the West in the nineteenth century. A
master carpenter of the old order was rigorously trained
The classical tradition of Europe is based on a similar modular order. It
undoubtedly required less mental gymnastics on the part of a carpenter well
versed in the ratios of temple design to build the pillars and entablature of the
Sögukudö than might be supposed. Indeed, the fondness for entablatures
exhibited by buildings created around 1890, including the Japan Red Cross
Building and the Tokyo Postal Communications Office, may relate to the
traditional fondness for the cusped gable, kara-hafu. From the later sixteenth
century in Japan, the kara-hafu had festooned eave lines to give frontal emphasis
to castle and bathhouse alike. It is intriguing that a butcher shop built in Kobe in
the 1880s, and preserved at Meiji-mura today, has a perfectly executed
neoclassical fagade, complete with columns and pilasters, and a conventional
Japanese roof gable set above the doorway at the center.

When it came to learning Western techniques of building, the Japanese
Carpenters resorted to their traditional Practice of compiling instruction
manuals. In the 1880s and 1890s there was a proliferation of woodblock printed
Shin-hinagata (“new hinagata”) explaining Western systems of rigid triangulated
roof trusses and the methods for inserting iron bolts into wall frames. As early as
1871 a manual in the traditional Style was issued describing the techniques of
brick construction, including methods of laying bricks as well as techniques for
framing the structures (fig. 157). This manual may have assisted in the
construction of the Ginza Project under the direction of Waters.

Why was there this obstinate Preference for traditional ways of doing things?
Was it sheer stubborness, the inherent conservatism of customary tradition, or
was there some inherent justification for continuing old methods to build a new
age? Viewed objectively, there were many aspects of traditional Japanese
building Practice which were well in advance of those of the West in the latter
nineteenth century. With the exception of large-scale engineering, such as that at
Tödai-ji or for making bridges, Japanese tools and techniques of building still
had a compelling logic in the Japanese context. Europe and America may have

## S. 189

devised new methods of construction using ferrous metals. The West may have
been the source of new designs and stylistic inspiration, the point of origin for
new ways of life and government which required new containers to house them,
but traditional architecture in Japan offered distinctive features, many of which
have become ideals for even the most advanced architects working in the West
today. Among these are modular coordination of design, a high degree of
prefabrication of the parts, and superlative standards of finish.

In the nineteenth and for much of the twentieth century, the Japanese were
not as thorough in adopting Western carpentry techniques and framing
procedures, and types and methods of tool use, for the simple reason that their
own traditional technology was better. They soon discovered the shortcomings
of Western-style brick construction in both seismic and climatic terms, as noted
earlier in relation to the Ginza project. Moreover, in timber-frame buildings
Western-style diagonal bracing proved less than effective. Traditional building
design in Japan was based on the “flexible frame” principle now widely used for
high-rise buildings. Diagonal bracing makes large flexible frame structures rigid
and hence liable to damage in a severe earthquake, as was discovered in the
1923 Great Kanto Earthquake. Western-style diagonal braces and tensioning
devices, added to the main shoin complex of Katsura Imperial Villa in the Meiji
period, were systematically removed in the recently completed restoration
project. They were found to have impeded the earthquake resistance of its
original traditional flexible frame structure. Why should the carpenters of the
Sögakudö have abandoned their razor-sharp tataki-nomi for Western chisels?
The answer is simple. The laminated steel produced by the master metalsmiths
of Japan was superior in every way to anything produced in any American or
European factory. The Western push saw, with its thick blade and cumbersome
cutting action, offered no advantages to the Japanese already well served by
their flexible, fine-bladed pull saws. It is not surprising, then, that today in the
West there are master carpenters and builders concerned with quality and

craftsmenship who are adopting Japanese chisels and saws for their most
refined work.

HAND TOOLS VERSUS MACHINES IN THE POSTWAR ERA

“In the olden days, laborers Possessed their own hand tools to engage in
production,” wrote Abe Isoö in 1901. “They lived an independent life. However,
today intricate machines are invented, and unless one can invest a substantial
sum. ... we cannot readily obtain such machines. In this way, one by one,
workers are forced to leave their handicraft works at home and engage in
factory work.”" Such was the situation in the first decade of the twentieth
century when Japan had accomplished most of the material and political goals
set by those responsible for the implementation of the Meiji Restoration of 1868.
Abe’s words were to Prove even more apt in the postwar era, for much of

Japan’s Success as an economic giant has been obtained at great cost to
“handicraft works.”

From the early nineteenth century, the replacement of hand tools and

complex, interrelated building „ were still intact until the time of the
war in China and the Pacific (1937-45) The 1943-44 survey of the Institute for

variety of traditional hand tools in their professional work. However, an entire
generation of carpenters, not to mention roof tilers, plastererS, stonemasons and
others, was swallowed up during the eight long years of the war, as were stocks

RSUS MACHINES IN THE POSTWAR ERA + 167

## S. 191

craftsmen, from roof thatchers to master carpenters, vanished and their skills
died with them. They were unable to compete with the machine-made products
of the modern economy or to convince their children, the “new breed,” or
shinjinrui of the postwar generation, that there was dignity and fulfilment to be
found in the traditional professions. The Way of the Carpenter was diverging
from the way of most Japanese. It had suddenly gone from being a pillar of
society, central to its industry and culture, to become a curiosity of another age.
Cost-efficiency was part of the problem, but the loss of respect and self-esteem
through deprivation of design initiative was its root cause. The separation of the
designer-architect from the carpenter-builder meant that the organic nature of
the traditional architectural process was destroyed. Carpenters were demoted to
the role of manual workers in a society which sets great store on high
educational attainment.

Most building craftsmen now begin their training after graduating from high
school, because compulsory education to the age of sixteen as instituted under
the Allied Occupation virtually eliminated the traditional system of
apprenticeship commencing from childhood. The modern carpenter may be
better educated in general than his predecessors but his manual skills are
regarded as “retarded” by the carpenters trained from early adolescence, along
with a dozen or more fiercely competitive peers, under the traditional system.
Today the fully trained apprenctice is already twenty-three or twenty-four
before he can begin to establish himself in his profession. The trade schools
(senmon gakkö) for carpentry and construction emphasize the mastery of power
tools and of two-by-four and panel construction, not traditional carpentry
techniques. The master metalsmiths’ forges, which produced the finest
laminated steel the world has ever seen, are now cold while the master
technologists of Japan today concentrate their efforts on producing high-quality
Ryobi radial arm routers and Hitachi band saws. Traditional building techniques
are reserved for temple and shrine construction and creating Japanese-style
restaurants, ryökan (inns) and the villas of the wealthy. Even at these sites power
tools are in evidence. Today, as the second postwar generation of carpenters
8tows to maturity, the typical carpenter rarely uses more than thirty of the
traditional hand tools. Senior carpenters may still own several hundred tools,
but most lie unused in the toolbox. Carpenters now use sashigane marked in
centimeters and millimeters, and many younger carpenters do not recognize
shaku and sun. Those precious distillations of Heian- and Kamakura-period
design experience, the scales on the rear of the main arm of the square, are today
rarely used to calculate curvature or lumber dimensions. Such mainstays of the
Way of the Carpenter as the adze have long since disappeared from the modern
construction site.

The widespread dissemination of Power tools, first manufactured in Japan in
1957, has not only changed work Practices; it has altered attitudes as well. Power
tools have fractured the long-established and creative relationship between
master carpenters and their hand tools. As one old carpenter remarked recently,
“The children of the neighborhood used to come and watch us as we worked.
When we started using power tools we had to shout abunai (watch out!) and
warn them to go away.” The happy scene of children playing around the

168 © THE INVENTION AND EVOLUTION OF TOOLS

## S. 193

Industrialization of building components has had a significant impact on
conventional housing. Contemporary houses are frequently designed around the
dimensions of standardized manufactured components. In traditional practice,
the fittings carpenters measured the completed framework of the building and
then made the necessary sliding screens, frames and furnishings. Now the
custom builder is taking the dimensions of manufactured aluminum window
frames, prefabricated bathroom units, and system kitchens and designing the
building around them.

Like the way of life of which it was a part, the Japanese timber-frame
residence is becoming a thing of the past, even though surveys show that 78
percent of Japanese still would like to live in a traditional timber-frame house.
There has been a startling decline in the construction of Japanese-style, timberframe buildings and a rapid increase in the industrialized vernacular and steel
and concrete high-density housing. In 1963, wooden structures comprised 86.2
percent of housing units constructed nationwide. By 1983, just twenty years
later, this had dropped to 46.3 percent. This figure included the popular
Western-style houses made from imported two-by-four studs, making the actual
number of conventional Japanese houses even lower than the percentage
suggests. In Tokyo, Japan’s biggest housing market, the 1987 figure for
conventional timber-frame residences was only 27.8 percent; the soaring cost of
land is forcing the Japanese salaryman away from a free-standing home to a
concrete condominium unit.

Traditional carpentry techniques, therefore, are in danger of becoming extinct
as Japan turns away from its wood-building traditions to embrace new materials
and methods of construction. In the shrinking private house market, the smallscale local firms, the daiku-kömuten that comprise more than half of Japan’s
construction companies, are going out of business, particularly in the large cities.
The houses they build are unable to compete in a market dominated by large,
diversified and imaginative corporations offering well-marketed industrialized
houses and attractive package deals of house, land and loans. Traditional joinery
techniques require training and time to execute, increasing the cost of housing
above market rates now set by the machine-led sector.

The design and hand tool techniques which were a universal part of
traditional building practice are now confined to a few kömuten or design-build
firms which specialize in the preservation of historic buildings or in making the
highly refined sukiya architecture. There are several associations of traditional
craftsmen, architects and academics which act as umbrella organizations,
seeking ways to revitalize the Way of the Carpenter in the modern context
through workshops and training, conferences and publications, educational
reform and fund raising. These include the Kyoto Guild of Master Builders
(Kyöto Dentö Kenchiku Gijutsu Kyökai), based in the old capital, the Japanese
Association for Conservation of Architectural Monuments (Bunkazai
Kenzöbutsu Hozon Gijutsu Kyökai), based in Tokyo, and the Timber-frame
Architecture Forum (Mokuzö Foramu), at the University of Tokyo.

In these desperate circumstances, an odd marriage of convenience has taken
place between some small-scale local builders and machine tool manufacturers.
The progeny of this marriage has been computer-controlled joinery machines

170 © THE INVENTION AND EVOLUTION OF TOOLS

## S. 194

achines at Kinoshita Kömuten, Saitama

Fig. 160. Use of joinery m

(fig. 160) whose advent marks the latest chapter in the history of carpentry tools
in Japan. These machines were developed in the late 1970s by ingenious local
carpenters working in collaboration with machine tool manufacturers such as
Miyagawa Köki and Makita Company, the Japanese equivalent of Black &
Decker Corporation in the United States. This new generation of tools makes the
special spliced and angled joints which lie at the very heart of the Japanese
building tradition, producing @ quick-fix solution to the cri of costeffectiveness in the conventional housing sector. A technician with no training as
a carpenter is able to use these machines t0 make the complicated gooseneck
mortise-and-tenon joint for splicing sills, taking only twenty seconds complete a
joint that requires twenty minutes of precise, arduous work bya trained
carpenter using hand tools- The machines also eliminate need for the
comprehensive measuring and marking of each timber which was such an
important part of traditional carpentty pracice. It is no longer necessary to mark
each pillar and beam to show the location and configuration of each joint. Now
the carpenter s mply pro | rk for the machine operator and
mbol indicating w

vides a centering ma
stamps the wood with a hich type of joint is to be used,
making it possible to prepare about forty pillars a day. Howevet, it is interesting
to note that a plan board is still drawn UP from the detailed architectural
drawings, and traditional marking tools, including the sumitsubo, are still in use

at modern building sites- i
Kinoshita Kömuten Precut Timber Compan
HAND T! g VERSUS MACHINES IN THE P!

y, a member of the National

OSTWAR ERA » 171

## S. 195

Machine Precut Wooden Residence Association, is typical of a number of smallscale firms which survive in the housing market today through automated
jJoinery machines. It began using these machines in 1978 and is able to produce
framing timbers for nearly four houses each day by the use of the new methods.
Its output is nearly double that of firms of similar size still using hand tools, and
the firm has the additional advantage of much lower training overheads.

The company uses nine different joining and finishing machines on its factory
floor. One of these is a multifunction molder imported from West Germany, but
the rest have been specially developed in Japan to re-create traditional joinery
types without, of course, the traditional joiners. These range from a basic tenon
cutter to machines which make the traditional gooseneck mortise for connecting
groundsills and beams. The tenoner (hirahozo-ki) consists of a series of revolving
blades mounted on a steel platform which moves systematically back and forth
across the end of the piece of wood. The first blade trims the end neatly before a
pair of blades close in on the timber in a pincer movement to fashion the actual
tenon. Other machines of similar design fashion dovetailed lap joints (koshikakearitsugi), large, inset mortise and tenons (öiri), the lapped gooseneck joints
(koshikake-kamatsugi) used for splicing groundsills, and the complicated rabbeted
oblique scarf joint (okkake-daisentsugi) for splicing together major beams.

Such manufacturing tools, though extremely ingenious, have some
limitations. For instance, the quality of the finish can be quite rough, and
experienced carpenters are still employed to finish with chisels certain details
which the machines cannot create and to smooth over some of the rougher
routing in the large mortises. The precision of the machines also gives rise to
some unexpected problems. The “male” and “female” parts of the machinemade joints are identical; thus, should any shrinkage of the wood occur, the
interface becomes loose and structurally weak.

The development of joinery machines is a demonstration of the Japanese
carpenter doing what he has always done best—creating tools based on longestablished principles but adapted to the needs of a particular situation. It is
possible that this time he may have created a tool which, while preserving the
physical forms of Japanese joinery, will threaten the very existence of the
carpenter himself. On the other hand, traditional joinery techniques were
already under serious threat as a result of competition from other building
systems, and the new joinery tools are certainly stimulating interest in the form
and structural performance of traditional joinery. Miyagawa Köki, a machine
tool manufacturer based at Toyohashi in Gifu Prefecture, adapted the shape of
the conventional squared tenons and dovetails to create curved edges (fig. 161).
This resulted in a significant increase in their structural strength. Their T-shape
half-lap dovetail, which is 105 millimeters at the mouth and is used for timber
105 millimeters thick, was certified by the Aichi Prefectural Industrial Standards
Council in 1979 with an average breaking strain of 369 kilogram-force. This

compares with the 236 kilogram-force for a handmade conventional joint of the
same dimensions. "

But, one should ask, is newn

\ ecessarily always better in the case of hand tools
and machines in J

apan? Even where machines seem to have made some of the
most spectacular savings in energy and time, some of the benefits are illusory.

ery but, by using the hand ripsaw, the

sawyer was able to cut long floor boards and ceiling panels with less wastage
than can be done with modern methods. Moreovet, traditional methods
preserved the natural oil ofthe timber which is so vital for its durability. Modern
power saws, with the high-speed rotation of their cutting blades, actually burn
the wood, destroying the oil And sharply reducing its effective life.

At the same time as we recognize the advantages of the techniques and tools
used by traditional carpenters, W€ should not let sentiment cloud reason.
Concerns about the advent of the machine and the demise of handcrafts were
expressed in the eighteenth century when the spinning jenny Was invented in
England. In the case of Japan, the mechanization of the building process has
assisted the nation to lift its standard of living through higher quality and safer
housing. However, many of the developments since 1945 have been short-term
responses to necessity. It is now essential to rebuild the infrastructure of the
crafts traditions upon which the cultural and economic prosperity of the nation
has long rested so they may play a vigorouS " he future. Of all the

advanced, industrialized nations, Japan can best afford to take long-term steps

they meet the needs of the future.

HAND TOOLS VERSUS MACHINES IN THE POSTWAR ERA » 173

## S. 197

CHAPTER FIVE

Tools Past and Present,
Unique and Universal

This has been the story of Japanese carpenter’s tools, the men who made and
used them, and the buildings they fashioned. We started with the carpenters and
allied building professions, their organization and activity at the building
site—in other words, the “who” of the Way of the Carpenter. Later we addressed
the “what,” “how,” “when” and “why” of the tools which link the men,
materials and methods in the vocation of building carpentry.

We have also traced the course and probed the logic of Japanese carpentry
tools from earliest times to the present. It came as something of a shock to
realize, when proper attention is paid to the chronological sequence of tool
development, that many of the tools now considered indispensable in traditional
building practice came into use relatively late in the development of Japanese
architecture. What are now considered to be basic “Japanese tools” were not
used to make the historical Japanese buildings whose design and construction
techniques have been so extravagantly praised in both Japan and the West.
When we look at historic Japanese buildings, such as the Katsura Imperial Villa,
we tend to conclude that they were made with tools familiar to us today by
assuming that the saws, planes and chisels described in chapter 3 have always
been staples of building practice.

Nothing could be further from the truth. Some of the best known Japanese
carpentry tools evolved into the form in which they are used today only during
the last hundred years. The combination crosscut ripsaw and the plane with
capping iron, both considered typical Japanese tool types, are modern
inventions. Katsura Imperial Villa and other fine examples of Japanese palatial
architecture were created without the assistance of these tools. Other important
tools came into existence much later in Japanese history than we might have
expected. The block plane was not introduced until the sixteenth century. The
use of the ripsaw dates to the fifteenth century, and an effective crosscut saw for
rough hewing logs did not come into general use until the seventeenth century.

174 © TOOLS PAST AND PRESENT, UNIQUE AND UNIVERSAL

## S. 198

Thus, erroneous impressions are formed about the way some of the most famous
hitectural tradition were erected, and false

monuments of the Japanese arc
conclusions reached about the meaning of their fundamental design features. As

noted in chapter 4, Höryü-ji was constructed before the advent of many of the
standard carpentry tools, making extensive use of hewing axes and adzes,
chisels and wedges, planing knives and a preposterously inefficient type of
crosscut saw imported from China or Korea. The limited capabilities of the tools
available influenced the final appearance of Höryü-ji as much as any overt

stylistic intent of its makers.
Japanese tools came from t

the two. It is this latter process that lies
and unique in Japanese civilization. It has been shown that many of the basic

tools used in Japanese building practice were introduced from the Asian
mainland. They were adapted to suit Japanese materials and circumstances,
such as the character of the wood and the Japanese preference for sitting down
while working. Significant indigenous developments in tool type and use also
occurred. The Japanese turned the humble carpenter s square into a versatile
instrument of design by adding a scale for working out the curvature ofa roof
using square roots and tangents. The carpenter’s square WAS also furnished with
a scale for determining the dimensions of a squared beam that could be cut from
a round log. Metallurgy, associated with the development of the samural sword
in the latter Heian and Kamakura periods, led to remarkable advances in the
quality of cutting blades used for chisels and other Te Bi ns

Tools have furnished m tablishing a tangible link with the now intangible

TOOLS PAST AND PRESENT, UNIQUE

AND UNIVERSAL ® 175

## S. 199

processes of building construction. They provide, literally and metaphorically, a
handle on the processes of the past and offer deeper understanding of the
buildings they shaped and the builders who used them. An understanding of
tools also helps correct an overabundant enthusiasm for aesthetics alone in the
Western view of traditional Japanese architecture. Although beauty is of
profound importance in Japanese architecture, its full appreciation must take
place within the broader realm of the Way of the Carpenter—the craftsmen, their
tools and their techniques that gave buildings physical reality.

Japanese architecture, therefore, should be seen as craft before art, and tools
are the best guide from the craft of building to the art of architecture. Tools are
created to enable a craftsman to do a particular job. They act as an extension of
his hand and mind, enabling him to transform practical intention and aesthetic
desire into physical form. By their very nature they guide the actions of the hand
and arm, dictating limits but opening up new possibilities in design and
execution. In so doing a tool may be said to shape the mind itself, either overtly
or subliminally. This reciprocal relationship between man and his tools is buried
deep in the very process of evolution. The anthropologist Sherwood Washburn
contends, on the basis of the discovery of protohumanoid bone fragments in
Africa, that the invention and use of hand tools stimulated the growth of parts of
the brain in prehuman primates.ı This had profound consequences for physical
as well as mental man, ranging from the ability to analyze, articulate and feel
emotion, to patterns of child development and physical changes such as smaller
teeth because these were no longer used for many of the functions which tools
could fulfill. Stone tools such as choppers, scrapers and cutters, however
elementary they may seem, significantly expanded man’s interior horizons as
well as the organization of his exterior world. Tools, in fact, made man as much
as man made tools.

Anthropologists may accept such an important connection, but architects and
historians have been slower to grasp its implications. Recognition of the
ramifications of tool use on the human psyche reveals the limitations of the still
current Renaissance-inspired belief that architecture is the product of the mind,
and that the process of building is separate from and subservient to the
application of theories of formal harmony and proportion. In the Way of the
Carpenter, no such dichotomy exists between intellect and application. Design
and execution are inseparable; tools are instruments of both the mind and the
hand, particularly at the critical creative junctures that arise in the invention of
new forms.

Historians generally shy away from analysis of the physical aspects of the
building process because of its largely undocumented nature. Trends in
architectural theory and practice since the later 1920s and the establishment of
the Bauhaus, the seminal school of the modernist movement, have also hindered
the appreciation of this issue. The modernist assertion of a universal equation
between form and function, prevalent for much of this century, has left little
room for pondering the role of process. Subsequently, the rediscovery that
buildings serving similar functions are not necessarily identical in form has
stirred fresh awareness that Process is an active agency in shaping building
design. However, misplaced faith in the “mind over matter” adage lingers in our

176 » TOOLS PAST AND PRESENT, UNIQUE AND UNIVERSAL

## S. 200

thinking, excluding the possibility that matter may have an influence on the
mind, particularly in the case of tools. A major objective of this book has been to
identify the role of Japanese carpentry tools in the evolution of Japanese style
and the stimulus they gave to the formation of the Japanese artistic mind as
expressed in architecture—from curved window mullions to cedar door panels,
from wide floor boards to delicate lintels. Necessity may be the mother of
invention, but in Japan invention has also fathered necessity. Not only were tools
created for specific needs but many tools created for one purpose were found to
be useful for other functions as well. This expanded the range of detail and
design, in turn enlarging the horizons of human consciousness in that

fundamental anthropological sense.
It is important to see Japanese tools not only in their historical setting but also
in the universal matrix of tool types and use. There is a tendency to emphasize
and capacity for adaptation

u mai PAST AND PRESENT, UNIQUE AND UNIVERSAL « 177

## S. 201

and techniques, which have already been noted in passing, is a useful way of
concluding this examination of the Way of the Carpenter.

First, the large number of high-quality tools in traditional Japanese practice

and their specialized character parallels the large variety and specialized
character of woodworking tools in preindustrial Europe and America, reflected
in the old maxim “a tool for every job.” In both contexts, this was an inevitable
result of trade diversification and the search for greater mechanical efficiency. |

Second, the special regard of the Japanese carpenter for his tools, which has a |
religious dimension, would not come as a surprise to many of the European and |
American workers of wood of a mere two generations ago. As Walter Rose noted |
about English circumstances between the wars, “the sawyer’s regard for his saw |
cannot but be something of a mystery. ... [It] is difficult to understand what it
must be like to use a single tool from morning to evening, from end to end of the
week. But it is easy enough to believe that such continual use would be reason
enough for his devotion to its welfare, his meticulous care to keep it ever in
perfect condition.” This continual use of a variety of tools, themselves specially
crafted to perform a special task for a particular craftsman, employing skills and
techniques perfected over many generations and absorbed by the individual
carpenter from childhood, helps explain the special devotion of the Way of the
Carpenter in Japan in universal terms.

Third, the carpenter’s square is a remarkable and versatile tool of design and
execution, linking practice and principle. It is very different from the modern set
Square of the West but is similar in many ways to the European roofer’s square
with its complicated scales and tables for setting the pitch and position of rafters
and purlins. The Japanese and European traditions of timber-frame roofing

| design had much in common. In both traditions, for example, roof incline was
|| expressed as a number of units rise to units span, not in geometric degrees.

Fourth, long traditions of wedge splitting and axe hewing of structural
timbers link both Japan and the west. Japanese, European and American
carpenters all recognized the efficiency of splitting straight grained wood with
wedges in the making of building timbers and appreciated the longevity,
strength and freedom from warping that this process bestowed on the final
product.

Fifth, Japanese and European carpenters alike were trained in hereditary
practice from an early age. They learned their manual skills as they learned to
master the very functioning of their own growing bodies. As Walter Rose again
observed, “many are the times, for example, that I have been asked how I know
the difference between the various woods—oak, ash, elm, pine, sycamore, beech,
and so on. This knowledge I gained so early in life that I do not remember how
| or when I acquired it.” Their thinking about building design matured as their

minds themselves matured.

Sixth, methods of working wood in Japan are dictated by universal
considerations of wood grain type, direction and density. Fine differences may
occur, but the integrity of the relationship between wood character and
Carpentry technique remains constant. There is a predominance of “softwoods”
over “hardwoods”—that is, of conifers over broadleafed, deciduous trees—for
structural timber in Japan, but softwoods tend to be the principal timbers used

178 « TOOLS PAST AND PRESENT, UNIQUE AND UNIVERSAL

## S. 202

ide. There is also a universally shared sense of

for structural purposes worldw
wood cleanly and well.

satisfaction and pride gained in working good

Seventh, arising from the previous point, is the universal love of and
sensitivity to wood among the workers of wood. Walter Rose recalls his
childhood love for examining newly cut planks and boards propped up for
initial drying, “the alcoves thus formed beneath were lofty and cool, even in the
hottest weather; the light was subdued within. There was seclusion, complete in
its separation from the active life outside. . . . We were sensitive to a subtle
influence, as of a Gothic church, to which we likened it; the massed formation of
impassive timbers curving upwards, the sweet scent of newly cut wood. We
loved to examine those rugged planks and boards and to compare the
companion burrs, knots,sloped and grain markings, all taken by man from the
beneficent hand of nature, and standing quiet there, in the cool shock, waiting
man’s further dealings.” This deeply spiritual awe of materials and the special
role of the woodworker in taking them from nature for man IS as much part of
European and American traditions as it is part of the Japanese tradition.

Eighth, Japanese and Western saws may NOW differ in form and use but the
bow and frame-saws, once the mainstay of both the European and Asian saw
traditions, are similar in form and operational logic. All used a blade kept under
tension by a tightened cord. We have already noted how an Be en
of British pit-sawyers at work could be mistaken for a description © uromachi

sawyers.

THE MOVEMENT TO PRESERVE AND REVITALIZE THE TRADITIONAL BUILDING

The Japan Architect (May 1987), 6-7.

INDUSTRIALIZED HOUSING: William H. Coaldrake, “Manufactured Housing—The
New Japanese Vernacular,” The Japan Architect, four-part series (August-November
1986, and January 1987). The authoritative source for statistical data on the
contemporary housing industry is the annual White Paper on the Housing Industry
(Jütaku sangyö hakusho) issued by Yano Keizai Kenkyüsho (2-10-1 Hama-cho,
Nihonbashi, Chuo-ku, Tokyo). Close to one thousand pages in length, it summarizes

information from housing industry sources for the preceding fiscal year and analyzes
long-term trends.

CHISEL QUALITY: Bill Stankus, “Testing Wood Chisels: Lab Finds No Secrets in the
Steel,” Fine Woodworking no. 51 (March/ April 1985), 44-48.

GUIDE TO FURTHER READING

## S. 213

Specialized Studies in
Japanese on the Building Professions

Endö, Motoo. Nihon shokuninshi no kenkyü (Research on the history of Japanese

craftsmen). Tokyo: Yuzankaku, 1961. Studies on the building craftsmen up to the
sixteenth century.

. Shokunin no rekishi (A history of craftsmen). 1956. Revised. Tokyo: Shibundo,
1965. Concentration on the Momoyama and Edo periods.

Shinkenchiku-sha, ed. Ezu daiku hyakutai (A hundred pictures of carpenters at work) 2
vols. in English-Japanese. Tokyo: Shinkenchiku-sha, 1974.

Specialized Studies in Japanese on Carpentry Tools

Muramatsu Teijirö. Daiku dögu no rekishi. (The history of Japanese carpenters' tools)
Tokyo: Iwanami Shinsho, 1973. The first major study of the history of Japanese
carpenters’ tools. Some of the details and conclusions are now out of date.

Muramatsu Teijirö, ed. Waga kuni daiku no kösaku gijutsu ni kansuru kenkyu (Research on
the building techniques of Japanese carpenters) 1949. Reprint. Tokyo: Rödö Kagaku

Kenkyüjo Shuppanbu, 1984. The 1943-44 Institute of Labor and Science survey of
Japanese carpentry tools.

Nihon Gakushiin Nihon Kagakushi Shinkökai, ed. Meijizen Nihon kenchiku gijutsushi. (A
history of Japanese architectural technology before the Meiji period). 1961. Reprint.
Tokyo: Nihon Gakujutsu Shinkokai, 1982. See especially pp.187-251.

Shinkenchiku-sha, ed. Daiku dögusha (A collection of Japanese carpenters' tools).Tokyo:

Ce

Shinkenchikusha, 1984. The best illustrated description of the main types of tools.

Introductory Studies in English on Carpentry Tools

Lanz, Henry. Japanese Woodworking Tools—Selection Care and Use. New York: Sterling

Publishing Company, 1985. The book emphasizes tool making and preparation for
use.

Odate, Toshio. Japanese Woodworking Tools: Their Tradition, Spirit and Use. Newtown,
Connecticut: The Taunton Press, 1984. An introduction to the woodworking tools and
techniques of the tateguya, the screen-maker.

Special Collections of Japanese Carpentry Tools

The Takenaka Carpentry Tools Museum (Takenaka Daiku Dögukan) in Kobe, Japan, has
extensive displays and holdings of tools, and the staff of the museum has prepared a
comprehensive set of videotapes showing how each tool is used and made. In the
United States, there is a fascinating collection of carpenters’ tools, dating from the

GLOSSARY

## S. 218

bakufu (EM), literally, “tent government.” Shogunate, first by the Minamoto at
Kamakura (1185-1333), then by the Ashikaga at Muromachi in Kyoto (1333-1573), and

finally by the Tokugawa at Edo (1603-1868).
banshö (IE: ), term for carpenter used from Nara period (710-794) into medieval times

banzuke (Ef ), a system of labeling and marking timbers

bu (43), traditional unit of measure equivalent to 10 rin, or approximately 3.03 millimeters
chintaku reifu (BU@ERF), Shinto god who protects carpenters and their work

daiku (KL), general term for carpenter; master carpenter. Originally title for highest

rank of artisan, meaning "great craftsman.”

hinoki (Ki), Chamaecyparis obtusa, Japanese cypress
ibara-me ( HB), “thorn” shaped tooth; the raked tooth used for ripsaws

jichinsai ( HURL& ), ceremony for the gods of the earth, ground-breaking ceremony
iö(3K), traditional unit of measure equivalent to 10 shaku, or approximately 3.03 meters
jotöshiki ( -EHR ), ridge-raising ceremony, marking the completion of frame assembly.

Also known as mune-age-shiki.
kama-tsugi (BERE ), gooseneck mortise-and-tenon joint
kara-hafu (NEREER), cusped gable. Most elegant form of ornamental roof
ken (I4]), traditional unit of measure equivalent to 6 shaku, Or approximately 1.82 meters

keyaki (#8), Zelkova accuminita, zelkova

kiwari (Kl), literally, “timber splitting.”
modules, such as the span of the bay.

kobiki ( Kit & ), sawyer. See also oga-hiki. ‚

kuromatsu (BR), Pinus Hhunbergi, black pine, common pine me

principal participants in its construction
munengeshiki ( HELER ), see jotö-shiki j .

of refinement and conscious rusticity associated with the tea-ceremony tradition

sun ($), traditional unit of measure equivalent to 10 bu, or approximately 3.03

tateguya (HER,

, carpenter specializing in screen frames and doors

urame ($&H), square root scale on rear of long arm of carpenter’s square. Used for
calculating curvature and for determining sectional dimensions of a squared beam
obtainable from an unhewn log.

## S. 230

“ (continued from front flap)
ary sources, he charts a fascinating crosscurrent of
influences of changing supplies of building materials, newly imported architectural styles, and
innovations in tool design that combine to give
architecture in Japan the shape we know today.

The author follows Japanese tools up to the present, documenting the influences of Western building techniques on Japan and concluding that the
Japanese Way of the Carpenter, though unique,
also shares much with premodern craft traditions
worldwide, including their contemporary fate.

The Way of the Carpenter is an indispensable book
for all with an interest in carpentry and architecture, art and craft, and the history of the Japanese
artisan’s remarkable achievements in design and
construction. A bilingual bibliography and a glossary of technical terms with Japanese characters
makes this a valuable reference and lasting contribution to the field as well. The Way of the Carpenter
vividly recreates the life and work of many generations of master carpenters whose tools and
techniques have been lost, restoring them to their
rightful place as innovators in traditional Japanese
architectural design.

WILLIAM H. COALDRAKE was born in Japan
and educated at The Australian National University and Harvard University, where he received his
Ph.D. in the History of Japanese Architecture and
later taught courses on Japanese art and architecture. Having worked alongside master carpenters
on the preservation and rebuilding of several
temples in Kyoto, he became the first non-Japanese
accepted as a member of the Kyoto Guild of Traditional Master Builders. A faculty member of the
Research School of Pacific Studies at The Australian National University in Canberra, he contributes widely to publications on traditional and
contemporary Japanese architecture.

Front cover: detail showing master carpenter (Banshö no zu),
from the Töhoku-in shokunin uta-awase. Early fourteenth
century. Tokyo University of Fine Arts. Back cover: scene of
shrine construction. Detail from Plate 3,
